THE ACTS OF THE SESSION
(FIRST SESSION: THIRD PERIOD)
Air Navigation (Charges) Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. 123 of 1981)An Act to amend the Air Navigation (Charges) Act 1952, and for related purposes.
Apple and Pear Export Charge Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. 147 of 1981)- An Act to amend the Apple and Pear Export Ch~rge Act 1976.
Apple and Pear Levy Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. 1460f 1981)- An Act to amend the Apple and Pear Levy Act 1976.
Appropriation Act (No.1) 1981-82 (Act No. 1640f 1981)An Act to appropriate certain sums out of the Consolidated Revenue Fund for the service of the year ending on 30 June 1982.
Appropriation Act (No.2) 1981-82 (Act No. 165 of 1981)An Act to appropriate a sum out of the Consolidated Revenue Fund for certain expenditure in respect of the year endingon 30June 1981.
Australian Apple and Pear Corporation Amendment Act (No.2) 1981 (Act No. 145 of 1981)- An Act to amend the Australian Apple and Pear Corporation Act 1973.
Australian Capital Territory Stamp Duty Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. 124 of 1981)- An Act to amend the Australian Capital Territory Stamp Duty Act 1969.
Australian Capital Territory Tax (Cheques) Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. 126 of 1981)- An Act to amend the Australian Capital Territory Tax (Cheques) Act 1969.
Australian Capital Territory Tax (Insurance Business) Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. 125 of 1981)- An Act to amend the Australian Capital Territory Tax (Insurance Business) Act 1969.
Australian Capital Territory Tax (Insurance Business) Amendment Act (No.2) 1981 (Act No. 130 of 1981)- An Act to amend section 6 of the Australian Capital Territory Tax (lnsurance Bus;ness) Act 1969.
Australian Capital Territory Tax (Life Insurance Business) Act 1981 (Act No. 128 of 1981)An Act relating to the imposition of tax in respect of certain life insurance business having a connection with the Australian Capital Territory.
Australian Capital Territory Tax (Vehicle Registration) Act 1981 (Act No. 129 of 1981)An Act relating to the imposition of tax in respect of the registration of certain vehicles in the Australian Capital Territory.
Australian CapitalTerritory Taxation (Administration) Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. 127 of 1981)- An Act to amend the Australian Capital Territory Taxation (Administration) Act 1969.
Australian Meat and Live-stock Corporation Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. 150 of 1981)- An Act to amend section 50 of the Austrillian Meat and Live-stock Corporation Act 1977.
Australian National University Amendment Act (No.2) 1981 (Act No. 1660f 1981)- An Act to amend the Australian Natiollal University Act 1946.
Broadcasting Stations Licence Fees Act 1981 (Act No. 168 of 1981)An Act to amend the Broadcasting Stations Licence Fees Act 1964, and for related purposes.
Census and Statistics Amendment Act (No.2) 1981 (Act No. 177 of 1981)An Act to amend the Census and Statistics Act 1905, and for related purposes.
Commerce (Trade Descriptions) Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. 151 of 1981)An Act to amend the Commerce (Trade Descriptions) Act 1905 in relation to the exportation of food.
Commonwealth Grants Commission Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. 173 of 1981)- An Act to amend the Commonwealth Grants Commission Act 1973.
Companies and Securities Legislation (Miscellaneous Amendments) Act 1981 (Act No. 153 of 1981)- An Act to amend laws relating to companies and securities and for related purposes.
Continental Shelf (Living Natural Resources) Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. 182 of 1981)An Act to amend the Continental Shelf(Living Natural Resources) Act 1968 to facilitate the collection of levy imposed by the Foreign Fishing Boats Levy Act 1981 and of amounts payable under certain agreements, and for certain other purposes.
Crimes (Currency) Aat 1981 (Act No. 1220f 1981)An Act to make provision with respect to offences connected with the counterfeiting of money or certain kinds of securities and other activities injurious to Australian currency, and for related purposes.
Customs (Unlawful Exportation of Food) Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. 152 of 1981)- An Act ~to amend the Cllstoms Act 1901 in relation to the exportation of food.

Tuesday, 17 November 1981
Mr SPEAKER (Rt Hon. Sir Billy Snedden) took the chair at 1.45 p.m., and read prayers.
PETITIONS The Clerk-Petitions have been lodged for presentation as follows and copies will be referred to the appropriate Ministers:
Sales Tax on Publications
To the Right Honourable the Speaker and Members of the House of Representatives in Parliament assembled: We, the undersigned, wish to protest against the Federal Government's proposed 2+ per cent sales tax on books, newspapers and magazines.
This proposed tax is a tax on knowledge and an impediment to the free flow of information. We respectfully request that these items be exempted from the sales tax provisions.
. And your petitioners as in duty bound will ever pray.
bySir William McMahon and Mr Hicks.
Petitions received.
Sales Tax on Publications
To the Honourable the Speaker and Members of the House of Representatives in Parliament assembled. The undersigned citizens of Australia respectfully showeth:
That the Government's decision to impose a tax on books, magazines and newspapers, particularly educational publications and books of a religious nature, is abhorrent to the notion of the right to information, a clear infringement of our democratic freedom, and that no such tax is levied in any other country with which Australia is usually compared.
Your petitioners therefore humbly pray that the Government will withdraw its announced intention to impose a sales tax on books, magazines and newspapers.
And your petitioners as in duty bound will ever pray.
by Mr Sinclair, Mr Robert Brown, Mr Hodges, Mr Les McMahon, Mr Millar.. Mr O'Keefe and MrPeacock.
Petitions received.

Sales Tax on Publications
To the Honourable the Speaker and Members of the House of Representatives in Parliament assembled: The petition of the undersigned citizens of Australia respectfully showeth:
That Librarians and library users throughout the nation call for the exemption of books, periodicals and newspapers from the imposition of sales taxes.
The tax exempt status of libraries and schools will not protect them from general cost increases which sales tax and its collection will cause. Indeed, exemption claims on millions of items per year will themselves add to the cost burden of both libraries and suppliers.
This tax is socially injurious and unworkable, and a burden on education, science and culture.
Your petitioners therefore pray that your honourable House will exempt books and periodicals including newspapers from the proposed sales tax.
And your petitioners as in duty bound will ever pray.
by Mr Kerin and Mr Young.

To the Honourable the Speaker and Members of the House of Representatives in Parliament assembled. The humble petition of the undersigned citizens of Australia respectfully showeth:
That we the undersigned, having great concern at the way in which children are now being used in the production of pornography call upon the government to introduce immediate legislation:

2834 REPRESENTATIVES 17 November 1981

Petitions

I. To prevent the sexual exploitation of children by way of photography for commercial purposes;
2. To penalise parents/guardians who knowingly allow their children to be used in the production of such pornographic or obscene material depicting children;
3. To make specifically illegal the importation, publication, distribution and sale of such pornographic childabuse material in any form whatsoever such as magazines, novels, papers or films;
4. To take immediate police action to confiscate and destroy all child pornography in Australia and urgent appropriate legal action against all those involved or profiting from this sordid exploitation of children.

And your petitioners as in duty bound will ever pray.

To the Honourable the Speaker and Members of the House of Representatives in Parliament assembled. The humble petition of the undersigned citizens of Australia respectfully showeth that:
I. The decision by the Australia Council to discontinue the General Grant of $20,000 (which is in the nature of recurrent funding) to La Boite Theatre, Brisbane, will jeopardise the continued viability of the theatre, and in particular, the Main House Theatre; Adult Theatre Workshops; Youth Theatre activities and the existence of the professional core of the theatre.
2. The decision has been taken without sufficient consultation and without giving sufficient notice to La Boite Administration.
3. The absence of the Federal Government funding will mean the loss to Brisbane of genuine community-oriented and community-based projects and of the regular presentation of Australian plays.
Your petitioners therefore humbly pray that the House will request the Government to cause the Theatre Board's General Grant of $20,000 to La Boite Theatre to be reinstated without delay.
And your petitioners as in duty bound will ever pray.

Trans Australia Airlines
To the Honourable the Speaker and Members of the House of Representatives in Parliament assembled. The humble petition of the undersigned citizens of Australia respectfully showeth-
Many Australians depend almost exclusively upon Trans Australia Airlines for the provision of essential air services.
The Federal Liberal Party policy objective of selling Trans Australia Airlines within 4 years is contrary to the national interest.
Sale of Trans Australia Airlines would inevitably result in-
(i) the creation of a private monopoly of major airline services in Australia;
(ii) higher domestic air fares.

Your petitioners therefore humbly pray that the House of Representatives will reject outright any proposal to sell Trans Australia Airlines.
by Mr Humphreys and Mr Morris.

National Roads Construction Program
To the Honourable the Speaker and Members of the House of Representatives in Parliament assembled. The petition of certain citizens respectfully showeth that your petitioners believe that:
The decision to subject the National Roads Construction program to the tendering system will:
(a) threaten the jobs of 790 workers employed in 17 main road depots in New South Wales.
(b) impair the day labour system and undermine the capacity of Departments of Main Roads to train staff and test road construction concepts
(c) have a disruptive effect on the employment opportunities available in small communities
(d) reduce the flexibility of the Department of Main Roads to handle emergencies caused by flood or fires.
(e) ultimately increase the cost of road construction at Taxpayers' expense.
Your petitioners therefore pray that the Federal Government withdraw this disruptive proposal.
And your petitioners as in duty bound will ever pray.
by Mr Humphreys and Mr West.

Unemployment Benefits
To the Honourable Speaker and Members of the House of Representatives of the Parliament of the Commonwealth of Australia, in Parliament assembled. The humble petition of the undersigned citizens of Australia respectfully calls for:
The immediate and unconditional withdrawal of any proposals to deny Unemployment Benefits to unemployed people under 18 years old.
That the level of benefits for all unemployed people, and including people receiving sickness benefits and pensions, be raised to at least 120 per cent of the poverty line.
That future employment policies be devoted to job creation and the restoration of full employment rather than the evasion of this responsibility by hiding the unemployed in short term training and education schemes.
Public acknowledgement of the fact that unemployment is the result of insufficient jobs and not the fault of any minority group, the education system or the unemployed themselves.
And your petitioners as in duty bound will ever pray.
by Mr Malcolm Fraser.
Petition received.
Retail Trading Hours in the Australian Capital Territory
To the Honourable Speaker and Members of the House of Representatives in Parliament assembled. The humble petition of the undersigned citizens of Australia respectfully showeth:
That we wish to express our strongest opposition to any deregulation of trading hours in the Australian Capital Territory.
That if trading hours are deregulated in the Australian Capital Territory, the present restrictions on trading hours in other parts of Australia may be lifted.
Past performances have proven that longer trading hours do not lead to increased sales. However, they do increase costs, because shops have to be staffed for longer periods. To cover these increased costs, retailers are forced to increase prices and reduce staff levels. This means that longer trading hours increase both prices and unemployment.
I. There is no real consumer demand for longer trading hours in the Australian Capital Territory.
2. The deregulation of trading hours would force small retail companies out of business.
3. Extended trading hours would gravely disrupt weekend recreation and family life of shop assistants.
Your petitioners most humbly pray that the House of Representatives, in Parliament assembled, should: Ensure that retail trading hours in the Australian Capital Territory continue to be regulated by Ordinance as happened in the past.
And your petitioners as in duty bound will ever pray.
by MrFry.
Petition received.

Self-goyernment for the Australian Capital Territory
To the Honourable the Speaker and Members of the House of Representatives in Parliament assembled. The petition of the undersigned citizens of Australia respectfully showeth that;
I. Residents of the Australian Capital Territory are the only Australians who have taxation without representation at State and local government level;
2. Australian Capital Territory residents are amongst the highest per capita income taxpayers in Australia yet do not participate in the Commonwealth Government's tax sharing arrangements with the States and the Northern Territory (including local government grants);
3. Responsibility for raising and spending State-type and municipal taxes and charges should be vested in elected local representatives;
4. It is inappropriate for members of the Federal Parliament to continue to deal with local matters in the Australian Capital Territory-members of the governing body responsible for such matters should live in the Australian Capital Territory and be accountable to the residents of the Aus~ tralian Capital Territory.
Accordingly, your petitioners call upon their legislators to-
Confer on the Australian Capital Territory State-like self-government established on a sound financial basis which is to be agreed between elected representatives of the Australian Capital Territory and the Commonwealth Government.
And your petitioners as in duty bound will ever pray.
by MrFry.
Petition received.

Broadcasting and Teleyision Act

To the Honourable the Speaker and Members of the House of Representatives in Parliament assembled:

The humble petition of the undersigned citizens of Australia respectfully showeth:

That because television and radio-

(a) affect our social and moral environment,
(b) are family media watched and heard by many children at all times, and
(c) present too much explicit violence and sex.

They therefore need stronger control than other media and the existing standards need stricter enforcement in both national ABC and commercial sectors.

(a) for adequate and comprehensive programs in the best

interests of the general pUblic,

(b) for a "Dual System of Regulation" enforced by the

Australian Broadcasting Tribunal by internal regulation and

external control,

'

interests of the general public, and

for breaches of program and advertising standards.

And your petitioners as in duty bound will ever pray.

Epping North Post Office
To the Honourable the Speaker and Members of the House of Representatives in Parliament assembled:
The humble Petition of the undersigned citizens of Australia respectfully showeth that the Non-Official Post Office at Epping North is under threat of closure and the Postmaster, Mr R. Rowley, has been told by Australia Post that if he retires in order to transfer the office to another person, that the office would be closed. He has also been informed that even if he remains as Postmaster the matter of closure will be reviewed in twelve months' time.
We submit that the office has been serving a community need for many years. The area.is in a cul-de-sac so that the residents only alternative would be to travel three kilometres by bus to the nearest Post Office at Epping. At least four schools in the area use the office for banking and the office is not far from the group of small shops used by the community who would be inconvenienced by the loss of postal and banking facilities. There are many pensioners in the area using the Post Office and plans have been approved for a hostel to be built nearby to house 100 aged people who would certainly require an accessible Post Office.
Your petitioners therefore humbly pray that the Epping North Post Office be retained and that the office be reclassified as one to remain in the Post Office Network.
And your petitioners, as in duty bound, will ever pray.
by Mr Howard.

QUESTIONS WITHOUT NOTICE
PROFESSIONAL RADIO AND ELECTRONICS INSTITUTE OF AUSTRALASIA: INDUSTRIAL DISPUTE
Mr HAWKE-I address my question to the Minister for Industrial Relations. I refer the Minister to the report dated 21 October 1981 by Commissioner Vosti to the Full Bench of the Australian Conciliation and Arbitration Commission dealing with the industrial dispute involving members of the Professional Radio and Electronics Institute of Australasia employed by the Department of Transport on the maintenance of radar facilities. Will the Minister comment upon the findings by Commissioner Vosti condemning the Public Service Board for, amongst other things, 'constant harassment of the PREIA advocate whilst putting the Institute case' and 'displaying a lack of industrial integrity'? The commissioner's finding states:
If the tactics adopted in this inquiry are a true rellection of the standard of industrial relations and integrity established by the Board in its handling on industrial relations generally then it leads me to the conclusion that those necessary ingredients for successful employer, union relationships are almost non-existent at Board level or, to put it more charitably, are at a very low ebb.
Will the Minister also comment on that finding? Finally, I ask the Minister: In view of this totally damning indictment of the Public Service Board by an independent member of the Conciliation and Arbitration Commission, what steps does the Government intend to take on its side as the employer to create these necessary ingredients for successful employerjunion relationships in the Commonwealth Public Service? Will it now cease its persistent attempts to apportion blame solely to the trade unions for problems in that important area?
Mr VINER-I will investigate the matter to which the honourable gentleman refers and give him a reply as appropriate.
HOME MORTGAGE INTEREST RATES
Mr McLEAN-My question is directed to the Treasurer and relates to the Treasurer's statement of 8 November that a survey of major lending authorities indicated that they had not foreclosed any mortgages and taken possession of any homes as a result of interest rate increases. I ask: Does the Treasurer accept that this kind of survey result does not adequately or even partially reflect the difficulties currently faced by home buyers? Is the Treasurer aware of the time lag between interest rate increases and mortgage foreclosures

as home buyers undertake a wide range of financial arrangements and sacrifices to maintain their capacity to meet increased mortgage repayments? Can the Treasurer assure the House that, notwithstanding the comments made in his statement, he is aware of the difficulties presently being experienced by home buyers as a result of high interest rates? Can he assure the House that the Government is aware of, and is prepared to take action to alleviate, the increasing difficulties now confronting present and prospective home buyers?
Mr HOWARD-The short answer to the honourable gentleman's question is yes. I inform the House that I made those statements in the context of some fairly ill-informed remarks by the Victorian Minister of Housing, who sought to suggest that the Commonwealth Government was totally responsible for the problems being experienced by Australian home owners at present and that all that was needed to solve the problems was the injection of a few hundred million dollars more by the Commonwealth Government. The fact of the matter is that State and local governments are at least as responsible as, if not more responsible than, the Commonwealth Government for the cost of housing and land in Australia. In fact; all the information which comes to me indicates that by far the most disabling factor for potential home buyers in recent years has not been increases in interest rates but rather increases in the price of a house and land throughout Australia, particularly in the metropolitan area of Sydney where, because of the very rapid escalation in prices in that city, partieular problems have been created for home buyers in that part of Australia. That information indicates that, except in relation to people who have very recently taken out loans, the great majority of people now paying off their homes would be paying less, as a proportion of their weekly earnings, notwithstanding the interest rate increases, than they would have been paying, say, back in 1978. That is because of the fairly obvious fact that, although interest rates have gone up since 1978, in the generality of cases wages l1ave gone up ilt a faster rate.
, GREAT BARRIER REEF MARINE PARK
Mr WEST-My question is directed to the Prime Minister. Is it a fact that under the Coastal Waters (State Powers) Act the Queensland Government will be able to issue oil drilling .licences in the 70 per cent of the 3-mile territorial sea in the Great Barrier Reef region where the Cairns section has not been declared? Why did the Prime Minister not declare the whole Cairns

Questions Without Notice

17 November 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 2843

section to the low water mark instead of just 30 per cent? Does the Prime Minister still believe that current research may show that drilling may be possible on or near the reef in future? Finally, I ask whether the Queensland Premier was correct when he stated this morning:
We will run the whole thing. We--
That is, the Queensland Government-
will make the zones, the regulations and neutralise the Commonwealth proposals. We will write the rules, we can write them all ourselves.
Will the Queensland Government, as stated by the Premier this morning on AM and in the media, be in a position to impose its own regulations and zoning plans involving yet another Commonwealth capitulation to the belligerent Queensland Premier?
Mr MALCOLM FRASER-I am not at all sure that the honourable gentleman has interpreted correctly what was said this morning, but in any case I had not heard it. The basis of the proposal, the understanding, that had been worked out between my colleague and Mr Ahern, the Queensland Minister, was the advice of the Great Barrier Marine Park Authority so that the ecologically sensitive areas that impinge on the coastline would be protected. Tomorrow we expect to get the formal advice in terms of the Act from the Great Barrier Reef Marine Park Authority, and proclamations will proceed on the next day, Thursday. So the boundary is based on the advice of the Authority, which is charged with that responsibility and which is in the best position to make proper judgments in relation to it. The protections for the reef come not only from the declaration of marine parks but also from the overriding power and authority of the Commonwealth-from the Commonwealth Act, the Great Barrier Reef Marine Park Act. That is something that we need to understand and remember. A Commonwealth Act provides the backdrop to our total capacity to protect the reef, and in any circumstances in which its use is necessary it will be used.
I think it would be a good thing to understand precisely what has been agreed. The understandings between my colleague and Mr Ahern are basically those that were put to the Premier last Friday, which he was unwilling to accept then and which I understand he is now willing to accept, although I have not heard that from him. I understand that the Queensland Government has accepted the propositions which were put to the Premier last Friday and which at .that time, as honourable gentlemen probably understand, were not accepted in full by the Premier.

Once the proclamations have been made on

Thursday a number of steps need to be under-

taken by the Great Barrier Reef Marine Park

Authority. It will call for submissions for a man-

agement plan. Those submissions can be made by

a great variety of people-by conservationists, by

people who are interested in undertaking activi-

ties such as tourism, fishing, or whatever else they

might be. Then it is for the Authority to develop

and publish a draft plan which will be the subject

of further comment. That comment can come

from any party, including the Queensland

Government or the Commonwealth Government

or people in Queensland who might be in the con-

servationist arena or the commercial arena. Once

there has been further comment as a result of the

initial draft plan, the Authority will submit a plan

to the Minister, together with representations that

have been made to the Authority. The Minister

can either accept the plan or refer it back with his

own suggestions.

.

Once the Minister accepts the zoning plan, which obviously will have gone through a number of stages involving public comment but which basically will have been in the hands of the Authority to determine-that is the important point for this House to consider-the plan will then be
put to both Houses of this Parliament and within 20 days either House may reject the plan. So there is ultimately a parliamentary process which the zoning plan also has to pass. In all of this the public can be involved, the State Government can be involved, and the Commonwealth Government is involved, but it is the powers of this Parliament under the Acts of this Parliament that ultimately provide our total capacity to do what needs to be done.

These are the procedures that were worked out in relation to the Capricornia Section of the park. The procedures in relation to the Cairns Section are similar except in one respect and, again, this was part of the Ahern-Wilson understanding. When .the regulations are made through the processes I have outlined-managed by the Authority, determined by the Authority, recommended to the Minister, and all the rest of it-if in relation to the 5-kilometre limit, where it comes into the coastline, the Queensland Parliament wants to pass some complementary legislation applying precisely what has been agreed through the processes I have outlined, of course it can do so to put a back-up to the processes. That is the only difference between this section and the Capricornia Section, which did not come into the 5-
kilometre limit. However, at that point no

2844 REPRESENTATIVES 17 November 1981

Questions Without Notice

changes to what had been agreed would be accepted by the Commonwealth in any sense, shape or form. That needs to be understood.
Mr West-That is not what he said.
Mr MALCOLM FRASER-J am just telling the House what the position is, what the position was last Friday, what the position was when the Minister was negotiating an understanding with Mr Ahern, and what the position was last Thursday, as stated by the Deputy Prime Minister and me. If Queensland wants to pass some legislation in that area to back up the five kilometres it may do so, and we would welcome that. Obviously we hope that there will be co-operative arrangements so that it is the Queensland ranger service and all the rest that apply the rules and regulations of the park. There is really no point in the Commonwealth being forced to establish a separate service to do that. Again, it is the Authority that would have the overall responsibility of reporting to this Government and of making sure that any zoning plan had been properly complied with. If it were not, something would have to be done about it. That is the position. J think it would be useful for honourable members if I could have leave of the House to incorporate in Hansard the joint statement by the Deputy Prime Minister and myself of last Thursday, 12 November.

The document read as follows-

For Media

Thursday, 12 November 1981

JOINT STATEMENT BY PRIME MINISTER AND DEPUTY PRIME MINISTER
Cairns Section of the Great Barrier Reef Marine Park
The Great Barrier Reef is one of the wonders of the world. It is a focus of national pride, and all Australians treasure it. There is deep concern in the community for the welfare of the reef, and a universal conviction that proper care of the reef is a priority responsibility of governments.
Since 1975, the whole of the reef has been protected by the Commonwealth Government under the Great Barrier Reef Marine Park Act. Australia is indeed a world leader in conservation management of the marine environment. The policy of the Commonwealth Government is to proclaim the various sections of the Great Barrier Reef Marine Park progressively. The Capricornia section was proclaimed in 1979.
The Cairns section has been the subject of negotiation for more than 12 months, the one outstanding issue being whether the western boundary should be the low water mark on the mainland or a line some distance off the coast.
The view of the Commonwealth Government is that the low water mark on the Queensland coast must form the boundary where features of ecological significance are involved.
The Commonwealth Parliament has full jurisdiction and responsibility right up to the low water mark. The Commonwealth Government was willing to allow discussions with the Queensland Government to be extended to a very considerable length in the interests of achieving co-operation and

agreement. But despite the length of the discussions, agreement has not been reached.
Accordingly, the Commonwealth Government has decided to move at once towards proclamation, and the enabling action leading to the proclamation of the Cairns section will be started immediately.
The Minister for Home Affairs and Environment will immediately request the Great Barrier Reef Marine Park Authority to identify precisely the boundaries involved and report to him on its recommendations to allow him to recommend to the Governor-General that the area be proclaimed.
The total length of the Western Boundary will be some 550 kilometres, and for about 30% of this distance the boundary will be the low water mark on the coastline.
Among the ecological features included, will be the sea grass beds north of Cape Flattery, which are an important habitat for dugong~an internationally recognised endangered species; high quality reefs fringing the mainland and islands; and marine areas adjacent to Queensland-proclaimed national parks which extend down to the shoreline.
The Cairns section of the Great Barrier Reef Marine Park will be some 36,000 square kilometres. When this is added to the 12,000 square kilometres in the Capricornia section, the park will then be many times larger than any existing or proposed marine park anywhere in the world.
The Marine Park concept and the way it is being implemented will ensure the conservation of the Reef ecosystem. It is the Government's policy that regulation should be the minimum necessary to conserve the Reef. As with the Capricornia section, there will be perfectly satisfactory provision for recreation, shipping, tourism, commercial and amateur fishing and other activities. The Park boundaries will exclude port and other marine areas adjacent to intensive industrial or agricultural developments on land, unless conservational reasons dictate otherwise.
The selection of the boundaries of the Cairns Section of the Marine Park has been made only after the most careful consideration of alternatives by the Government and by the Great Barrier Reef Marine Park Authority.
The eastern boundary of tlie new Cairns section will follow the eastern boundary of the Great Barrier Reef Region itself. In the south, the boundary will be a line commencing at low water mark and coincident for most of its length with the line adopted for State purposes between the Cardwell and Johnstone Shires and thence passing immediately south of Taylor Reef to the eastern boundary of the Regions. In the north, the boundary will be a straight line commencing at low water mark on the mainland at 14 degrees 40 minutes south and passing through Two Mile Opening to the eastern boundary of the Region. Lizard Island is within the new Section of the Marine Park.
Once the Cairns Section is formally proclaimed, the Great Barrier Reef Marine Park Authority is required under the Act to develop a zoning plan for the Section and, in this context, will be seeking public submissions. All submissions which the Authority receives will be examined carefully and will be takbn into account in preparing a draft Zoning Plan. The draft plan will then be published so that members of the public are aware of the detailed nature of the Authority'S proposals and are able to comment on them. The draft Zoning Plan will be open for comment for at least one month.
I regret that the Queensland Government has not come in with us on the western boundary of the Cairns Section. But I believe there is support throughout the whole of Australia for the course of action being followed. I hope that now a decision has been made, co-operation between the two Governments in Park matters will proceed.

17 November 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 2845

BRISBANE AIRPORT: NEW WORKS
Mr HODGES-My question, which is directed to the Minister for Housing and Construction, concerns works associated with the construction of Brisbane's new international airport and terminal. Is the Minister aware of complaints of flooding from Nudgee residents living in the vicillity of the recently constructed Schultz Canal? Have new works in the area caused ponding of areas which would increase mosquito breeding? Have recent heavy rains and flood run-off causederosion of the banks of the Schultz Canal? If so, what action is proposed to rectify the damage?
Mr McVEIGH-I am aware of concern expressed some months ago by people who live in the general area of Nudgee beach. But, following the fall last week of 330 millimetres of rain-the sort of fall which can cause considerable flooding in areas of Brisbane-those fears have been largely allayed. A fall of that order l2 months ago caused a flood of 1t metres in the Brisbane Airport area. Last week, because of the protective works undertaken, there was no such flooding. It is now reliably estimated by engineers from Commonwealth and State government departments that the present works will withstand a flood six times larger than that of last week. To indicate to the honourable member for Petrie the severity of a flood of that order, the floods in Brisbane in 1974 were only five times larger than the flood of last week. The honourable member has drawn to my attention what could cause a problem with regard to mosquito breeding. I assure him that the Department will continue to monitor that so that any inconvenience will be limited.
In reply to the last part of the question, I advise the honourable member that, following representations made by him earlier in the year, the length of the rock wall was increased by 110 metres to 260 metres. That has withstood all the problems in the flooding last week, and Nudgee Road was not flooded. Another protective measure which it was fortunate we adopted was the erection of a man-proof fence around the lower reaches of Schultz Canal, because people were using the banks for fishing purposes and were not allowing the mangroves to be established to stabilise the banks. All in all, last week was a good test and the honourable member can assure the residents about whom he is concerned that the proposals adopted and the plans and procedures carried out can allay any fears that they may have had six months ago.
PERFORMING ARTS
Dr JENKINS--Will the Minister for Home Affairs and Environment recommend that the

urgent request by the Confederation of Australian Professional Performing Arts for a supplementary grant of $1.18m to the Australia Council be met in order to alleviate the crisis facing the performing arts? If the Minister does not propose to support this request, what steps is he prepared to take to restore funding to the Australia Council at a level that will allow it to maintain all artistically viable companies?
Mr WILSON-The amount of funds allocated by the Government for the arts, in particular that section of the development of the arts dealt with by the Australia Council, is determined at the time of the Budget. The amount provided to the Australia Council is provided to it on the basis that, except in the case of the Australian Opera, the Australian Ballet and the Elizabethan Theatre Trust, it is in the hands of the Australia Council to determine how it will divide its funds between, firstly, administration and, secondly, general support for the arts. Having made that determination, it is a matter then for the Australia Council to determinedistribution between its boards and the allocation within each of its boards to provide assistance and encouragement to artistic endeavour in this country.
It should be pointed out, however, that the Commonwealth Government is not the only sphere of government that has some significant responsibility for providing support to the arts. It is a matter also for State and local governments, the corporate sector and individuals. The Australia Council allocated the resources available to theatres and performing arts companies so as to give them encouragement by way of a reward for expanding the resources available to them. It was a matter for the Australia Council to determine whether it simply cut the amounts across the board in respect of an increased number of performing arts companies or whether it adopted a different approach so as to help others who should have and do have an interest in the development of our performing arts.

SINAI PEACEKEEPING FORCE
Mr HAYDEN-I ask a question of the Prime Minister. It refers to Australia's role in the proposed Sinai multinational military monitoring force. Can the Prime Minister inform the House how far the accumulation of weapons has proceeded among Israeli settlers in the Sinai and especially in the Rafah salient who are preparing to resist by force Israel's withdrawal next April as provided for in the Camp David Accords? Can he also tell the House how far advanced is the systematic takeover by these settlers of areas left vacant in the Sinai by people who have returned to Israel in advance of the withdrawal next April? Does this resistance affect in any way the understanding that Australia's participation in the peacekeeping force be conditional on all parties to the Accords adhering to them 'by word and deed', to use the words of the Prime Minister of 22 October in this House? Finally, if this resistance is not considered to be an infringement of the Accords, is it intended that Au~tralian troops be involved in removing or dealing with those settlers who resist withdrawal by force of arms?
Mr MALCOLM FRASER-Mr Speaker, that would not be part of the responsibilities of the multinational force. What was said in relation to that force obviously applies to commitments by the governments of Egypt, Israel and the United States. I have no reason to believe that those governments are not strongly in support of the maintenance of the Camp David Accords and the withdrawal processes in a proper time and a proper manner. I have no reason to believe that the Israeli Government will not fulfil its part of

Questions Without Notice

17 November 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 2849

the bargain in relation to its own people and its own personnel. I can advise the House of nothing else in relation to these particular matters at the moment except that from reading the newspapers honourable gentlemen will well understand that various negotiations are proceeding which could well involve a number of other countries making public statements about participation in the force.
One thing in an overriding way which has altered the views of many people in many different places is that since President Sadat's assassination people have suddenly been confronted with the fact that, even though there have been many proposals for peace in the Middle East, the Camp David peace process is the only one that has achieved anything. If that, therefore, is thrown out the window by lack of support in ways in which that support is needed and necessary and capable of being provided, then the only peace process that has achieved anything at all in the Middle East will have been abandoned. I think many countries have come to the view that it would be a major tragedy if that occurred. That is why there has been the obvious reassessment and, indeed, the bringing forward of any decision by Australia before there was a fully balanced force. We hoped that our decision would assist in achieving a proper balance in that force because that, of course, is necessary to give it its fully multinational character. I think the fact that many countries are reassessing their positions in relation to that and the fact that the Australian decision may have had some part, however small, in helping to bring that about, is an indication of the way in which a decision by a government in a country such as Australia can help in a peace process.
Let me emphasise again that this is an observing force and a monitoring force. It is not a fighting force and it is not an enforcing force. It is a monitoring and observing force. Of course, if fighting were to break out or if any of the parties were not to fulfil their obligations under the Camp David process, then the whole matter would have broken down. I hope that within the next week or two some of the countries may have got into a position in which they can make decisions and reinforce what has already occurred.

MEAT SHORTAGES
Mr O'KEEFE-Can the Minister for Primary Industry reconcile the low cattle prices and the decreasing population and yardings with the shortage of meat that is often complained about by housewives endeavouring to purchase meat supplies? Is the Minister satisfied that the present sales arrangements offer a satisfactory return to the producer and provide adequate supplies, economically priced, to the Australian consumer?
Mr NIXON-There has been a significant drop in the number of cattle available for slaughter over the past two or three years for a variety of reasons. Cattle numbers have fallen from 33 million head to about 24 million head. As a result, the volume of beef produced has declined significantly. I am not aware of any complaints from housewives that they are unable to purchase beef. Indeed, I thought the contrary position obtained. There are still significant volumes of beef available for the export market. I cannot say that the matter has been brought to my attention in any way at all. Despite the fall off in the numbers available for meat production, the fact is that the price of beef is very soft. I think this can be attributed mainly to the problems of the United States of America with its depressed economy, oversupply of white meat-chicken and porkand low beef prices. That reflects very much on the international scene. It reflects finally on the saleyard price paid for Australian beef. The last part of the honourable member's question raised queries as to the method of sale and the return to the producer. Studies on carcass classification are still in train. I know that a great deal of the producing sector of the industry hopes that it will advance the situation in terms of the sale price ultimately achieved by the producer. No final decision has been made on that yet. That still lies in the future. The producer is dependent upon supply and demand. That situation has existed in the industry for generations. I am unable to make any comment at this point on when work will be completed on carcass classification or even on the impact that that will have on the market place. I hope that it will be completed soon and that it will have some impact.

LAW REFORM COMMISSION
Mr VINER (Stirling-Minister for Industrial Relations)-Pursuant to section 35(3) of the Law Reform Commission Act 1973, I present the 1980-81 annual report of the law Reform Commission.

seek leave to have the statement incorporated in Hansard.
Leave granted.
The statement read as follows-
The Government is grateful for the comprehensive report of the Law Reform Commission which recommends changes in virtually all aspects of child welfare in the Australian Capital Territory and in the procedures of the ACT Children's Court and the Australian Federal Police. These matters are within the province of my colleagues, the Ministers for Administrative Services, Health and the Capital Territory as well as the Attorney-General.
On 15 May 1980 in response to the Senate Standing Committee on Constitutional and Legal Affairs, the Attorney General informed honourable "senators that, when tabling a report of the Law Reform Commission, the Government would indicate the arrangements for handling the Government's consideration of the report. He also mentioned that reports of the Commission require close and careful consideration before they can be implemented.
Careful study is clearly required in respect of this report on child welfare. My colleagues will be arranging for relevant officers to consider the report in detail. In addition, views will be sought from the ACT Children's Advisory Committee. As child welfare is a State responsibility relevant State Departments will also be consulted as will be the ACT House of Assembly and organisations concerned with child welfare in the Territory. In due course there may be a need for further discussions to be arranged with the Law Reform Commission.
Following those considerations of the report, my colleague the Minister for the Capital Territory expects to present options to the Government for child welfare legislation in the Australian Capital Territory.

PERSONAL EXPLANATION Mr HICKS (Riverina)-Mr Speaker, I wish to make a personal explanation.
Mr SPEAKER-Does the honourable gentleman claim to have been misrepresented?
Mr HICKS-Yes.
Mr SPEAKER-He may proceed.
Mr HICKS-On 20 October 1981, when we were dealing with Appropriation Bill (No.1) 1981-82, consideration of the report of Estimates Committee B, the honourable member for Melbourne Ports (Mr Holding) was making a speech regarding Aboriginal land rights. I refer honourable members to the bottom of the second paragraph on page 2241 of Hansard where an interjection was attributed to me. I was supposed to have said: 'Land grab'. That interjection was not mine.

LAW REFORM COMMISSION REPORT: CHILD WELFARE
Mrs KELLY (Canberra)-by leave-I want to take this opportunity to make a brief statement on the report on Child Welfare as it is an issue of great concern to the people of Canberra. I think it highlights the difficult problems we have in the Australian Capital Territory of getting changes to ordinances which in fact are antiquate<:i. The particular child welfare ordinances was described as: 'Confused both in concept and language and
a reflects values and attitudes of times gone by'.
This ordinance has been investigated now for period of three years. When I was a member of the Legislative Assembly I can remember that we investigated that matter for two years, brought down recommendations, but were then told that this would be investigated by the Law Reform Commission. It is an issue of great concern to the people of Canberra because it deals with social issues such as child abuse, what we do with children in need of care, care proceedings, how we

Australian Financial System

17 November 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 2855

treat juveniles, and what sorts of conditions we lay down for child care. It is an important issue that needs to be discussed by this community. I hope it will be fully aired in the community. I welcome the report. I thank you, Mr Speaker, for giving me an opportunity of making a brief comment.
AUSTRALIAN FINANCIAL SYSTEM: COMMITTEE OF INQUIRY
Report and Ministerial Statement
Mr HOWARD (Bennelong-Treasurer)-I present the final report of the Committee of Inquiry into the Australian Financial System.
Motion (by Mr Howard)-by leave-agreed to:
(!) That this House, in accordance with the provisions of the Parliamentary Papers Act 1908, authorises the publication of the report, and
(2) That the report be printed.
Mr HOWARD (Bennelong-Treasurer)-by leave-In tabling the final report of the Committee of Inquiry into the Australian Financial System, I wish to express the gratitude of the Government to the members of the Committee and the Committee secretariat for the work they have done over a period of almost three years in putting together this very comprehensive docu~ ment. I especially acknowledge the distinguished leadership role and contribution of Mr Keith Campbell as Chairman of the Committee. The Government is also very appreciative of the contribution that the financial and broader community of Australia has made to the workings of the committee.
The report is certainly the most detailed analysis ever of the Australian financial system. It has made a number of quite sweeping and fundamental recommendations for change which cover many aspects of the financial system, and which call for a considered and careful government response. The report will now be very closely studied by the Government. We will welcome public comment and debate. In particular the views of State governments will be sought as many recommendations affect the States. As -already announced, a special task group has been established to advise the Government on its response to the report.
Whilst for obvious reasons I am unable at this stage to respond to specific recommendations in the report, I can make two observations of a general character. The broad approach of the Committee has been to argue for a less regulated, more competitive and freer financial system and one which places emphasis on stability and investor

protection. Such an approach is consistent with the Government's basic objectives in this area. If implemented, many of the recommendations of the Committee would continue the process of measured and prudent deregulation in the financial system which has taken place in recent years.
However as the Committee itself recognises the report is essentially an economic document which concentrates on the efficient workings of the financial system. It is inevitable, therefore, that the Committee has made some recommendations which raise social and political sensitivities. Indeed, in certain areas the Committee has advocated changes to long-standing methods whereby governments have provided either assistance or a preference to different sections of the community. I have in mind, in particular, areas such as housing finance and the role of certain institutions in providing finance for different areas of the community, such as the rural and small business sectors.
In considering the report the Government must of necessity address itself not only to considerations of economic efficiency but also to social and political issues which are of enormous importance to many Australians in their everyday activity. It is important to note in this regard that the Committee hilS not questioned whether the Government should provide special assistance in any area nor has it questioned the level of that assistflnce. It has quite properly acknowledged that governments do have reasons other than purely economic ones for providing special assistance or conferring a preference. The Committee has simply made recommendations regarding the way' in which that assistance might be delivered or preference conferred more efficiently.. It is now up to the Government to assess carefully the Committee's recommendations. In doing so we shall naturally seek to achieve a balance between economic, social and political considerations and realities. However, I make it clear that the level of assistance to particular sections of the community should not and will not be an issue in the Government's consideration of the report. The Government's priorities in these areas are well known and will be maintained. Furthermore, the Government will not change existing arrangements for the provision of assistance in these areas unless it can be completely satisfied that the alternatives are not only more efficient but also fully meet the Government's social and political priorities.
I again thank all of those who have worked so hard to put together the report which touches virtually all of the financial institutions of our nation. The Committee's recommendations are

2856 REPRESENTATIVES 17 November 1981

Australian Financial System

wide-ranging and cover many areas of great complexity which will require thoughtful examination. I look forward to a period of public debate and scrutiny of the report which will be invaluable in guiding the Government's own deliberations upon its contents. I conclude by expressing on behalf of the Government my thanks to the members of the federal parliamentary Press Gallery for the first class manner in which all members of that Gallery, to my knowledge, have observed a 48-hour embargo on the publication of this report. I think documents of this kind ought to be made available to the Press well in advance of publication. I thank the members of the Gallery for their superb co-operation on this occasion.
Mr WILLIS (Gellibrand}-by leave-This is a momentous report. I thank the Treasurer (Mr Howard) for making a copy of it available to the Opposition over 24 hours before the announcement.
Mr Howard-Thirty-six hours; don't be mean.
Mr WILLIS-Well, I am being gracious. It is a most momentous report because of the extraordinary amount of information that it reveals about the operations of our financial system. I think we have all seen that in the interim report; there is, indeed, more of that in the final report. It is of interest because of the devastating impact on our society that would flow from the adoption of the recommendations of the Committee of Inquiry into the Australian Financial System. It is momentous because it confronts this Government with the full implications of its own ideology. To that extent it is indeed a very momentous report. We shall now see to what extent the Government really believes in its free enterprise rhetoric by the degree to which it adopts this report.
The report is based squarely on the Government's professed ideology, as shown firstly by its terms of reference. The terms of reference of this report make it quite clear that the committee has had to bear in mind the Government's free enterprise objectives and broad goals for national economic prosperity, et cetera. The emphasis on free enterprise objectives is of importance. The Treasurer, in announcing the establishment of this Committee and its terms of reference, made various comments which are quoted in the preface to the final report. Those comments ran along the lines that the objective of the inquiry was not to bring about more regulation by the Government-this is reported by the Government in thick black type; in other words it knew from the very outset what its instructions werebut that it was to bear in mind the Government's

free enterprise philosophy and not in any way to produce recommendations which would involve increased regulation. Those were the Committee's marching orders. To make sure that this objective was accomplished the Government established a Committee which was composed of persons who were very largely from the finance industry-a technocratic group the members of which undoubtedly believed in the ideology propounded by this Government.
The Chairman of this Committee was the head of a property development company; the chairman of a merchant bank which is 49.9 per cent owned by Citibank, a foreign bank and one of the largest banks in the world-he has since resigned from that position but he held it when he was appointed chairman of the committee; and a director of IBM Australia Ltd. In addition, the Committee was composed of a merchant banker; the head of our largest life insurance company; the head of the Australian Resources Development Bank, which is owned by the trading banks; a Reserve Bank of Australia official; and a Treasury official. I think that it can be seen from that list that there was no Ben Chifley in this committee of inquiry into our financial system, as there was in the previous committee in the 1930s. There was no-one with an opposing view or a broader view than the very narrow view of the technocrats involved in the finance industry of this country. I believe that the report suffers enormously because of that and because of the enormous constraint which was placed upon it by the terms of reference and the instructions from the Treasurer, which were faithfully reported by the Committee in its preface.
The report confronts the Government with the implications of the implementation of its own ideology. It does so because it shows quite clearly that deregulation-of which we have heard so much in this House-in the finance industry means higher interest rates for home buyers, higher interest rates for small business, higher interest rates for farmers, higher interest rates for government, increased government taxes and charges to cope with increased borrowing costs, less finance for housing, a more volatile exchange rate with decontrol of our foreign exchange and less control by the government over the form of development of the Australian economy. That is what deregulation means. This report brings out those implications very fully indeed. It also shows that reliance on competitive markets to ensure competition in the finance industry involves the introduction of foreign banks and higher levels of foreign investment in the Australian finance industry. It shows that reliance on private

Australian Financial System

17 November 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 2857

enterprise means selling off-the Committee strongly recommends this-the Commonwealth Banking Corporation, the State banks, the State government insurance offices, the Australian Industry Development Corporation, the Housing Loans Insurance Corporation, which the Government has already announced it intends to sell off, and the abolition of the Commonwealth Development Bank. If the Government finds an that a little difficult to carry out, the report says that at least an these organisations should be made to operate on exactly the same basis as the private enterprise organisations in those areas so that they will have no regulatory influence at all other than that of an additional competitor in the market. They are the broad implications of the Government's ideology in respect of this industry. Doubtless the Government win find some part of those regulations comforting indeed. I suggest that it will find many of them extraordinarily difficult to deal with.
The Government has a profound, severe problem in accepting the implications of its ideology, particularly in the area of interest rates. It is shown quite clearly in the report that the interest rates will be increased. A commissioned study reported in the report shows that it is expected that, with deregulation, interest rates on housing loans by banks would increase to up to 2 per cent above the government bond rate. At the time when the study became available to the Committee of Inquiry, the bond rate was 13.1 per cent. The bond rate is now 14.6 per cent at the lower end and 15 per cent at the top end, for longer term loans. A 2 per cent margin above that would mean housing loans at 17 per cent. Admittedly, the Inquiry does not.accept that this will necessarily be the case and gives the reason why it thinks that deregulated home loan interest rates would not rise to that margin above the bond rate, but it does not in any way deny that they would rise to the bond rate and perhaps go above it.
One of the reasons it gives for interest rates not rising to the fun 2 per cent above the bond rate is that there would be much less demand for hous-
ing. Of course, that is fairly likely and is also a very important implication of what has been recommended in this report, that is, that a lot less finance should be available for housing, that housing should compete in the market on a thoroughly unsupported basis with an the other demands for capital in this country. In that situation home buyers will have less capability of obtaining finance against the demands for finance by multinationals and others seeking to develop resources in particular. I believe that area is of extraordinary importance in this report.

The Treasurer in his statement today produced a report which the Opposition simply cannot accept. We cannot accept it on varying grounds. Firstly, we cannot accept its ideological basis. That basis is, of course, that reliance on markets will bring about a more optimum result for society than will government regulation. We believe that that is not likely to be the case because, firstly, markets are not perfect. Indeed, they are marked by oligopoly in this country, particularly in the banking industry, and there is thus a lack of real competition, especially as the Government has allowed amalgamations which have diminished the degree of competition. That diminution of competition is then used as a reason for the introduction of foreign banks, as we forecast at the time when
the Government allowed those amalgamations to occur.
At times the report seems to say that it is satisfied with the degree of competition. At other times it stresses the need for an effective trade practices Act to ensure competition. In fact, as we all know, the Trade Practices Act has been weak-
ened by this Government and reliance on the Trade Practices Act, as it is likely to exist under this Government, seems to us to be relying on a very weak reed. Even if markets are perfect, the results may not accord with what we as _11 society regard as optimal because we want to assist various disadvantaged groups. The report acknowledges that but says that it is best to give that assistance by other means, that is, by Budget measures such as subsidies or tax concessions. It does not attempt to define the increasing costs that would come from those measures or the implications for tax levels of having to provide increased taxes to cover the tax assistance that would be given through the Budget by way of subsidy or tax concessions to home buyers to overcome the enormous increase in interest rates which is likely to come from deregulation. By not giving that costing the report is quite deficient. Despite such a scheme it is quite clear from the report that housing would lose out to other investments.
We regard the impact of the implications of this report as thoroughly unacceptable. Increased interest rates at a time when interest rates are already at a record high level is something that society as a whole simply does not want. I cannot believe that the Committee, in propounding a proposal which would have that implication, really has its finger on the pulse of this nation. It suggests in the report that it feels that the community wants some further deregulation. I suggest that the last thing the community wants is higher interest rates for home loans. The last thing the community wants is more difficult availability of

2858 REPRESENTATIVES 17 November 1981

Australian Financial System

finance for housing. I believe in those circumstances that the implications of this report are quite important and that the report will be rejected completely by the community. Not only home buyers but also small businessmen and farmers will reject totally a report which has these implications. There are. many. other indications which we find deficient but Ido not have time to go into them.
We regard the report as deficient in a few particular ways. It is absolutely amazing that in the interim report and, so far as I can see, in the final report-I have not had time to read the 830-odd pages-there is no mention whatever of bank profits or the profits of any element of the finance industry. I find it an extraordinary deficiency that in this mammoth report, with the mountains of information that have been produced, it has not been thought necessary by the Committee to study the profits of the industry to see whether they are at a reasonable level or whether they may be regarded as excessive and whether that may have some implications for the degree of competition or the necessity for government regulation. There is not one mention of banking profits or the profits of any other element of the industry. This is at a time when banking profits have been soaring. Studies have shown that bank profits in Australia are amongst the highest anywhere in the world. Today we see in the Press that one of our largest banks has produced a 30 per cent increase in its profitability. This follows a similar increase announced by the National Bank of Australasia Ltd last week.
It seems to be an extraordinary deficiency that the Committee has not at any stage deemed it necessary to report on 'or study the level of profitability or the performance of banks at large. There is no mention of the way in which the banks have flouted the attempts of the Reserve Bank of Australia to impose quantitive controls on bank lending. They have lent in excess of the amounts to which the Reserve Bank made it clear it wished to limit them. I think that is also an extraordinary deficiency. There is an amazing reliance of a very unspecified form of the ability of welfare policies or some other budgetary policy, which is unexamined, not speJt out and uncosted, to offset the damaging effect that the deregulation procedures recommended in the report would have on the majority of people in this country. There has been no attempt at all to go into those details. The report simply ~tates that it will leave those matters to the welfare sector or to a budget measure of some sort. I regard that as a major area of deficiency.

Finally, there are some aspects of this report of which we approve. I will make those elements clear in the flext few days. We think there are a number of minor recommendations which are extremely helpful and important, some of which we believe fly in the face of government policy. I commend the report for that. I believe that overall this report is one which cannot be accepted by the Australian people. It puts the Australian Govt:mment on its mettle. It should say whether it really believes in its ideology. If it does so, I believe the Australian people will say that they no longer want this Government to run the country.

HOUSING
Discussion of Matter of Public Importance
Mr SPEAKER-I have received a letter from the honourable member for Reid (Mr Uren) proposing that a definite matter of public importance be submitted to the House for discussion, namely:
The willingness of the G~vernment to make the housing sector a victim of its misguided economic policies.
I call upon those members who approve of the proposed discussion to rise in their places.
More than the number o/members required by the Standing Orders having risen in their places-
Mr UREN (Reid) (3.l6)-Mr Speaker, after unemployment the most serious social problem in this country is housing. Yet over the past six years of this Government the growing problems of housing have suffered tragic neglect. As a result this country has a large and growing number of homeless people. Hundreds of thousands of people are living in substandard, over-priced rental accommodation. Thousands are living in caravans, temporary or makeshift accommodation. An increasing number of people are being excluded from the chance of buying their own home, particularly first home buyers. A growing number of people who are now paying off their homes are faced with crippling repayment burdens.
We have stagnation across the home building industry in all but a few regions in this country. In fact, the Victorian housing industry is in a situation of over 22 per cent under capacity. This has been occurring for several years. Home building firms are going to the wall. We have a loss of skilled labour from the home building industry but, under the policies of this Government, there is worse to come. The recent speech of the Treasurer (Mr Howard) is an indication of the line that has been followed by this Government. This Government has no policies for creating stability in the housing finance market. It has no intention of stimulating the home building industry or of expanding the provision of public housing. It has no concern to ensure security of housing for the Australian people. Under this Government we face a future of continuing housing neglect. This Government is pursuing a policy which deliberately sacrifices the housing sector for the purpose of expanding corporate profitability in the resource development sector.
Last night the Secretary of the Treasury, Mr John Stone, made clear the thrust of the Government's economic policy-its deliberate intention to make a victim of the housing sector. Today the

TreasllTer has tabled the report of the Campbell Inquiry into the Australian Financial System which signals a further phase of victimisation for the housing sector. I might say that it is also a threat and a sell-out to the overwhelming majority of ordinary Australian people. Yet in the view of Mr Stone and the Treasury-a view which has dominated Government thinking over the past six years-significant sectors of the Australian economy should be squeezed back in order to make more room for expanded foreign capital inflow and to facilitate a transfer of domestic funds towards investment in resource development.
The thrust of this Government's misguided policies is to transfer wealth away from wage and salary earners, the public sector, the housing industry and other domestic activities that the Treasury considers relatively unproductive. It has been the thrust of this Government's taxation policies, industry assistance policy, budgetary policy, monetary policy and interest rate policy to transfer funds towards a wealthy elite for investment in resource development. In the pubiic sector the housing industry has been one of the main victims of this Government's ruthless policy. Funds made available by the Federal Government for public housing have dropped from 3.9 per cent of total Budget outlays in 1974-75 to one per cent this year. In real terms Federal housing funds have fallen from $1,413m in 1974-75 to $399m in 1981-82. This Government has transferred not only funds away from housing but it has also transferred its national responsibilities to ensure housing security on to the States; and it has thrown it back on to the individual, and particularly that large group of individuals, details of which have been given in the Campbell report.
There is now a dangerous backlog of neglected housing needs which requires a substantial increase in outlays for public housing if Australia is to avoid serious social upheaval. But the private housing sector has also been a major victim of this Government's misguided policies and, given the glasshouse recommendations of the Campbell report, the private housing sector is likely to face even more serious disruption. The Campbell report has recommended:
All official intervention in the determination of bank deposit and lending interest rates should cease and existing controls should be abolished.
The acceptance of that recommendation-it is the philosophy of this Government to in fact accept it because the whole trend has been on that

2860 REPRESENTATIVES 17 November 1981

Housing

line-will mean a great tragedy not only now but also in the next decade or two when we have increasing population, both natural and through immigration, in this country. The recommendation for across-the-board deregulation of interest rates, if implemented, will create havoc in the housing industry. It will cause a further rise in home loan interest rates which will act to exclude a greater number of people from access to home ownership. Already four out of five wage and salary earners are excluded from access to affordable housing finance. Interest rate deregulation will impose heavier burdens on those people now paying off home loans. Already under this Government home loan interest rates for savings banks have risen in less than two years since February 1980 from 9.5 per cent to 12.5 per cent. On a $30,000 loan repaid over 25 years, that increase in the interest rate of 3 percentage points has raised the monthly repayments by $65 a week. People paying $262 a month now have to pay $327 a month which adds $19,800 to the total loan to be repaid over the 25 years. This has occurred to new home buyers and is also placing an increased burden on people paying off their mortgages at present. That is only a part of the story that will occur if deregulation continues.
The Campbell report recommends that housing loan interest rates should be determined by so called market forces and not be held down to affordable levels by Government intervention. The prevailing market interest rate at present on semi-government authority loans is between 15.5 per cent and 15.7 per cent. A $30,000 loan repaid over 25 years at 15.5 per cent interest rate would mean a monthly repayment of $395.92. That repayment would exclude virtually nine out of \0 wage and salary earners from access to housing finance. In addition, the charging of such an interest rate would impose intolerable burdens on existing home buyers and people paying off their mortgage at present. Monthly instalments would rise a further $69 a month andtotal loan repayments would rise something like $20,650 which would make the total repayments for a $30,000 loan over 25 years $118,780. To give the House further details I seek to incorporate in Hansard a table I have prepared on interest rates.
Leave granted.

Mr UREN-I thank the House. This table sets out the monthly loan instalments and total loan
repayments on interest rates at 9+ per cent, 12+ per cent and 15+ per cent for a $30,000 loan over 25 years. I have been talking about a $30,000 loan. House prices at the bottom end of the market in Sydney are $55,000. So if one is able to get a $30,000 loan one has to raise a deposit of $25,000. That is how impossible the position is. In fact, the median price of a house in Sydney is $84,000 while in Melbourne the median price is about $55,000 and a house at the bottom end of the market is about $45,000. So we can see the serious situation that arises in those areas. If free market forces are allowed to prevail, people will be repaying $134 a month more on a $30,000 loan than they were in February 1980, or a total increased repayment of over $40,000. That is what the policies of this Government are doing.
