Mr DEPUTY SPEAKER (Mr Millar)- Order! The honourable member's time has expired.
Mr McVEIGH (Darling Downs-Minister for Housing and Construction) (3.31 )-1 am gratified indeed that the honourable member for Reid (Mr Uren) has placed this matter before the House because it provides me with the opportunity of enunciating once again the Government's very real achievements and the responsibility and integrity of the Budget handed down in August. I want to rebut some of the honourable member's misguided statements. I have no wish to debate the report of the Campbell Committee of Inquiry into the Australian Financial System. It is not now the time to debate it, and we have made that quite clear. It is a pity that the honourable member for Reid did not read the statement made by the 1;'reasurer (Mr Howard) because he said that adequate time will be allowed for a proper and sober assessment of the report. I am a little concerned because although the honourable member for Reid does not usually do this, today he took a selective, biased reading of carefully culled quotations in order to give a distorted picture of the report. I am disappointed that he has seen fit to do that. However, on the credit side for him, for once he is right. The housing sector is sensitive to overall government economic policy. The housing sector needs an economic policy which maximises long run economic growth; provides sustained demand through lifting real family incomes; maintains a healthy rate of population growth; ensures young people can get jobs and, consequently, form households; keeps building costs within reason; promotes efficient finance markets; minimises inflation and price increases; and assists a steady availability of housing finance.
The Government's achievements in those areas are already clear and I want to repeat them. We have come a long way since the economic ruin of those disastrous years of 1972-75. We have rebuilt the economy. That is not an idle statement. I will cite statistics which show the job we have done. Inflation is down from 17 per cent to 9.1 per cent. In the last two years government policies have created 320,000 new jobs, which indicates that the

2862 REPRESENTATIVES 17 November 1981

Housing

economic climate we have created is one of confidence. Real income which is, after all, the thing that really counts, has increased by almost 4 per cent in the last year bringing increased living standards for all. Migration has increased steadily each year and is up from 21,000 in 1975-76 to almost 120,000 at present. Gross new farm product grew by 4 per cent in 1980-81 and private business fixed investment increased by 21.2 per cent in the same year. Quite obviously, that is a climate of confidence.
This climate of confidence has been reflected in the housing industry. The year 1980-81 saw approximately 148,000 housing commencements. That figure allows, of cou.rse, for revision of the Australian Statistician's June quarter figures. That is the highest level of commencements in almost a decade. It is interesting to note that commencements in New South Wales and Queensland were at an all time historical record level. However, there is always a price to be paid. We have to be realists. Economic achievements we have made so far have caused a price to be paid and that price has been discipline-a steady discipline over government spending, a discipline over the growth in financial aggregates, a discipline over the deficit, a discipline over the grandiose borrowing plans of State governments and local and semi-government authorities. Some State Ministers, unfortunately, seem to think that money grows on trees.
I want to mention a couple of key factors. The 1980-81 Budget was a milestone. It saw, for the first time in many years, a substantial domestic surplus. That, more than any other factor, will help us to reduce the pressure on interest rates. The second fact that is vitally important is that it reinforces our very real efforts to cut the heavy pressures that State, local and semi-government authority borrowings were placing on financial markets. We achieved that discipline through decisions we took in the Loan Council in June of this
year. It was a pity that our attempts at discipline and financial responsibility were not matched by the States. When they thought that they would have to exercise some discipline and that they would have to go out on the hustings and indicate to people that they could not give them the things that they wanted they roared like scrub bulls because, for the first time in their lives, many of them had to be responsible.
We realised that these decisions were not popular decisions, but being a government of responsibility and a government with steel in its spine our economic policy is all of one piece. It is a policy that emphasises responsibility and the need to get the fundamentals right. Our policies do not need

to be popular, but, by George, they have to work and they are working. This Government is a good government. It is not a government composed of members who believe that they can pander to the whims of everyone. That would be a government of weak reeds, swaying and bending in the breeze. One cannot look at our economic track record without conceding that our policies are continuingtowork.
Our decision to let banks increase their interest rates on home loans was not popular, but it was responsible. If we had bowed to pressure, such as that coming from the irresponsible fringes of party politics to keep interest rates artificially low, we would have witnessed the drying up of the finance pool. That would have been far morc:< unpopular. We would have been denying young people access to home loans. We were not prepared to do that. Unfortunately, there has been an inevitable easing in the number of loan approvals for housing. While the savings banks were able to increase their lending in early 1981, building societies, particularly those subject to urirealistic State government interest .rate con-. trois, did have to cut the funds they were providing to home seekers. Some State governments were unwilling to take responsible decisions prior to an election, but once the elections were over they did what we told them they should .have done.
We must get this matter in perspective. The year 1980 was probably the best year we have seen in terms of the number of loans approved by banks and building societies. There is already evidence that building societies are getting into a position where they too will be able to improve their lending. The latest advice coming to me is that,in most parts of Australia, their funds intake has improved, that they have been able to restore their liquidity levels and that, therefore, we can expect an improvement in the flow of loans from permanent building societies through 1981-82.
I do not and I know my Cabinet colleagues and other Government members also do not want to give the impression that the Government is not' concerned about or sympathetic towards young home seekers, home buyers, pensioners who have to rent their dwellings, Aboriginals, other special groups and those who are being helped through the Commonwealth-State Housing Agreement. The Government has' taken firm action in the 1981 Budget to lift its overall spending on housing and accommodation. I repeat, expenditure on housing and accommodation in the Budget rose

Housing

17 November 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 2863

from $555m last year to $660m this year, an increase of 18.9 per cent covering the whole spectrum of housing and accommodation for all types of people.
As honourable members know, we are currently reviewing our whole range of housing policies. This review underlines our flexibility in responding to changing circumstances. It will determine whether the Government is getting value for money from its current programs and examine ~he need for new home ownership assistance measures. However, it is not just a Commonwealth problem. Far too many Australians try to sheet home the blame for all financial problems to the Commonwealth Government. The States and local government have their own responsibilities. I support the statements made by the Treasurer in answer to a question without notice earlier this afternoon.
I want to speak about one area of concernconveyancing and stamp duty. In Victoria, a first home buyer currently pays up to $1,256 State stamp duty on the purchase of a house worth $50,000 with a $40,000 mortgage. However, in Tasmania a first home buyer receiving a full home savings grant pays absolutely no stamp duty at all. This shows that with a bit of flexibility, common sense and concern, the States could do a lot more to help first home buyers to bridge the deposit gap. In the case of Tasmania, I like to see this sort of co-operation between the States and. the Commonwealth.
I get a little concerned when I pick up the Press and read certain statements made by other Ministers for housing. I can assure the Minister of Housing in Victoria that vilification and vituperation are not the hallmarks of a fighter. Success in his own sphere of endeavour and responsibility is the hallmark of a fighter. The fact is that in Victoria, as I stated, it costs $1,256 for stamp duty. If the Victorian Minister followed the example of the Tasmanian Minister and reduced the charges in line with these in that State it would help considerably people who wish to purchase their first homes. Victoria spends less money on welfare housing than any other State in Australia. I challenge the Victorian Minister to do two things: Increase his State's spending on welfare housing to equal the Australian average and give a rebate of, say, $400 or $500 over and above the present rebate which is given to first home buyers. If he can do that, I believe that he can quite rightly claim to be interested in housing. What does he do at the present time? He has spent on advertising $15,000 which would help people to get their first home. He is wasting money in trying to camouflage the

issue. Instead of being a fighter and a man of responsibility he is what we might term a political gelding. He seems to think there is something to be done, that there is a problem and he would like to be involved. He realises that he cannot do anything about it, and all he can do is whinny and neigh.

All we are getting at the moment is a fight between the Liberal Party and the National Country Party about housing. When are the parties to the coalition going to come together and solve the people's problems?

Mr McVEIGH-Another area in which State and local governments bear a heavy responsibility is housing costs. Studies carried out for the Commonwealth-State ministerial council on housing costs showed that land development costs could be cut by thousands of dollars in many local government areas if State and local governments would work together to rationalise standards. The same sort of remark could be made about building regulations. Members of this House would be well aware of the manufactured housing awareness program that we have launched jointly with the States. This innovation could offer a real solution to housing cost problems in many of the high growth development areas. We will be supporting wholeheartedly a conference organised by the Master Builders Federation in April next year to keep up the pressure on State and local governments to remove the causes of unnecessarily high housing costs. The success of our economic policies in holding down building costs can be seen in the latest figures for material price increases. In the 12 months to October 1981 the figure was 8.4 per cent, contrasting with 15.4 per cent in the 12 months to October 1980.
In its figRt against rising costs, the Government has taken a firm and very positive line against the introduction of the 36-hour week which, on current estimates, would add somewhere between $4,000 and $6,000 to the cost of new housing. When we look at the last six years of the FraserAnthony free enterprise coalition Government, we can identify those economic gains which have placed Australia as one of the Western world's most favoured nations. With a degree of selfsatisfaction, although the battle -is far from over, we can view our controlled inflation rate, our employment situation and various other so-called barometers in the light of performances achieved

2864 REPRESENTATIVES 17 November 1981

Housing

in other developed countries. Our performance, by comparison, has been good. In its latest report, the Reserve Bank of Australia notes that we are maintaining our high rate of home ownership. Our home ownership rate of almost 72 per cent compares with 62 per cent in Canada, 50 per cent in England and Wales, 69 per cent in New Zealand, 65 per cent in the United States of America and 33 per cent in West Germany. While interest rates in this country have increased-;-no government, no Treasurer., no minister likes to see them at these levels-they are most favourable when compared with rates which apply in the housing markets of other countries.
The current rates in Australia for housing loans vary between 13t per cent and l4t per cent. Interest rates on new loans overseas are 15 per cent in the United Kingdom, 17 per cent in the United States and 21 per cent in Canada. The successes of these policies are self-evident and they are being reflected in various areas of commerce and the community together with an associated restoration of confidence.
Mr DEPUTY SPEAKER (Mr MilIar)- Order! The Minister's time has expired.
Mr DUFFY (Holt) (3,46)-We are all aware of the obsession of this Government with the need for restraint in government expenditure. However, I still find it impossible to understand why housing has been forced to bear such a disproportionate share of such restraint. Nearly all home buyers have to borrow to buy a home and, consequently, interest rates and the availability of finance are crucial factors in the quest for home ownership. Because of the policies of the Fraser Government, interest rates have reached record levels; there is intense competition for finance both in the private and public sectors to finance resource based industries and related infrastructure; the Government, through government bonds, is taking an increasing share of household savings; and the housing sector is being squeezed.
In this Parliament, we are subjected to claims that government policy is directed at giving scope for a wide range of innovative policies. When the Housing Assistance Bill 1981 was debated in this House on 2 June, the Minister for Housing and Construction (Mr McVeigh) stated:
The current Bill increases the Hexibility given to the States and continues the prc~ess of ensuring that assistance is directed to people in need.

This is absolute nonsense and such statements of principle bear no relationship to the facts! Yet the Minister for Housing and Construction continues to make such ridiculous statements while he presides over vicious cutbacks in funding for housing and supports policies which have caused record interest rates and a severe restriction on funds available for housing finance. As recently as 17 September 1981, on Estimates Committee E when considering estimates for the Department of Housing and Construction, the Minister again expressed confidence that our high level of home ownership by world standards would continue, a matter which he repeated today in the debate. It is difficult to know whether the Minister for Housing and Construction is ignorant of the facts or whether he prefers to ignore them.
Figures released yesterday by the Australian Bureau of Statistics show a substantial slowing down in the price of building materials. This is caused by the lower demand for housing because of record interest rates and lack of housing finance. Commenting on the figures, the Deputy Executive Director of the Housing Industry Association, Dr Silberberg, stated:
There are now thousands of Australian families squeezed out of the home market because of the present level of interest rates.
Dr Silberberg further predicted that, unless this Government moves quickly to restore home ownership opportunities, there will be 15,000 fewer homes produced in 1981-82 with a shortfall of at least 22,000 new dwellings in 1980-81. This Government refuses to face the fact that half of all employees in Australia earn less than $200 a week. Yet to borrow $30,000 to purchase a home requires a gross weekly income of about $350. The Government's cavalier attitude to increases in interest rates ignores the fact that a one per cent increase in interest rates reduces borrowing power by about $2,500 for a family which can afford mortgage payments of $1 00 weekly.
The policies of this Government have caused tragic consequences. These are clearly seen in the report of the Commonwealth Scientific and Industrial Research Organisation Building Research Division which states that the number of families able to afford maximum loan conditions for an average lower cost house in Melbourne has fallen from 80 per cent 10 years ago to less than 50 per cent now. It makes one wonder whether the Minister looks at any of these independent reports or whether he continues to prefer to run the line that, because of the fact that we have always had a high rate of home ownership, it will continue. The facts indicate quite clearly that, unless this Government takes action as a matter of urgency,

Housing

17 November 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 2865

that claim by the Minister will not be able to be maintained.
Home buyers have become so desperate for housing finance that they are now prepared to borrow from finance companies at exorbitant interest rates. The magnitude of this problem is clearly seen in the figures released by the Australian Bankers Association Research Directorate on 3 September this year. These figures disclose that value of housing loans from banks grew by 5.2 per cent over 1980-81. For the same period, the value of housing loans from building societies increased by 2 per cent but the value of loans from finance companies for that period increased by a massive 44 per cent. The response of the Minister for Housing and Construction to this information clearly indicates the appalling attitude of this Government on this crucial issue. His reaction was that, if people are prepared to go to finance companies for housing finance, this indicated that they still have a commitment to home ownership. What is does show, despite the view of the Minister, is that people have become desperate to obtain finance to purchase homes. It is not a matter of them having a commitment; the fact is that it is the only finance available to far too many people who are desperately seeking finance to purchase a home. Many of these people borrow money from finance companies on a first mortgage but the tragedy of people who borrow that sort of money on second mortgages is seen in what is happening throughout Australia at the moment.
The Treasurer (Mr Howard) has recently indicated that he does not accept the fact that mortgagees auctions are being conducted throughout Australia at the moment. That is incredible because it completely refuses to accept the facts. At the present time people have been caught and locked into the increases in interest rates from building societies and banks but more particularly the people I indicated earlier who are forced to borrow on second mortgages from finance companies, are finding it absolutely impossible to maintain their mortgage payments. The position now is that not only will people find it impossible to purchase a house but also, tragically, so many people who have a house are likely to lose it in the near future. In Victoria, the problem of the public housing sector has reached a desperate stage. Despite the claims today by the Minister, it was announced that Victoria will, over the next year, pay to the Commonwealth Government $2.6m more in interest and loan repayments than it receives in housing allocation.
Mr Uren-That is for public housing.

Mr DUFFY-That is for public housing as the honourable member for Reid has indicated. Despite the rhetoric of the Minister this Government has indicated its contempt for those Australians so desperately in need of housing. The reality is that this Government has singled out housing for unprecedented cutbacks in funding. Public housing is the only category to have suffered a fall in Commonwealth outlays in real terms. Clearly, when one looks at the tragedy of people unable to get a home, I agree entirely with what the honourable member for Reid (Mr Uren) has said-after unemployment this is the most significant social problem in this country. This Government must be judged as having failed dismally.
Mr. ROCHER (Curtin) (3.56)-At the outset I acknowledge that, given that interest rates have risen rapidly, there is concern in Government circles for the house purchaser who is suffering hardship. Already it has been widely reported that Cabinet has before it for consideration proposals which will come to grips with short term problems. This well-publicised circumstance, coupled with whatever is contained in the report of the Campbell Committee of Inquiry into the Australian Financial System-which I have not yet seen-and whatever it recommends affecting housing finance gives the lie to any alleged lack of
care on the part of the Government and, at the same time, gives rise to doubts as to the motives for raising this matter of importance at this time. Knowing that the Minister for Housing and Construction (Mr McVeigh) has proposals before the Cabinet and under consideration, it is obvious that he will not pre-empt Cabinet deliberations in this debate; not that it is proved to be necessary, given the argument advanced by Opposition speakers. However, I am not under that constraint because I do not know the details of what is before the Government.
The problems associated with higher mortgage interest rates seem to have elicited three short-run reactions: Firstly, income tax deductibility or tax credit on housing loan interest; secondly, interest subsidy; and, thirdly, hardship relief. Any arbitrary rules which must be attached to either tax deductions, credits or interest subsidies are almost certain to exclude some cases of demonstrable hardship. Studies undertaken by the Perth Building Society, the largest in my State of Western Australia, and the first or second of the three largest and most reputable building societies in Australia, indicate that at most 1t per cent of purchasers who have taken out loans over the past two years may be in some sort of difficultysometimes serious difficulty. I might add that that estimate of 1t per cent of purchasers is not the

2866 REPRESENTATIVES 17 November 1981

Housing

Perth Building Society's experience. In its experience, the percentage is much lower. Therefore, the estimate probably errs on the high side.
These purchasers deserve-I am confident that they will receive-priority in any considerations now before the Government. Reference has already been made by the Minister to expectations as to new housing starts during the financial year. He referred to an estimated and staggering 140,000 to 150,000 dwelling units expected to be commenced this year, which is up 13 per cent on the previous year-hardly evidence of restraint by the Government in relation to its housing policy or to the housing sector generally. The largest national housebuilder in Australia, Jennings Industries Ltd, has aln,ady recently reported a 30 per cent increase in new orders over last year. Other cottage builders with whom I have consulted, with products which cater for the market, have similar stories to tell. There has been an increase in orders. Sydney might be the exception but that can probably be explained by the extraordinary level of dwelling construction in that city over the past three years. House builders whom I know and to whom I have spoken are already anticipating a scarcity of resources, particularly labour in metropolitan and probably remoter areas. Well they might, because the non-residential construction sector and the building sector generally are operating at a very high level. Labour is interchangeable between non-residential, high density residential and the cottage building sector. If anything is likely to exacerbate the problem of rapidly increasing house building costs it is a scarcity of suitable skilled' labour. I suggest that that is potentially in the offing. The maintenance of what I see as a resurgence of the cottage building industry will be the catalyst for further cost pressures to the detriment of intending purchasers of houses.
With that scenario the Government's overall economic management remains of paramount importance. Within the framework of continuing sensible economic management Cabinet will consider and decide what must be done with the best interests of those in need and the Australian tax~ payer in mind. It must be recognised that the outcome of adopting some of the alternatives open to the Government will not be confined to obvious and costed impact on expenditure, either in the form of subsidy or, in the case of tax credits or tax deductibility, in the form of revenue forgone.
Both tax credits and tax deductibility schemes will have the effect of making house loans cheaper, thereby increasing the demand for new housing. Given likely pressures on costs, to which 1 have already referred, a likely outcome will be

increased prices for houses which will increase the average size of home loans and, anticipating a shortage of funds, further upward pressure on interest rates. The net result of tax credit or tax deductibility schemes will be that net loan repayments will end up being the same as they were before either scheme was adopted. In considering what is to be done for those currently suffering hardship, care must be taken not to look for quick-fix short term solutions which have after effects which will be of detriment to future purchasers.
The Opposition advocates control of interest rates. It does not say how funds will be attracted into lending institutions and become available to those seeking to purchase a house. The options open to the Commonwealth Government in relation to levels of interest rates in the one sector where it has control, that is the banking sector, appear to be these: Firstly, by exercising control in the areas in which it has the legal power-the banking sector-the Commonwealth may stipulate that bank borrowing and lending rates are arbitrarily kept at levels below those offered to investors and borrowers generally. Or, secondly, it may allow interest rates to rise to something like the going market rate enabling institutions such as banks and building societies, which are predominantly lenders to dwelling purchasers, to attract investment funds. The first option has been disregarded because the effect would be a general absence of funds from investors who could logically be expected to seek better returns elsewhere. Reductions in, or an absence of, investment funds would see demands for borrowing outstrip available funds and probably culminate in demands for injections of taxpayers' funds for long term lending as one industry association did a couple of years ago. The alternative, which has been adopted, has the advantage of attracting investors' funds into institutions permitting borrowers access to funds which would not otherwise be available even though the interest on those borrowings will be, and have been, at rates higher than we in Australia have been accustomed to paying. Although it is difficult to be categorical on the point it seems that the availability of funds at current interest rates remains good. That was supported, I think, by a recent article in a newspaper which indicated that building societies had funds available, an improved situation over that which they experienced earlier this year.
It is a basic tenet of the coalition Government parties to maintain a higher level of home ownership in Australia. To that extent we have been successful, as evidenced by the fact that Australians enjoy one of the highest levels of private

Censure ofMinister for Communications

17 November 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 2867

home ownership in the world. As evidence of our concern I refer to a program already under way. The Opposition has conveniently ignored the fact that tax deductibility for interest rates was abandoned in Australia after the demise of the Whitlam Government. It was replaced by the Homes Savings Grant Scheme which had the advantage of allowing a front-end reduction of debts up to $3,000 thereby reducing the principles secured by mortgages and, in turn, eliminating the interest commitment of recipients over the nominal life of a mortgage. I add that I am sympathetic to any proposition which calls for that benefit to be paid at, say, the date of occupation or settlement rather than nine months later.
Mr SPEAKER-Order! The honourable member's time has expired. The discussion is now concluded.

INSURANCE (AGENTS AND BROKERS) BILL 1981
Bill received from the Senate, and read a first time.

Second Reading
Mr JACOBI (Hawker) (4.35)-After seven years of struggling it is with pleasure that I move the second reading of this important and much needed legislation. I move:
That the Bill be now read a second time.
This private member's Bill was presented in the Senate by Senator Evans on 19 August this year. In his second reading speech he dealt fully with the purposes and proposals of the Bill. I commend Senator Evans for his foresight and initiative. In essence, the Bill, with minor modifications, seeks to implement the recommendations of the Australian Law Reform Commission's report entitled 'Insurance Agents and Brokers'. What is particularly pleasing to note is that the Senate supported the Bill without any division on 29 October this year. Equally important, the Senate flatly rejected

the reasons upon which the Treasurer (Mr Howard) claimed in this House on 10 June this year that a Federal government should not legislate at a national level and that it is best left to the States. In fact, no body of opinion with any understanding of the insurance industry supports the Treasurer's stand, with the exception of one or two Press editorialists. The statement of the Treasurer-in repeat what I said in Junedestroys five years of detailed and constructive investigation and recommendations on the need for legislation for insurance brokers. It is a slap iri the face to the industry, to the community and, above all, to Professor David St Leger Kelly and the staff of the Law Reform Commission, To my knowledge up to now the Treasurer has not even had the courtesy to pay tribute to the work of the Commission. Let me congratulate equally Senators Bolkus, Watson, Jessop and Missen on their support in the Senate for this Bill. I recommend that honourable members opposite study the excellent speech of Senator Missen in particular.
Let me canvass the Government's track record in this matter. As I said on 10 June, the need for regulation has long been recognised overseas and within State jurisdictions by all sections of the community involved in the brokering iridustry. The Queensland Insurance Act 1960-1976 provides for the licensing of brokers. The United Kingdom brought in legislation in 1977. The Whitlam Government drafted legislation before it was dismissed in 1975. Since then, there have been a number of somersaults by this Government. In April 1976 the then Minister assisting the
Treasurer announced that the Government would introduce legislation for the supervision of insurance brokers. In March 1977 the Government reversed the decision and announced its intention to pursue a scheme for self-regulation in conjunction with the Insurance Brokers Council. In 1979 the Treasurer announced that he would seek government approval to develop legislation for voluntary regulation arrangements for brokers. Six months later he again changed his mind. In effect, the Government refused to grasp the nettle claiming that there was no consensus amongst the various bodies representing brokers on the forms of control that ought to be or might be introduced.
The Government cannot claim in its defence that the insurance broking lobby is against legislation. Bodies representing the insurance brokers in all sections of the insurance industry have persistently supported and demanded legislation. We have the spectacle of the Western Australian Government being obliged to bring in legislation. The New South Wales Minister has repeatedly

2870 REPRESENTATIVES 17 November 1981

Insurance (Agents and Brokers) Bill

indicated that if the Commonwealth fails to act New South Wales will be obliged to do so. The Victorian Minister is committed to bringing in legislation. Unquestionably the only two States left will be obliged to bring in legislation to protect the consumer section of the industry. What will the Minister have? I will tell him what he will have. He will have a plethora of mis-matched, ineffective, inefficient State laws. The situation is scandalous.
Let me briefly advise. the House just what a mess this Government's negative approach will inflict on a national industry. In Western Australia the licensing system excludes life insurance but includes general insurance. If a life insurance agent is also selling general insurance he is obliged to register. As I said, Queensland has had legislation in respect of the insurance industry for many years but it is confined, in effect, to general insurance. It applies only to brokers. If a life insurance agent is selling general insurance policies he is not obliged to register. New South Wales intends to legislate. It will follow the Western Australian Act but will include life insurance. Victoria has made no official announcement but has indicated that it will be obliged to legislate. Tasmania and the Northern Territory have made no indication at this point that they will legislate. In South Australia we have the spectacle of the State Liberal Government implementing negative licensing. What this means is that, if a broker commits a misdemeanour or an offence and fails to comply with government criteria, he will be deregistered even though there is no registration. What a shambles we will have. Senator Missen, in support of this much needed legislation, perhaps summed up this aspect more cogently. He said:
I think it also ought to be borne in mind that the report of the Commission came down in 1980, the consumer affairs Ministers of the States met in Melbourne after that-on 14 November I980-and resolved as follows:
Insurance brokers
Ministers agreed to express their concern to the Commonwealth that the need for urgent action to implement the Australian Law Reform Commission's proposals to regulate the activities of insurance brokers.
It was agreed that there was a need for the Commonwealth to examine legislation involving a fidelity fund to compensate consumers for losses caused by cases of brokers failing to perform. It was also agreed that the legislation should provide for the registration of insurance brokers.
This is not something which has been imposed on the States, it is something which they have requested. The Victorian Minister urged the Federal Government to introduce legislation to register insurance brokers and require them to set up trust and fidelity funds. He pointed out that there had been recent .:xamples of collapses of insurance brokers. One liquidator had been

appointed for Kinloch (Insurances) Pty Ltd with debts of $3m. The same month Anthony H. Brown and Associates Pty Ltd went into liquidation and One Stop Insurance Shop Pty Ltd collapsed with debts of $322,000. So there is a constant need. Those examples call for action. State Ministers have supported those claims and demands.
The legislation is based upon the report. It is also based upon defalcations, details of which are set out in the second reading speech, which totalled about $7m in the years before the Law Reform Commission Report. From the time that the report was prepared at least another $7m appears to have been defaulted. The total is probably more like $20m now because it is not known precisely what are some of the losses among a number of the companies. It is utter nonsense to claim that this important aspect of insurance, namely the role of the intermediary-that is the broker or agent-can be isolated from the national legislature' and dealt with on a fragmented basis by the States. The Commonwealth Parliament has already intervened and taken action in regard to insurance. We have the Life Insurance Act of 1945 and the Insurance Act of 1973. Certain regulatory bodies and commissioners are operating already in those two fields. As is proposed in the Bill they will playa very active role in the regulation of brokers. Intermediaries are an integral part of the insurance industry. Laws must be not only uniform but also national if the industry is to have the effectiveness, the efficiency and the credibility it deserves.
There are two myths Paul Revered by the Treasurer that must be destroyed once and for all. The Treasurer constantly states that losses represent 0.1 per cent of total broker premiums. My answer is that a vast majority of broker premiums relate to commercial and other large scale insurance. The losses as a percentage of consumer insurance premiums would be much higher than 0.1 per cent. Furthermore, some policyholders will suffer a loss of 100 per cent of premium. Secondly, the Treasurer claims that 92 per cent of losses represent amounts due to insurers. What is the answer to that? This analysis is utterly misleading. Every cent of that 92 per cent of losses is recoverable by insurers from their clients. Furthermore, the remaining 8 per cent owed directly to those insured is a sizable sum. Losses between 1967 and 1979 were 8 per cent of $7m, or $560,000. I seek leave to incorporate in Hansard a document setting out what the Bill does and what it provides.
Leave granted.

Insurance (Agents and Brokers) Bill

17 November 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 2871

The document read as follows-
INSURANCE (AGENTS AND BROKERS) BILL 1981
What the Bill does
I. Statutory System of Registration of Brokers
Administered by Insurance Commissioner and Life Insurance Commissioner;
Obligations upon registered brokers:
compulsory proFessional indemnity and fidelity guarantee insurance (cI.17);
separate insurance broking account in which all premium and claim moneys must be kept and investment of which is limited to 'prescribed securities' (cl.2l);
regular inspection or audit of books, accounts and records (cL33);
full disclosure to insured and insurer of details of insurance contracts, including details of the broker's remuneration (cL25);
Registration liable to be suspended or cancelled if broker or its employee convicted of any offence relating to insurance or to dishonest conduct (cL20).
Funding: The system of registration is intended to be selffunding. Based on an estimated registration fee of $200 and an expected registration of 1,000 brokers, the budget of $200,000 would allow For 2 permanent staff plus 2 casual. It is not envisaged that there will be elaborate investigative machinery.
2. Regulating the Conduct ofI nsurance Agents
Bill establishes clear demarcation between agents and brokers (cl.ll);
Insurer responsible to the insured for any conduct of his agent which is relied on by the insured (cLIO) except where it would be unreasonable for the insured in his particular circumstances to rely on the agent's conduct;
No-one other than a registered broker may describe himselF or his business in such a way as to mislead a person into believing that he was a broker (cl.30);
Agents are prevented from behaving as brokers because cl.28 (as amended in the Senate) requires them to forward all premium moneys to the insurer forthwith, thus removing any possibility of investment of premium moneys by anyone other than a registered broker.
3. Regulating the Conduct of Brokers and Agents Alike
Payment of premium by an insured to an agent or a broker discharges the insured's liability to the insurer (cI.l3) i.e. the agent or broker receives the premium as agent for the insurer and the insured is not vulnerable if the broker collapses or defaults before paying over the premium;
Bill creates offences for an agent or broker to deliberately misrepresent the cost or effect of, or benefits available under, an insurance policy or to misrecord inFormation on a proposal or claim form or to advise an insured or intended insured todoso (cI.l2);
Penalties are graded on the same basis as those in the Companies Act 1981 and the Securities Industry Act 1980, and are of similar severity for similar offences.
Mr JACOBI-I thank the House. I will indicate the principal headings of the Bill. The first is 'Statutory System of Registration of Brokers'; the second is 'Regulating the Conduct of Insurance Agents'; and the third is 'Regulating the Conduct of Brokers and Agents Alike'. The legislation is

well set out at the beginning of the report of the Law Reform Commission. The Commission stated:
The private insurance industry is today organised on a national basis. It is undesirable that, in important aspects of the law governing relationships with the insuring public, this national industry should be subject to vague and uncertain rules developed long before the growth of modern insurance. It is equally undesirable that it should be subject to differing legislative requirements From one jurisdiction to another. The needs of the industry and of its customers can only be met by development of a national law of insurance contracts .in Australia. This law should promote:
uniformity, to the extent that the Federal Constitution permits
clarity, by removing doubts in existing case law and statutes--
I will deal with this matter later-
relevance, in recognising the realities of the respective positions of the insured, the insurer and the intermediaries (agents and brokers) who Facilitate and effect contracts of insurance.
I believe that statement sets out very fairly the need, and the recommendations that justify the action proposed in the Bill. I also note that the need for regulation has been supported by the Campbell Committee of Inquiry into the Australian Financial System. I seek leave to incorporate in Hansard page 360 and page 363 of the report.
Leave granted.
The pages read as follows-
IV INSURANCE AGENTS AND BROKERS
A. Background
20.187 There is little regulation of insurance brokers or agents in Australia and selF-regulation has been fragmented.
20.188 Until recently, Queensland has been the only State which has provided for the licensing of brokers. In that State, requirements are laid down For the handling of premiums and the delivery of documents and the State Insurance Commissioner has powers of supervision extending to the suspension or cancellation of a licence. In other States the actions of both agents and brokers are constrained, by and large, only by common law." (In August 1981,Iicensing requirements were introduced for brokers in Western Australia.)
.. Details of the requirements applying in Queensland and of the efforts of agents and brokers to regulate themselves are outlined in Chapter 16 of the Interim Report.
20.189 The Law Reform Commission published its report on Insurance Agents and Brokers in September 1980. A number of its recommendations relate to issues which have been raised with the Committee or which bear on the stability and efficiency of this sector of the financial system.
B. Approach to Regulation
(a) Agents
20.190 The Law Reform Commission cites various State Consumer Affairs Departments/ Bureaus as noting complaints in regard to misrepresentation by agents of: .
premiums payable for motor vehicle insurance cover; the size of surrender values for life insurance policies; and the type oflife insurance policy being bought.

2872 REPRESENTATIVES 17 November 1981

Insurance (Agents and Brokers) Bill

20.191 Neither common law nor statute are clear regarding the insurer's responsibility for the actions of their agents; this confusion has been compounded by attempts to exclude liability via contractual exclusions.
20.204 On the other hand, the Committee would not favour sole reliance on self-regulation. Governments clearly have a role in protecting individual consumers against fraud and misrepresentation.
20.205 The Committee also stresses the desirability of consistent regulation. This has been recognised by the Treasurer, who has expressed the hope that any state legislation will recognise the benefits of the existing self-regulatory arrangements and have regard to the advantages associated with uniformity in controls. He has indicated the Government's readiness to assist in achieving uniformity between the States in this area.
20.206 The Committee favours a system of 'co-regulation', with government legislation laying down the ground rules for an arrangement basically involving self-regulation by an appropriate industry body on which there would be some government representation. It believes early action should be taken by the Government to ensure that appropriate cooperative national legislation is developed. This could provide for the holding of funds in trust accounts in connection with their business as brokers, as recommended by the Law Reform Commission.
20.207 It is also desirable that guidelines for the disclosure of information by brokers to their clients should be developed, as this would facilitate caveat emptor. Such guidelines might cover disclosure of such matters as:
the nature of any contractual relationship between an insurer and the broker; and
the remuneration received by a broker from the insurer in respect of a client's business.
20.208 On the latter question, as the broker represents the policyholder, not the insurer, it is reasonable to expect that such information should be made available. As well, details of brokerage fees in other areas of the economy are invariably available to those using brokers (e.g. stockbrokers, real estate agents).
20.209 The Law Reform Commission has suggested that disclosure of such information would:
provide a concerned client with the information which is essential for a check to be made on whether the broker's remuneration appears unusually high, a fact which may itself suggest that a broker has been motivated more by his commission than by his client's needs. Disclosure would also encourage and promote informed assessment and, perhaps, questioning by the client of the cost of the services of an insurance broker. It might well lead to a client comparing the cost of the services of different brokers. There are limited opportunities for inquiry and comparison when the client is not told of the amount paid to his broker. As the amount of remuneration is already determined as between insurer and broker, disclosure to the insured would not itself involve significant costs and might in due course even encourage a reduction in the total costs of insurance. The market forces which operate in favour of competition can be most effective when the consumer is made aware of the cost of services rendered on his behalf.
20.210 The Committee endorses this view.
Mr JACOBI-I thank the House. The purpose of this Bill is to deal with the severe problems faced by members of the insuring public in relation to misconduct by insurance agents and brokers. There are thousands of insurance agents and brokers in Australia. The vast majority of

them act honestly in the interests of their clients, but cases of misconduct occur and, when they do, severe losses are needlessly inflicted on honest people. This Government, indeed the Treasurer, has shown a distinct lack of enthusiasm for the controls suggested by the Commission after an exhaustive inquiry. Yet the Commission's central recommendations have the support of the insurance industry as a whole. It is for that reason, and because of the factors noted in the Commission's report, that Senator Evans has introduced this Bill.
Insurance intermediaries are of two classes, namely, agents and brokers. Agents act for one or more insurers, not for insureds. Brokers act for insureds and place business with several insurers. A person may call himself a broker whether he is a true broker or an agent tied to a single company. The public is readily misled by a practice like that. The major problem with agents is that some insurers will not accept responsibility fpr them. They choose their agents, but when something goes wrong it is for the insured to worry. The insurer simply denies liability. In the past this has led to scandalous decisions. Insurers have made insureds sign a statement to the effect that the insurer's agent is not the insurer's agent at all. Instead, he is the agent for the insured. The courts have upheld this charade. Insurers have also been able to avoid liability by denying that their agents had authority to do certain things. We all know how agents and employees often fill in proposal forms themselves. When they make a mistake, the' insurer should accept the responsibility. This Bill provides for that. Unfortunately this often does not happen.
The most frequent complaint that I have had to handle is in relation to motor vehicle insurance. A car salesman is an agent or a sub-agent for insurance. He offers to arrange insurance for the buyer, often at scandalously high rates. He fills in the form on the strength of the answers given him by the buyer. The buyer tells him he has had two accidents in the last five years. The salesman knows that the insurer may not be willing to insure a buyer with that record, so he writes the word 'no' alongside a question concerning the buyer's claims history. In that way he makes sure of the contract and, would you believe it, his commission. If the insured makes a claim, the inaccuracy of the answer as recorded by the agent is discovered. The insurer denies liability on the basis of the insured's misrepresentation, When he says: 'It was your agent who made the mistake', the only answer is: 'Too bad'. The insured loses out. I suggest that position is simply intolerable.

Insurance (Agents and Brokers) Bill

17 November 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 2873

Disreputable insurers are encouraged by the law to have no concern for the behaviour and ethics of their agents. This is bad business practice. Insurers have a duty to train and to supervise their agents. They will uniformly do so only if they are made legally responsible for their conduct. The main problem with brokers is well known to all members. There has been an alarming number of broker collapses over the last decade. The Law Reform Commission at page 61 of the report stated that it had been able to find losses of $7m. from broker insolverrcy between the years 1970 and 1979. The true figure for that period will almost certainly exceed $lOm. Since 1979 matters have become much worse. In the last 18 months losses have exceeded $7tm, more than was proved to have been lost in the preceding decade. It is estimated now to have reached $20m. This is simply scandalous. One might say that much more is lost by businesses of various types which have become insolvent. Why then is there all the fuss about brokers?
Broker insolvencies are a matter of particular disquiet because what is lost in a broker insolvency is not the broker's own money, but money paid to him for transmission to an insurer. Brokers use premium funds in a quite unacceptable way in running their own businesses. Solicitors, real estate agents and others are forbidden to act in this way, but brokers are free to indulge in the luxury of spending other people's money. The result of the practice is that the premiums of thousands of clients are dissipated in badly run businesses and bad investments. Obviously either the insureds or the insurers are the losers. Under existing law it is not clear in fact who it is. Many insurers claim the right either to cancel the relevant policies or to ask for payment all over again when premiums are lost in this way. That leads to enormous confusion, to additional costs for the insureds, to legal disputes and to a general lack of confidence in the probity of the industry.
Insurers allow brokers to retain premium funds on credit for months at a time. The details are set out in the Law Reform Commission report at pages 62 to 64. It is unthinkable that they should be permitted any longer to put the loss upon innocent insureds. If, for business reasons they want to give brokers credit, they must pay the cost of that de,cision. Making sure that insurers cannot avoid this responsibility is not enough. Successive insurance companies have warned of another danger associated with broker insolvency, namely, the failure of one or another, or more, of broker reliant insurers. The confusion and loss to innocent insureds in the event of an insurer collapse are well known to all members. They are worse

than those directly resulting from the insolvency of a broker. An insured who has a claim against the company for the loss of his house simply cannot enforce it. If he gets paid at all, it will only be in part, usually a small part, and then only after many years have passed. The failure of the Government to heed the warnings of successive insurance commissioners and of the Law Reform Commission itself is reprehensible.
What can be done to reduce the risk? Laws cannot make people honest, as the Treasurer has often said to me time after time, but laws can inhibit people, including brokers, from acting dishonestly. They can provide ameans of uncovering dishonesty and of punishing it when it is revealed. By making the price too high, the risk of crime is reduced. Moreover, by no means do all broker insolvencies arise from deliberate dishonesty. Many brokers already keep their premiums separate from their own moneys. Others would certainly do so if the law obliged them. They are encouraged by the present law to indulge in bad business practices. Dishonesty is simply irrelevant.
What should be done to prevent this type of practice? Clearly, premiums and other insurance moneys should be held in trust accounts and their use should be restricted. In the event of insolvency, they would not be available for the general body of creditors, but only for the individuals and insurers to whom they are due. The practice of investments might be continued, but subject to restrictions aimed at reducing the risk of poor investment by a broker. A set of laws should be implemented so that it would be easily avoided without back-up mechanism. Some supervision, minimal in scope, is essential. Audits on an annual basis should be required. To enable this to be supervised, all insurance brokers should register with insurance commissioners, or with one or other of them, depending on the type of business which they run. The cost of registration and suspension should be met by fees-in other words, by the industry itself. The government purse would not be affected.
In proposing this Bill, Senator Evans has drawn heavily on the draft Bill attached to the report of the Law Reform Commission. The Bill contains a number of provisions aimed at further protecting the public against the risk that a broker is not truly impartial. It also required brokers to possess professional indemnity and fidelity guarantee insurance. State legislation, undoubtedly, will be ineffective. What effect will this type of legislation have on the legal framework? Let us analyse that matter. A single Supreme Court judge in Victoria,

The next stop, obviously, will be appeals to the High Court of Australia. The States may even appeal to the Privy Council. Why? Because it is a common law issue.

What is the downstream effect of this separate State law, this diverse State law, this fragmented mish-mash which this Government has inflicted and wants to persist in inflicting on this country? I say bluntly to the Treasurer that there is not a single State statute on this issue which addresses itself to the real issue; that is, to make the insurer responsible for the loss of brokers' premiums. In no State statute is that provided for. We can do that only at a national level and we ought to do it. Confusion will mount and so will the legal costs until this Government is forced to face its national responsibility and enact national legislation.

Mr SPEAKER-I wish to draw to the attention of the House the fact that the purported amendments transmitted by the Senate raise issues of ~onstitutional significance as a result of the restriCtion of paragraph 3 of section 53 of the Constitution, which provides that the Senate may not amend any proposed law so as to increase any proposed charge or burden on the people. The Senate resolved to omit clause 6 and by that

States Grants (TertiarY Education) Bill

17 November 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 2877

action purported to amend the Bill. It has been argued that the Senate does not possess the power to amend the Bill but possesses only the power to request an amendment. My task, as Speaker, is merely to alert the House to the problem. It is for the House to decide the manner in which it wishes to deal with the matter.
Mr VINER (Stirling-Minister for Industrial Relations) (5.7)-1 move:
(I) That this House considers that the effect of the purported amendments of the Senate would be to increase the burden on the people in contravention of section 53 of the Constitution and therefore declines to take the purported amendments into consideration, and
(2) That the Bill be laid aside.
In the Committee stage of the Senate debate on the States Grants (Tertiary Education Assistance) Bill the Senate resolved to remove clause 6 of the Bill. Thirty amendments consequential upon the removal of clause 6 were also agreed to by the Senate. It is the Government's view that the effect of the purported amendments of the Senate would be to increase the burden on the people in contravention of the third paragraph of section 53 of the Constitution. The fi\ird paragraph of section 53 provides as follows:
The Senate may not amend any proposed law so as to increase any proposed charge or burden on the people.
The effect of the purported amendment to remove clause 6 is to increase the minimum amount of the Commonwealtn's expenditure under the Bill and, hence, increase the amount of the appropriation as the Minister would not have the power to make the determinations that would result in a reduction of the amounts payable to the States. The Senate could, however, have requested the House of Representatives to make the amendments instead of exceeding its constitutional power in the way it has done. But a Governor General's message, pursuant to section 56 of the Constitution, would be required before this House could agree to such requests, if made. The purpose of section 56 of the Constitution is to deny to private members any right to initiate measures for the expenditure of the public revenue and to keep the initiation of such expenditure in the hands of the Government, which has the overall responsibility of relating total expenditure to total revenue. This fundamental tenet of our Constitution has been breached by the action of the Senate in relation to this Bill. Therefore, on those grounds, I commend the motion to the House.
Mr LIONEL BOWEN (Kingsford-Smith) (5.l5)-The Opposition opposes the motion on the ground that, legally, the problem is not what the Government says it is. Mr Speaker, I think you might share our view-and we do not want to

embarrass you on this point-because you said in your message on this occasion that you wanted to alert the House to the problem. I think that is very fair. Earlier, when we had another problem with the Senate which was anxious to press its requests in respect of sales tax legislation you expressed the view that it should not be entitled to press its requests. We agree with you. On that occasion, the pressing of requests would have amounted to seeking to exercise a function to which it was not entitled as it had already rejected the Bill once. But on this occasion, Mr Speaker, I think you might share our views again because you have only taken us to the point of alerting us to the problem and I do not think it is as the Government states.
If honourable members look at the Constitution, they will see views about types of laws, whether they appropriate money for annual services or whether they impose taxation. It becomes very clear that the Senate may not amend laws which appropriate money for the annual services of government. Consequently, I draw a distinction between laws that the Senate has power to deal with and those. with which it has not power to deal. The Opposition remains very firm-it has not deviated from the point-that the Senate has no right to deal with annual appropriation Bills by altering the appropriation. The Government is really drawing a long bow in this case when it says that this legislation deals with the annual appropriation. Let us make it very clear. Under clause 6 of the States Grants (Tertiary Education Assistance) Bill (No. 2)-that is the clause which has been the subject of some discussion-the Minister for Education has the ability to determine tuition fees in respect of students undertaking later degrees. But the Bill gives an appropriation in to to which is completely sufficient to furnish the requiremen~s of tertiary facilities.
There is an additional provision in the Bill that, if the Minister decides that fees have to be paid by students who undertake later degrees, the amount payable to each State will be reduced by the amount of fees collected. So this measure could not possibly increase the charge or burden on the people. It is quite ridiculous to argue, as the Government has argued quite unconvincingly, that anything that might have the effect of increasing the appropriation or, to put it more accurately, decreasing the deductions from the appropriation necessarily involves a burden on the people. That is complete nonsense! The point I make is very clear. There will be no taxation burden as seen in the terms of our Constitution. This will not be one of those issues. Of course it would suit the Government to state in a Bill: 'We

States Grants (Tertiary Education) Bill

will give you a sum of money and that is the Bill that provides it'-it has appropriated that sum of money-'but we would like to reduce it if we decide to charge fees'. The Government could not possibly say that that is increasing the burden on the people. The appropriation has been made.
Section 53 of the Constitution states:
The Senate may not amend any proposed law so as to increase any proposed charge or burden on the people.
That is the only argument on which the Government could hang its hat at the present time and this Bill does not enable it to do that. In no way does the Bill, as designed and prepared by the Government, increase the charge or burden on the people. Perhaps what it is doing is increasing the burden on the Government which has already appropriated the money in the Bill. All it is trying to do is decrease its burden to which end it has alrea-dy appropriated thefunds. The Government is saying: 'Perhaps we can unload some of that burden by making students pay extra fees'. Naturally we do ~ot in any way support this view.
The Government would get itself out of its dilemma in this matter if it supported the new constitutional alteration proposition of fixed term parliaments that the Opposition put forward in the Senate. This proposition has a clause which states that any Bill appropriating revenue or moneys or imposing taxation shall be deemed to be passed by the Senate, whether it passes it or not, 30 days after it is transmitted to it. It is appropriate that, if that is the view that the Government now has, it might get going about a constitutional amendment and support the Opposition's legislation in the Senate. Then we would be ad idem on the issue. Matters such as this cannot be the subject of some ping-pong arrangement where the matter is continually transmitted between one House and the other. We must get on with the business of Government. The Government has a mandate and has brought down a Budget. It has responsibilities, fiscal and otherwise, and the Senate should not interfere. The Government as elected by the people should run its term of office. In 1974-75, we were not given that concession but our argument has not changed. It has always been that a government is entitled to its annual appropriation. The Senate agrees with that. But this measure is not an annual appropriation.
The Senate also has power to amend Bills but it cannot do this in a fashion that would increase a charge or burden on the people. The amendment does not do that. I make it very clear that it is time that something was done about the constitutional change and a bipartisan attitude is the only way to get it. At the present time the Bill in the Senate

says what I think everyone in Australia would applaud, that is, when a government puts forward laws that appropriate revenue or revenue which imposes taxation and these laws go to the Senate that chamber can certainly transmit a message on it but that is about all. It is not to hold on to the legislation for more than one month because, after one month, it shall be deemed to have been passed. Mr Speaker, I think you would agree with that proposal. No longer would we have the spurious argument that the Government advances in this case, that is, that the Senate does not have any power to deal with this Bill. The Senate has the power to deal with it and it justifiably deals with it. It is a matter of great concern to all people in the tertiary field that they may have to pay fees. But, if honourable members look at this matter in terms of the law, it is a specious argument to suggest that this amendment in any way increases the burden on the people or increases taxation.
Mr DAWKINS (Fremantle) (5.23)-1 join this debate briefly to support the remarks that have been made by my deputy leader. What the honourable member for Kingsford-Smith (Mr Lionel Bowen) has pointed out very clearly is that the effect of the Senate's amendment does not increase the burden on the people in a way which would infringe section 53 of the Constitution. I repeat section 53, the provision in question, which states:
The Senate may not amend any proposed law so as to increase any proposed charge or burden on the people.
In its original pristine form, the Bill provided that certain grants would be made by the Commonwealth to universities, colleges and the technical and further education sector via State governments. If the Minister for Education took two separate actions, the level of those grants could be reduced by amounts determined by the Minister. The action which the Minister needed to take was that he may determine levels of fees not exceeding certain amounts and in respect of certain groups of students. That is, he was not saying that he would determine certain levels of fees. The power did not require the Minister to do that. It simply gave him a power which he could exercise if he felt like it. If the Minister did determine that fees would be collected in respect of certain students at certain levels, he could take a decision to reduce the amounts of the grants to be made to the colleges or the universities. Or, if he was of the view that certain fees should be collected, he could reduce the grants by an amount which he thought should have been collected by way of fees and which equalled that amount.
The two discretionary powers which the Bill was to give to the Minister were: Firstly, he may

States Grants (Tertiary Education) Bill

17 November 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 2879

determine fees and, secondly, if he does, he may decide to reduce the amounts of the grants to be made to individual universities and colleges. It means that, as a result of the Senate's amendment, there was no prospect that the level of the grants to be made to universities and colleges could have been increased. The effect of the amendment was not to achieve that result. The maximum level of the grants was set by the original Bill. So there was no prospect of the Government having to find more money than was already included in the Bill as presented to this House. If the power to determine fees remained in the Bill then there was some prospect that the level of grants may have been reduced. But there was no absolute guarantee that there would have been any sort of reduction because, as I have already said, it relied upon the Minister exercising two separate discretionary powers.
I think from that we can see that the Government draws a very long bow indeed when it says that because the Senate decided to remove the Minister's power to set fees-a power which he may never have exercised in any event, even though he said he would-that would increase the burden on the people. The Minister and the Government arrived at this situation in the Senate because of the botched nature of this proposal. The proposal to reintroduce fees in this form came not from the Department of Education or even from the Minister for Education. It came from the Lynch Committee reviewing Commonwealth functions. It is a hobby-horse of the Minister for Industry and Commerce (Sir Phillip Lynch) which he has entertained ever since those sad days when he was Treasurer of this country.
On one occasion, I think in 1976, he proposed that the Government would reintroduce fees in this form. He was defeated on that proposal in the Cabinet. He has waited and has bided his time. Now, when he had another opportunity to pursue this matter he included it in the report of his committee reviewing Commonwealth functions. But it was put up in such a way that the Government would simply be able to re-establish the principle of fees while at the same time ensuring that its effect was fairly limited as far as the Government was concerned.
The Minister wanted to be able to exclude from the imposition of fees all students studying for their first qualification. Indeed, that provision remains in the Bill as amended by the Senate. As well as that, the Minister wanted to require students doing certain combinations of first qualifications to be charged fees. When students were doing one or more first qualifications, either consecutively or concurrently, the Minister

wanted some of them to pay fees and some of them not to pay fees. It was a very ill-defined group to which the exclusion would obtain. Therefore, the Minister wanted to reserve for himself the power to determine which students in this group would pay fees and which would not. Again, in relation to post-graduate students and students studying other forms of higher degrees, the Minister wanted some of these students to pay fees and some of them to be excluded from the burden of fees. But again it was not possible, in the
way in which the Government presented this proposal, for the Bill to go into the detail in relation to which of these students would be paying fees and which would not. The Minister wanted to keep to himself the power to determine which of these students would pay fees and which would not.
As a result of the Minister keeping to himself this largenumber of discretionary powersfirstly, whether fees would be charged at all, secondly, to which group of students the fees would apply and, thirdly, the amount at which the fees would apply-we then get to the second problem
where, having decided to charge fees at a certain level in respect of certain students, he mayor may not have decided to reduce the grants available to universities and colleges, either by an equal amount as collected by fees, or by someother amount as he determines.
The net result of all of these discretionary powers which the Minister was given in the original Bill meant that there was an extraordinarily tenuous connection between the power of the Minister to set fees, the level of grants and the amount of money which was required to be spent by the Commonwealth in respect of universities and colleges throughout Australia. If the Government really wanted to sustain its argument that the removal of the power to determine fees would, in fact, directly lead to a reduction in those grants and, therefore, reduce the burden on the Government's coffers-as has been stated by the Deputy Leader of the Opposition-then there would have to be a very much more precise requirement in relation to the setting of fees. Indeed, there would have had to be a very much closer, tighter linkage between the collection of those fees and the reduction of the grants made available to the universities and colleges.
The Government's problem is really entirely of its own making. The proposal was ill-considered. It was not, in my view, supported by anyone who was serious about the problems confronting tertiary education in this country, particularly the problems confronting Australia's research effort. However, the Government was landed with this

2880 REPRESENTATIVES 17 November 1981

States Grants (Tertiary Education) Bill

problem as a result of the Lynch Committee report. But then the Minister wanted to ensure that it applied to the smallest number of students in such a complicated way that in the end the connection between the payment of fees and the reduction of the grants, or the increase of the burden on the people-whichever way one wants to put it-was extraordinarly tenuous indeed. Under those circumstances I do not think the Government can in any way sustain the argument which it has put to the House this afternoon.
Mr SCHOLES (Corio) (5.33)-1 also oppose the Government's motion. I do so for a number of reasons. Firstly, this is an extraordinarily loose means by which the Government is seeking to appropriate funds if, in fact, it maintains that this is an appropriation of funds. I point out that the clause in question being deleted from the States Grants (Tertiary Education Assistance) Bill in no way alters the appropriation in the Bill. It takes away from a Minister of the Crown the right to determine whether or not the appropriation will be a certain amount, or whether a part of that appropriation will be raised from a section of the people as opposed to Commonwealth revenue. I think that is a distinction which has not been made during the course of this debate.
When the Government says that it increases the burden on the people, it increases the burden on some people, if the Minister for Education (Mr Fife) applies the terms of the provisions which have been deleted from the Bill. The only increased burden which could be involved is where the Minister imposes on the States an obligation to raise certain money as an offset to moneys which are already appropriated by this Bill, which obligation is then deleted from the Commonwealth grants and, in fact, becomes an additional burden on a section of the people. This is not only a loose means but also a means which obviously has been devised by draftsmen to get around this Parliament's attempt to use its monetary functions to impose a condition on the States' operation of universities which fall within its constitutional power. The Commonwealth Parliament could not impose a condition of that sort in legislation of its own. This Parliament could not legislate to impose fees on any sections of universities as it is outside its constitutional powers. So we have a provision in this Bill where a general appropriation is made to provide the States with financial assistance for universities. A clause within the legislation provides for the conditional application of fees to be imposed under the States grants legislation in a manner which would be unconstitutional and would be opposed in separate legislation.

There can be no question that any appropriation is involved in the clause which is under consideration in this Bill. The Bill does not specify any amount. It does not specify any fees which will be charged; rather it provides the Minister, at his discretion, with an open-ended opportunity to, alter the amounts of grants which will be made to the States from this appropriation. It could well be that under this clause the Minister could impose a fee of $1 and reduce the grant to the States by $Im. These matters are not co-related. There is also no suggestion of a relief from taxation or a relief of the burden on the people. It can be argued that there is a transfer of the burden, a transfer from Consolidated Revenue and the appropriations which are made to Consolidated Revenue, to a limited number of persons on whom a fee is levied by the States in exchange for the funds which are provided for in this Bill.
This is a loose or a smart Bill-one can take one's pick of which term to use. Certainly the appropriation whereby the Parliament gives its rights over the appropriation of moneys to a Minister of the Crown is not of the type which should be made by either House of the Parliament. This Bill transfers the rights to determine that appropriation. I use an extreme case as an illustration. It would be possible to bring in an Appropriation Bill under which an amount is appropriated, as in this Bill, to cover the general appropriations required for the running of the Administration. A clause could be written into the Bill enabling the Treasurer or another Minister to vary those amounts both within the legislation and in total in accordance with the wishes of the Treasurer or that Minister. In other words, if this is, in fact, legislation which meets the criteria of the Constitution and with which the Senate is not able to deal, it is also legislation which is capable of being manipulated in such a manner as to take away from the Parliament totally the right to consider appropriations in a fixed form-a form determined by the Parliament. This is an appropriation which has a rider attached to it in the form of the clause which has been deleted. This clause enables a Minister to alter that appropriation in any way in which he so desires. He has only to meet one basic requirement. There is no provision for offsetting reductions in the appropriations in accordance with the amounts of the fees.
The Government is seeking in this legislation to create a situation where, under its policy of reducing the general level of skills and the availability of training in this community, education is restricted to those people who can afford to pay. This is a very dangerous precedent.
