STATES GRANTS (TERTIARY EDUCATION ASSISTANCE) BILL
1981 [No.2]
Bill presented by Mr Fife, and read a first time.
Second Reading
Mr FIFE (Farrer-Minister for Education) (5.51)-I move:
That the Bill be now read a second lime.
The Government has reconsidered its position in the light of the action taken by the Senate to amend the States Grants (Tertiary Education Assistance) Bill and the Australian National U niversity Bill to delete the limited tuition fees provisions, and also to reject the Canberra College of Advanced Education Bill which dealt only with fees. The Senate has disagreed with the House in two important respects. It has not been prepared to endorse the provisions of the Bill passed by the House which authorise the limited reintroduction of student fees for selected second and higher degree students, and it has also taken a contrary view to the Government as to the Senate's powers in respect of this legislation. During the debate in the Senate the Government argued that the Senate did not have the power to amend the States Grants (Tertiary Education Assistance) Bill, but it did not dispute the right of the Senate to request amendments to this legislation. The Senate, however, decided that it did have the power to make amendments and proceeded accordingly. The Government regarded the Senate's action as contravening section 53 of the Constitution and it invited the House to set aside the States Grants (Tertiary Education Assistance) Bill in the form received from the Senate. The House has done so.
The Government is now proposing a new measure which will provide for the program of grants to the States without the fees provisions to which the Senate objected. If the House approves this Bill, we will then introduce a separate Bill to amend the first Bill to restore the fees provisions. In the case of the Australian National University,

we will propose that the House accept the Bill as received from the Senate. When that has been done, we will introduce amendments to restore the fees provisions. The Government is not proposing to take any action for the present about the Canberra College of Advanced Education Bill which the Senate rejected.
The Government has had to keep in mind that the principal purpose of the legislation is to appropriate funds totalling approximately $1,725m for the universities, colleges of advanced education and tertiary and further education institutions in the States and the Northern Territory for 1982 and further appropriations for some areas for 1983 and 1984 also. Provision has also to be made for cost adjustments in respect of the program for the 1979-81 triennium. Many institutions will be short of funds by late December unless this legislation is passed in sufficient time for the appropriate instalments of approximately $300m to be made available from the Commonwealth through the State treasuries to the institutions. Their position will be made worse if further instalments of about $lOOm cannot be made in late January. The grants reflect the Government's approval of the financial recommendations put forward by the Commonwealth Tertiary Education Commission in respect of State and Northern Territory institutions and in accordance with the guidelines announced by me on 4 June 1981. On 23 September I tabled Volume 2 of the Commission's report which responded to the guidelines.
The Bill is in the form of the Bill received from the Senate except for some minor differences to overcome technical defects in the amendments adopted by the Senate to remove the new tuition fees provisions. In putting forward this Bill without the tuition fee provisions, the Government has recognised the urgent need to ensure that funds will be available to tertiary institutions in
the States to proceed with their approved programs. Some funds are yet to be allocated for 1983 and 1984 and will be included in subsequent annual amendments. However, the Government will continue to press for the limited reintroduction of tuition fees for universities and colleges of advanced education. The Government accepts that fees should not be charged to first degree or diploma students, but it believes that
students entering second diploma or degree courses or higher degree courses should pay tuition fees. These fees would be applied to a min-
ority of students and would represent a small con~ tribution to the cost of operating the institutions.
The inclusion of this tuition fees provision in a States Grants (Tertiary Education Assistance)

States Grants (Education) Bill [No.2]

17 November 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 2883

Bill as a condition to the grants for recurrent expenditure for universities and colleges of advanced education is the only mechanism open to the Commonwealth by which it can take legislative action. There is absolutely no question of the Government attempting to tack-on some extraneous matter to an appropriation Bill. The Commonwealth has the sole responsibility for financing the universities and colleges of advanced education and has the authority to make a decision about fees and to attach that as a condition to its grants. It has been the practice since the grants for the 1974 academic year for the States grants legislation for tertiary institutions to include a condition requiring the States to ensure that the universities, colleges of advanced education and TAFE colleges do not charge tuition fees. The Government has sought to continue that provision, except for second and higher degree students. I commend the Bill to the House.
Mr DAWKINS (Fremantle) (5.57)-For reaSons which 1 have already outlined, the Opposition does not oppose this legislation. I take the opportunity to note, however, that the Government has done what it should have done in the first place, that is, retreated from the extraordinary decision of the razor gang to impose these fees. As 1said before, this proposal was not new. It was defeated when Senator Carrick was Minister for Education. He clearly opposed it. The fact that is was revived on this occasion was a source of huge embarrassment to the current Minister for Education (Mr Fife). I am sure that he, of all the Ministers in this Government, would be breathing a great sigh of relief at being able to see the passage of this legislation providing for grants for universities and colleges without the requirement that fees be charged in respect of certain students.
When we were last dealing with this legislation, 1 referred to the inadequacy of the level of funding and the fact that it does not represent, as the Minister has sought to maintain, the implementation of the recommendations of the Commonwealth Tertiary Education Commission. It diverts from the recommendations in a significant regard, firstly, and particularly, in relation to the capital requirements and, secondly, in relation to the recurrent requirements insofar as the Government's election commitment, its extra new expenditure, has to be met from within recurrent expenditure close to that recommended by the Commission for continuing to maintain the current level of quality and quantity within universities and colleges. However, those points were canvassed on the earlier occasion and we are keen to see the speedy passage of this Bill not only through this House but through the Senate as well.

Third Reading

Bill (on motion by Mr Fife) read a third time.
STATES GRANTS (TERTIARY EDUCATION ASSISTANCE) ,
AMENDMENT BILL (No.2) 1981
Bill presented by Mr Fife, and read a first time.
Second Reading
Mr FIFE (Farrer-Minister for Education) (8.2)-1 move:
That the Bill be now read a second time.
The purpose of this Bill is to provide for the limited reintroduction of tuition fees in tertiary education institutions in the States and the Northern Territory. The Bill specifically excludes the charging of tuition fees for students undertaking courses leading to first degrees or diplomas. It also exempts students, who commenced second degree or diploma courses before 1982, from paying fees for those courses, and it sets a maximum fee of $1000 per annum. Subject to these limitations the Bill contains a provision for the Minister to make determinations as to the circumstances in which students should be charged tuition fees or be exempt from such charges. The Bill provides as a condition of recurrent grants that institutions charge tuition fees in accordance with these arrangements and that they do not charge tuition fees in other circumstances. The Bill provides for recurrent grants scheduled in the States Grants (Tertiary Education Assistance) Bill 1981 to be offset by an amount equivalent to the money from tuition fees which those institutions will be required to charge. I commend the Bill to the House.
Mr DAWKINS (Fremantle) (8.3)-Many of the issues which are involved in the States Grants (Tertiary Education Assistance) Amendment Bill (No.2) 1981 were canvassed when the legislation was previously before the House as a composite part of the States Grants (Tertiary Education Assistance) Amendment Bill in its first version. The Government had intended that the grants made to the States for passing on to universities and colleges would be conditional upon the requirement that the universities and colleges charged fees in respect of certain students and at certain levels as

2884 REPRESENTATIVES 17 November 1981

Tertiary Education Amendment (No.2)

the Minister determined. It is history now that the Senate jacked up on that part of the legislation. Now the Government has decided to attempt to enact those provisions separately. The Government is attempting to amend the Bill which passed this House about 30 seconds ago. If it were successful, of course, it would reinstate the original version of this Bill as it was presented earlier in this session. My guess is that when this Bill gets to the Senate it will receive the same treatment that the clauses of the earlier States grants legislation relating to this matter received a couple of weeks ago, that is the Senate will reject it. The Government will then attempt to introduce the legislation again after a period of three months. If the Senate again rejects the proposal, as I predict it will, the Government will have the first of its pieces of legislation to constitute the grounds for the calling of a double dissolution. We are not the slightest bit perplexed or daunted by that prospect. We know that the Government will not want to fight an election on this issue.
We have to go back to the last election to discover the reasons why the Government, particularly the Minister for Education (Mr Fife), ran into trouble with this proposal. The Government made certain undertakings prior to the last election in relation to tertiary tuition fees. When we consider the power of the Senate in respect of what governments want to achieve and what they put to the people at the time of elections we have to accept-I put this very forcefully-that the Senate must use its power very carefully. We in the Australian Labor Party would go much further and say that the power of the Senate in respect of a number of issues ought to be substantially truncated. To the extent that the Senate has the power and retains it, it must, in our view, use that power judiciously. Firstly, if a government puts a proposal to the electorate prior to an election I think there is an obligation on the part of the Senate, unless extraordinary circumstances intervene, not to frustrate what the government has a mandate to implement. If the government has said before the election that it intends to do things then legislates to carry out those undertakings I believe the Senate ought to pass those proposals.
There is a second category of measures, those for which the government has no mandate and which it did not mention prior to the election. In those circumstances the Senate is entitled to consider the matter on its merits. Then there is a third group of measures, those for which the government has absolutely no mandate on the basis that it undertook, prior to the election, not to implement them. It is a sort of reverse mandate. The

government may have promised not to do something and may subsequently attempt to do it after the election. It is a sort of electoral fraud. In those circumstances the Sentate is duty bound in my view to keep the government to its undertakings and force it to abandon any proposal which absolutely and directly contradicts what it said prior to the election. I refer to what the Minister said on 8 October 1980 in an article produced in the Australian newspaper. I will read one paragraph. In a prepared statement the Minister said:
There continues to be speculation about Government plans to re-introduce fees for tertiary education. I have given an assurance publicly and I repeat that assurance, that no proposals are being considered by the Government for the possible reintroduction of tertiary fees.
That is a specific undertaking. The Minister and certain other members of the Government have subsequently sought to quibble with the fine print of that paragraph. They say it did not mean that the Government would never reintroduce fees but that it did not have anything under consideration on the day the statement was made. That quibbling has to be disregarded because there is no doubt at all what meaning the Minister intended to convey in that statement. The Minister was trying to dampen and quash speculation about Government plans. That is what the first sentence referred to. The Minister stated:
There continues to be speCUlation about Government plans to reintroduce fees for tertiary education.
The Minister wanted to kill that speculation and to leave in everybody's mind the impression that the Government had no intention of reintroducing tertiary education fees. Yet it took just a few months for the Government to abrogate that promise via a decision of the Lynch Committee, the razor gang, which recommended-the Government accepted the recommendation-the partial reintroduction of tertiary tuition fees. So quite clearly the Government went to the people before the election and said that it would not do it and then a few months later said that it was going to do it. Under those circumstances the Senate not only is empowered but also has a duty to keep the Government honest in respect of those sorts of undertakings which it gives prior to an election. I repeat that that is the main reason why the Government has run into trouble on this measure. However, the proposal itself can be opposed on its merits regardless of whether there was this problem of the breaching of an election undertaking.
In the first instance, the proposal intends to reintroduce fees for post-graduate students, higher degree students and second degree students; that is certain categories of students who are not exempt. This proposal, if enacted, would strike

Tertiary Education Amendment (No.2)

17 November 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 2885

quite directly at the heart of the research effort carried on in universities and colleges around Australia, but more particularly in universities. That research effort has already been greatly affected by the reduction in funds over a period of years in relation to general research funding which has been made available by this Government through a variety of sources. It has to be acknowledged that post-graduate students are central to the research effort of universities. They are the ones who are responsible for very much of the research effort. Even if the research itself is directed by full time members of the university community, the post-graduate students are fundamentally important to the carrying out of that research.
As well as that, this proposal represented a quite specific penalty directed at anyone who had the nerve or gall to undertake any sort of postgraduate education to get a second degree. Those classes of student were selected for this specific penalty of up to $1,000 a year. The quaint thing about it was that those people who had some measure, inadequate as it is, of public, university or college support by way of post-graduate awards were not to be charged the fees, and those who had no public support were to be specifically penalised in this way. It really is impossible to justify that sort of approach and to impose that sort of penalty on that quite specific, quite limited group of students. There is no question at all that it would have had a severe impact on the level of enrolments next year in those courses to which the fees apply.
It has been impossible to obtain any idea, in direct terms, of what the effect on enrolments might have been quite simply because the universities, at least in relation to post-graduate students, are the ones more particularly involved and their enrolment figures are not available until early next year. In any event, one suspects that because of the impending imposition of these fees a lot of people would have enrolled late this year in courses which they intended to undertake next year in order to escape the imposition of the fees because, as the Government made it clear, there was no intention to apply the fees to those people who were already enrolled. So it may have been in this first year that the effect of enrolments would not have been all that severe for that reason-that is for next year-but in succeeding years there is no question that in respect of those who were required to pay the fees it would have been quite substantial.
The point that we particularly took exception to was that the Government was quite clearly trying to re-establish the principle of fees. It always

has been keen. There is a very active and noisy group on the back bench in this House and in the Senate which is desperately keen to see university fees and college fees reintroduced for the undergraduate level as well as the post-graduate level. The numbers are variously estimated at anything from 20 to 30, which makes the group a quite sizable and influential minority within the Government ranks. This measure, partial as it was, was a sort of a sop to those people to keep them happy until such time as the Government could extend, when political circumstances might have been more favourable, to include undergraduate students as vvell.
The Minister can say for as long as he likes that there was no intention to reintroduce fees for undergraduate students, but the Minister unfortunately has to live with the fact that his word after the last election is no longer reliable and nobody believes a word he says. He broke a promise made at the last election and no one will believe him ever again. I suspect that that is why he will not be in this portfolio for very much longer. He will be off to other pastures where he can make new commitments and break those commitments subsequently.
The members of the noisy group on the Government back benches have constructed very elaborate arguments about the importance of the user-payer principle in relation to higher education. They argue that the benefits from higher education accrue to the community and to the individual and, therefore, there should be a contribution from both in respect of the cost of providing that education. At the same time they go to great and windy lengths to indicate there is a great bias in terms of the socio-economic composition of the university population. They point to the fact that the university and college populations consist of the sons and daughters who substantially come from the wealthier part of the community. They seem to ignore the fact that the im" position of fees will do absolutely nothing to improve that situation, but rather will exacerbate the problem to make universities and colleges more and more the preserve of the sons and daughters of the rich. They have made no attempt to demonstrate how the imposition of fees will create one extra place for somebody not already enrolled in a college or university.
The second point which they seem to assume erroneously is that Australia already has enough enrolments in the higher education area. We heard an even less elegant version of this argument from Senator Harradine a few nights ago.

2886 REPRESENTATIVES 17 November 1981

Tertiary Education Amendment (No.2)

Senator Harradine in the end supported the Australian Labor Party and the Australian Democrats in knocking out this fee proposal, but he spent a lot of time during that debate frothing at the mouth about how this had something to do with the fact that it made it easier and easier for a whole lot of bludgers to clog up the universities to the exclusion of kids from middle and lower income families. Somehow these people who were sitting around, in his words, doing their fourth and fifth degrees were denying places to kids from families headed by service station proprietors and the like. He went on at great length.
In the first case Senator Harradine, and I suspect others, labour under a huge delusion. It is no picnic hanging around a university with no public support. It has to be recognised that anybody doing a second qualification gets no Tertiary Education Assistance Scheme allowance, no scholarship and no public support at all. People are not entitled to social security benefits if they are involved in full time education. So anybody who is involved in doing a second qualification
either has to have substantial private means or must be extraordinarily determined to undertake that course at a huge private cost. Whilst students may not have to pay fees, they certainly have to support themselves during the undertaking of that course. As well as that, this whole argument, the whole user-pays argument, the whole reference to the bias in terms of the university population, ignores certain important facts; that is, if we are concerned about access to tertiary education we should ensure that everybody who qualifies for tertiary entrance should be allowed to proceed regardless of their economic circumstances. It was that problem which exercised the mind of my predecessor in Fremantle, the Labor Minister for
Education, Mr Beazley. He talked about bringing in a revolution of access through the introduction of the Tertiary Education Assistance Scheme. He understood the problem. He understood that the statistics were very unfavourable. Even today something like 18 per cent of the university and college population come from 5 per cent of the most wealthy families in the country, while another 18 per cent come from the poorest 50 per cent of the families in this country. He set about trying to correct that distortion, and quite rightly so. What he did was to try to improve the financial support which was available to those people who had qualified but who, for economic reasons, under the old arrangements were not able to undertake a tertiary education. What honourable members on the other side of the House fail to address themselves to is the question of how the imposition of fees would do anything but exacerbate

the sort of distortion which we set out to try to correct.
This also raises the question of the overall participation rates in higher eduction. What we have seen is a shift, a reduction, in the full time enrolments in colleges and universities over the last few years. There has been a fall of about 20 per cent in the number of kids who are going straight from school to full time education in universities and colleges. That is a matter of great concern to us and it should be a matter of great concern to the Government as well. In 1974, about 18 per cent of kids who finished their final year of secondary school went straight on to university or to college but now that number is down to about 14.5 per cent. That is a substantial fall in just five or six years and it is something which we ought to be concerned about. In that regard I quote a few lines from the most recent report of the Tertiary Education Commission. The Commission, in referring to the fact that one of the reasons which the Commission identifies as the cause of this problem is the inadequacy of student financial assistance, says:
In the context of changes to the Tertiary Education Assistance Scheme (TEAS) and the introduction of a loans scheme, the Commission believes that it should draw the matter again to the attention of the Government. Both the increasing pace of technological change and the fall in the number of full-time jobs for teenagers make it important that able young men and women avail themselves of educational or training opportunities. A decline of one-fifth in the participation of young people in higher education is surely a matter for community concern. It runs contrary to Government policies relating to technological change and to transition from school to adult life. It raises questions about Australia's long-term capacity to meet the challenges ahead of it.
When we couple those statements with the fact that more and more kids are dropping out of school earlier and earlier and therefore not even getting to the starting gate in terms of entrance to tertiary education, we really see the magnitude of the problem beginning to unfold before us. That is why we are so adamant that the question of fees should not proceed, that it is a matter which ought to be rejected on principle. We are deeply concerned about access to tertiary education. We are deeply concerned about improving participation rates in tertiary education. We are concerned about seeing kids from poorer families staying at school longer so that they get a chance to get to universities and colleges and that when they get to the starting gate they have sufficient financial support to ensure that they actually proceed with that education. Mr Deputy Speaker, I have mentioned to the Minister that I want to incorporate in Hansard a small section from the Tertiary Education Commission report. I now formally seek leave to have it incorporated.

PARTICIPATION IN HIGHER EDUCATION
1.10 Table 1.3 sets out student numbers in higher education from 1974 to 1981. Total enrolments have continued to grow over the whole period, although the rate of growth has slowed in the last few years. There has, however, been a major shift from full-time to part-time and external study. Not only have full-time enrolments declined as a proportion of total enrolments (from 63.5 per cent in 1974 to 53.2 per cent in 1981), but over the past four years they have declined in absolute terms: there are now nearly 12,000fewer full-time enrolments than there were in 1977.

1.11 The decline in full-time enrolments has been associated with a reduction in the participation of young people in higher education and a substantial increase in the enrolment of older people, particularly as part-time or external students. The decline in the participation of the young is clearly illustrated in Table 1.4, which gives numbers of students, by sex, commencing higher education direct from school, and the proportion they bear to the 17 year old population. In absolute terms, there has been a decline of nearly 4,000 between 1977 and 1981. As a proportion of the agegroup, the numbers declined steadily from 1974 to 1980; for 1981 they are about the same as for 1980, that is, about one-fifth less than they were in 1974. If the proportion has been maintained at the 1974 level, in 1981 there would have been about 8,500 more young people enrolling at universities and CAEs direct from school. The decline has occurred for both males and females, although it has been greater, absolutely and relatively, for males.

1.12 In Volume 1 (Part 1, paragraph 9.IS) the Commission indicated its concern with the decline in the participation of the young in higher education. In the context of changes to the Tertiary Education Assistance Scheme (TEAS) and the introduction of a loans scheme, the Commission believes that it should draw the matter again to the attention of the Government. Both the increasing pace of technological change and the fall in the number of full-time jobs for teenagers make it important that able young men and women avail themselves of educational or training opportunities. A decline of one-fifth in the participation of young people in higher education is surely a matter for community concern. It runs contrary to Government policies rdating to technological change and to transition from school to adult life. It raises questions about Australia's long-term capacity to meet the challenges ahead of it.
1.13 As pointed out in Volume I (Part I, paragraphs 2.30 and 2.31), the decline in the participation of young people in higher education appears to be related partly to their assessment of the benefits of higher education and partly to the

availability of financial support for full-time study. The latter is a matter for Commonwealth Government policy. Since 1975 a major source of support for undergraduates, namely the teacher training scholarships awarded by State education departments, has been virtually eliminated. Moreover, the real value of grants under TEAS has been eroded by inflation and the real severity of the means test increased. The.reversal of the downwards trend in the participation of the young will, at the least, require improved financial assistance to students. One possible way of achieving this is through a loans scheme. The Government has announced its intention to introduce such a scheme; this may increase access to higher education, although its impact will depend on the availability of credit, the conditions under which loans are made, and the arrangements for repayment.
1.14 In Volume I (Part I, paragraph 7.IS) the Commission supported the charging of fees for n-on-award courses; the Commission, therefore, does not argue against the charging of fees for courses of a continuing education nature. However, the Commission is concerned that the Government's decision

2888 REPRESENTATIYES 17 November 1981

Tertiary Education Amendment (No, 2)

that tuition fees should be charged for second and higher de-
grees runs counter to the Commission's support for increased
emphasis on enrolments for higher degrees in universities (Volume I, Part I, paragraph 9.16). Existing financial assistance for higher degree students is now seen by many to be in-
adequate. The situation could be improved if revenue from fees were used to increase support for higher degree students.

Mr FIFE (Farrer-Minister for Education) (8.36)-1 move:
That the amendments be agreed to.
The Government is prepared to accept the Bill as amended, It contains provisions to clarify the distinction between undergraduate and postgraduate students in the election of student representatives to the University Council and to enable the University to recognise certain persons who have undertaken studies in other institutions. These amendments were requested by the university and the Government wishes to see them enacted without further delay. However, 1 foreshadow again that following the completion of the House's consideration of this Bill, I will introduce a further Bill to provide for the limited reintroduction of tuition fees at the Australian National University, similar to what has been proposed for universities in the States.
Mr DAWKINS (Fremantle) (8.37)-The amendments which the Government is accepting are essentially those amendments which were moved by the Opposition in the Senate to the Australian National University Amendment Bill. For that reason we do not oppose the amendments.

AUSTRALIAN NATIONAL UNIVERSITY AMENDMENT BILL (No.3) 1981
Bill presented by Mr Fife, and read a first time.
Second Reading
Mr FIFE (Farrer-Minister for Education) (8.40)-1 move:
That the Bill be now read a second time.
The purpose of this Bill is to provide for the limited re-introduction of tuition fees at the Australian National University. The principles involved in this Bill are the same as those in the States Grants (Tertiary Education Assistance) Amendment Bill (No.2) 1981. 1 commend the Bill to the House.
Mr DAWKINS (Fremantle) (8.41)-We are all struck with a quaint sense of deja vu because the House has just rejected these propositions and and they are now being represented to us. We are now being asked to agree to proposals which the Government has just acknowledged should not be enacted In this legislation. It is a charade which the Government wants to go through because it recognises that it cannot get through the Senate i1 proposal for which it has no mandate and a proposal which it specifically promised it would not attempt to enact. This proposal relates to the Australian National University which, unlike all other universities in this country, is a legislative responsibility of this Parliament, set up by this Parliament and therefore its statutes can be amended by this Parliament along the lines of this sort of legislation. Of course, for reasons which J went into when a similar Bill was before this House and for reasons which were elaborated on in the Senate by an Opposition spokesman, we will oppose the Australian National University Amendment Bill (No.3) 1981.
Mr BEAZLEY (Swan) (8.42)-1 take advantage of this occasion to raise a couple of arguments that 1 would have.raised on the previolls Bill-the States Grants (Tertiary Education Assistance) Amendment Bill had 1 had an opportunity, but as this Bill contains the same principle that opportunity is presented to me now. 1 am sorry that these measures are being dragged through the House in the way in which they are because they are being made the basis, in announcements by the Prime Minister (Mr Malcolm F~aser), of the possibility of a double dissolution, 1 would have thought that, before the country was put through that trauma, some sort of explanation might be offered as to why it should be. No explanation has been offered here to this point. 1 am concerned that, in the general

2890 REPRESENTATIVES 17 November 1981

National University Bill (No.3)

community, there may be an impression that what has been happening to this Government in the Senate on the Australian National University Amendment Bill (No.3) (1981) and other Bills is similar to what happened to the Australian Labor Party when it was in office between 1972 and 1975. The circumstances are, of course, completely different.
In 1972-75 we had a government in office which sought to enact a program which it had obtained a mandate from the people to enact. Approximately 80 Bills were either rejected or severely amended during that period by the Liberal National Country Party majority in the Senate. On this occasion we have a piece of legislation for which no mandate has been sought. Indeed a reverse mandate, if I can put it that way, has been
given because the Government gave undertakings not to introduce fees. Nevertheless it has proceeded to do so. It is a very sad reflection, I think, on the quality of the relationship between this Government and the electorate that it should have seen fit to do that.
I make one or two other points which relate to the Australian National University. I can make them more directly on the ANU because it has the particular courses to which I wish to refer. I think that the Government, having decided that it wishes to proceed to the point where a double dissolution is threatened on this matter, has to present an argument to the Australian people that this is a weighty and sufficient issue to go to that expense and trauma. A reasoned analysis of this proposition suggests that this is nothing of the sort. In the first place this Bill in particular and the other one passed today raises negligible revenue. At the very best it would not be 40 per cent of one day's expenditure on education in Australia and it would probably raise much less. In the second place, it imposes a regressive burden on poor students. Pious statements have been made that what we have to do with this legislation is make people pay for the education that they are receiving but of course some people will pay and others will not.
For example, consider the case of a business student at the ANU who is doing a Master of Business Administration. That business student will now be obliged to pay, if this legislation goes through, $1,000 a year in fees. Let us assume that that business student, as is most likely, is a government employee or a businessman. That person will be able to relate that fee to efforts made to expand his earning capacity related directly to his job. The Taxation Commissioner will permit a tax deduction. The MBA student will therefore be able to claim, if he is on an income of $25,000 or

$30,000 a year, at least $500. Therefore, $500 will be all that that student pays. The same situation
applies to a lawyer who is seeking to do a Master of Laws degree. Such a student if he is a lawyer on
a similar sort of income will be able to make pre-
cisely that sort of claim. However, this will not apply to a person who seeks to move on in
qualifications from a Bachelor of Arts degree to a
Master of Arts degree if that person has been funding his own education through university on
the basis of a part time role in the work force and
a part time role in the university. Very consider-
able damage will be suffered by such a person be-
cause a tax deduction will not be open to him and
he will be living on pittances and will be obliged to pay an extra $1,000.

The Government has not looked into the ques-
tion of tax deductibility of these fees and the im-
pact of that on the revenue that it will raise from it. I know that the Government has not done this
because I asked a question in the appropriate esti-
mates committee as to whether it had. I was told
that the Government had not considered that matter. A very important element of equity in this
proposal-this most inequitable proposal-has not even been considered by the Government and
that I think is a scandalous situation. The prop-
osition hits at the very heart of the voluntary reseach effort in the Australian community, in
this legislation the voluntary research effort in
Canberra. Let there be' no doubt whatsoever
about that.

Whether a person has to pay for his education
has an impact on whether he or she will go to university. There is precious little research published
on this matter by the Government. One would have thought that it would have made some effort to do something, given that it was introducing this
legislation. However in its recent publication,
'Studies of Tertiary Student Finances', something does come out. Several pieces of research, which
have been done at Government expense and pri-
vately, have revealed substantial numbers of students who are dropping out of tertiary edu-
cation for financial reasons. One of the principle
categories, this report states, is:

Students of mature age, possibly married, highly motivated

but finding it difficult to meet their long-term financial

commitments.

'

Unquestionably, those sorts of students will find
themselves even more damaged. A student, who enters university or a College of Advanced Education on the basis of a mature judgment, will be the student who will find himself or herself disadvantaged and excluded from the education process.

This report concludes with the statement:

Social Sevices Amendment Bill (No.3)

17 November 1981 REPRESENT ATIVES 2891

In summary this section has presented a review of recent literature and research (primarily Australian) related to the importance of financial factors as an influence on student progress in tertiary education. The studies demonstrate that financial factors are of some importance to progress in tertiary education. But there is clearly a need for more research in order to investigate the types of students whose progress is most likely to be affected by financial factors, and the ways in which insufficient finance contributes to the decision of these students to withdraw from tertiary education.
Throughout this report there is no assumption that students withdraw on the basis of inability. They withdraw on the basis of inadequate finance. This Government really ought to have done some work on that matter before it introduced this legislation.

SOCIAL SERVICES AMENDMENT BILL (No.3) 1981
Bill presented by Mr Hunt, and read a first time.
Second Reading
Mr HUNT (Gwydir-Minister for Transport) (8.52)-1 move:
That the Bill be now read a second time.
Honourable members will be aware that clauses precluding spouses of persons involved in industrial action from receiving unemployment and special benefit were deleted by the Government from the Social Services Amendment Bill 1981 during its passage through the Parliament. This was done pursuant to a request of the Senate, not

because the Government agreed with the request, but to prevent delay in the passage of the remainder of that Bill which, inter alia, authorised the payment of additional unemployment benefit to single beneficiaries aged 18 years and over. In deleting those clauses, the Government announced that fresh legislation containing those provisions would be introduced at the first available opportunity. This Bill introduces those provisions.
Before October 1979, the Social Services Act precluded payment of unemployment benefit to a person who was a direct participant in a strike. However, it enabled unemployment benefit to be paid at the 'married' rate to the spouse of a person ineligible for unemployment benefit on those grounds. The Act was subsequently amended to provide that unemployment benefit payable to the spouse of a person engaged in industrial action was to be at the 'single' rate. A further amendment precluded payment of special benefit to a person who, because of industrial action, was not qualified to receive unemployment benefit.
This Bill now proposes that, for so long as a person's spouse is disqualified from receiving unemployment benefit because of involvement in industrial action; or unwillingness to undertake suitable work; or failure to take reasonable steps to obtai'n such work, that person will also be ineligible for unemployment benefit. Additionally, persons will be ineligible for unemployment benefit for so long as payment of that benefit to their spouses is postponed because of their misconduct as a worker, because they have become voluntarily unemployed or because they have failed to accept an offer of suitable employment.
Consistent with this approach, special benefit will also be withheld from persons not eligible for unemployment benefit in these circumstances. The Government has decided, however, that if a person is precluded from receiving unemployment benefit and special benefit, in a case of extreme hardship and where there are no other means of support, his or her spouse may be eligible for special benefit at the single rate, with additional benefit for any children.
We are not here concerned to discuss the merits of strike action as contrasted with other means open to employees in dispute with their employers to resolve these disputes. What we are concerned with is how the cost of strike action is to be borne. The Government holds strongly to the belief that these provisions should become law. In the case of the provisions relating to industrial action, we do not believe that Australian taxpayers should be

2892 REPRESENTATIVES 17 November 1981

Social Sevices Amendment Bill (No.3)

asked to subsidise strike action through the social welfare system.
Trade unions choosing to take such action make a free decision to strike. If they choose to take such action, they should bear the consequences of their action, not the tax-paying community. Trade unions cannot expect, in those circumstances, that the rest of the community will pick up the bill for the support of the families of members of the trade union involved in the strike. The legislation as it stands at the moment permits a situation where trade unions can choose to strike, knowing that through the operation of the social security system their members can be largely insulated from the financial effects of their actions.
Unless frivolously or maliciously entered into, the object of a strike is to obtain, by the exercise of economic pressure directed against the employer, a benefit for those who take such action. As a result, the benefit sought is itself economic, such as increased pay, a shorter working week, or special allowances. Not infrequently, strikes are settled on a basis that effectively compensates those who have gone on strike for pay lost during the duration of the strike. The point to be emphasised is that to strike is an exercise in freedom of choice. It is one taken by the membership of a union to promote its own interests and only its own interests. It is taken against the interests of the employer and, what is directly relevant to this Bill, it is one which is not taken in the interests of the community as a whole.
Indeed, the effect of strike action is often in conflict with the interests of the community as a whole or some part of it. Frequently, the community suffers as a result of strike action. This may be reflected simply in inconvenience or in terms of direct economic cost. Furthermore, in a larger sense, it may be said that in the longer term the community always suffers as the cumulative result of the strike action is to decrease the availability of goods and services, and sometimes to do great damage to particular sectors of the economy, or to particular employers. These matters need only to be mentioned to make clear the absurdity of any argument that benefits should be paid to strikers or their families, and should be paid by the community at large. For it is the community at large that provides through taxation the funds from which strikers are presently paid the benefits that this Bill proposes to eliminate.
There have been numerous cases where the wives of strikers, who have appeared to have no genuine intention of joining the work force themselves, have registered for work in places such as

mining and construction towns where there is no real possibility of employment for them. This has enabled them, under the existing legislation, to obtain unemployment benefit, and so transfer to the taxpayer some of the real cost of the industrial action taken by their husbands. So, the Australian pUblic'is attacked from two directions. First, it has to bear the personal disruptions, inconveniences and associated costs that result from the strike. In addition, it is then called upon to meet the cost of providing income support for the families of those involved in the strike.
To act to correct this situation is not to 'punish' the families of strikers but to ensure that people accept the financial consequences of their own actions and do not look to the rest of the community to bear the costs. Similarly, there is no good reason why the community should automatically and immediately be expected to assume the normal financial responsibilities of people who are not willing to work, who voluntarily and without good reason give up their job or who are dismissed through their own misconduct. This is happening at present because the spouses of such people can register for employment in their own right and, if they are unable to obtain employment, can claim unemployment benefit both for themselves and their 'dependent' spouses. We believe that whether people refuse to work because of industrial action or whether, in some other way, they show themselves unwilling to enter or remain in the work force, there is no reason why the rest of the community should shoulder their financial responsibilities. This Bill prevents them from manipulating the social security system to shift the consequences of their actions. I commend the Bill to the House.
Debate (on motion by Dr Blewett) adjourned.
SOCIAL SERVICES AMENDMENT BILL (No.2) 1981
Bill-by leave-presented by Mr Hunt, and read a first time.
Second Reading
Mr HUNT (Gwydir-Minister for Transport) (9.2)-1 move:
That the Bill be now read a second time.
Honourable members will be aware that the Social Services Amendment Act 1981 extended the scope of the double orphans' pensions provisions in the Social Services Act 1947 to enable those pensions to be paid to persons having the custody, care and the control of refugee children who are admitted to Australia where the parents of those children are outside Australia, or the

Air Traffic Services Centre, Perth

17 November 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 2893

whereabouts of the parents of those children are unknown.
A technical, but important, defect has been found in the definition of 'refugee child' in the legislation and this Bill will amend that definition to cover all children the Government considers should fall within the extent provisi011S. The existing legislation defines 'refugee child' in terms of a child granted refugee status by the Australian Government. There are, however, children who are admitted into Australia as refugees who do not have the formal status of refugee granted to them. The Government intends these children to fall within the class of children in respect of whom double orphans' pensions are payable. In addition, there are children admitted into Australia otherwise than as refugees, under special humanitarian programs, whom the Government considers should be regarded as within the class of children in respect of whom double orphans' pensions should be payable. I am sure that honourable members will share the Government's concern that the double orphans' pension provisions be extended in respect of those groups of children who came to Australia whose parents are outside Australia or the whereabouts of whose parents are unknown. I commend the Bill to the House.
Debate (on motion by Dr Blewett) adjourned.
NEW AIR TRAFFIC SERVICES CENTRE AND CONTROL TOWER, PERTH AIRPORT
Approval of Work: Public Works Committee Act
Mr McVEIGH (Darling Downs-Minister for Housing and Construction) (9.4)-1 move:
That, in accordance with the provisions of the Public Works Committee Act 1969, it is expedient to carry out the following elements of the work known as the construction of new air traffic services centre and control tower, Perth Airport, Western Australia, which was referred to the Parliamentary Standing Committee on Public Works and on which the Committee has duly reported to Parliament: Air traffic services operations building, technical maintenance centre, radio equipment building and services building.
The Parliamentary Standing Committee on Public Works has recommended the construction of these elements which are estimated to cost $2.7m. The Committee recommends also that a start on the construction of the control tower element estimated to cost a further $O.9m be delayed pending completion of a master plan for the Perth Airport. The Government notes the Committee's intention to report further on this element and its siting in the light of the completed master plan. These conclusions are acceptable to the Government and a start on the control tower will accordingly be deferred although detailed planning of

the tower to minimise potential delays will continue in accordance with the Committee's recommendation.
The Committee has further recommended that discussions with the Western Australian Government should be re-opened with a view to setting up the Western Australian Airfields Committee within six months. The Commonwealth will move to re-open these discussions as recommended. Finally the Committee recommends that the Department of Transport should re-examine the public parking at the airport to ensure it is compatible with peak loads. This recommendation also will be implemented. If the House agrees to support this motion planning can proceed in accordance with the recommendations of the Committee.
Mr BEAZLEY (Swan) (9.6)-1 have sought to intervene in this debate very briefly as the representative for the area in which the work is to be done. As I understand it the Minister for Housing and Construction (Mr McVeigh) seeks approval to proceed only with the work approved by the Parliamentary Standing Committee on Public Works and not that which the Committee recommended be held over until a master plan for the development of Perth Airport is produced. 1 think the Minister's statement is to be welcomed on that basis. I welcome also his statement that the Commonwealth Government intends to pursue the establishment of the Western Australian Airfields Committee. I understand that the Commonwealth Government and the Department of Transport have not been the problem in establishing that Committee; it has been the State Government that has resisted its establishment. No blame is to be attached to the Commonwealth Government for its not being created hitherto.
The Public Works Committee's recommendation followed substantial input and appearances before it by local authorities in my area and a number of local members of parliament including myself. 1 think an explanation is appropriate here as to why we sought delays in the construction of a new control tower-delays which the Minister has accepted. In the first place there is substantial concern in my constituency that no major construction which might pre-empt some options on future airport developments should be undertaken prior to a full consideration of those options in a master plan for the future development of the airport. This master plan was promised to the local authorities in my area as a result of recommendations published in 1979 by a joint committee consisting of representatives of Commonwealth, State and local governments in

2894 REPRESENTATIVES 17 November 1981

Air Traffic Services Centre, Perth

the area. It has yet to emerge though it is apparently being rushed through the process now.
The control tower related to these considerations because that which has been proposed by the Department of Transport and the Department of Housing and Construction would be unsuitable for one of the options under consideration in a master plan. I refer to the construction of a wide-spaced runway parallel to the present north-south runway. For this latter development to proceed an additional control tower to the one currently under consideration would have to be constructed at considerable expense to the Commonwealth. The local authorities in my area, particularly the Belmont Shire Council and the Canning City Council have put forward excellently argued propositions pointing out the very substantial number of additional houses that would be affected by airport-related noise and environmental pollution if the parallel runway option is not to proceed. It is very important that we keep faith with those authorities as they have, by associating themselves with the 1979 Joint Committee report, taken the unusual step of moving beyond the expected stolid opposition to the presence of the airport in their area to accepting it under certain conditions jointly agreed with the Commonwealth. There are not too many local authorities around Australia that, given the opportunity to argue a case for the removal of an airport, would actually come to those conclusions. That makes keeping faith with them very important.
One of the related considerations which must affect the timing of when the control tower should proceed concerns the question of compensation. Given that the control tower as proposed obviates any immediate development of a north-south parallel runway, another promise of the Joint Committee comes to mind; that is, the Joint Committee argued that, if a properly considered master plan recommended on economic grounds that the parallel runway should not proceed, the residents should be paid compensation to insulate their homes. The Department of Transport has written to the councils asking them to research the environmental impact of the usage of the airport in their area, I hope that this is not a blind but a prelude to legislation for compensation. I hope also that we will not see the master plan rushed through in an ad hoc fashion to accommodate early construction of the tower and later the new international terminal. The Minister's acceptance of the Public Works Committee's recommendations would be made a mockery of were that the case.

As I said, I welcome the Minister's acceptance of the recommendations. I welcome the other announcements made by the Minister and the fact that the Government is now consulting with the local authorities on the question of compensation. I hope that we will jointly achieve a development that is satisfactory to all the parties concernedthe users and neighbours of the airport.
Mr McVEIGH (Darling Downs-Minister for Housing and Construction) (9.11)-in reply-I am grateful for those comments. They appeared to be very practical ones. I will draw them to the attention of the Minister for Transport (Mf Hunt) for use on the appropriate occasion. I think it is always good when local members show an intelligent interest in what is going on in their electorates and bring matters of concern to the local people to the attention of the appropriate authorities.

REDEVELOPMENT OF BRISBANE INTERNATIONAL AIRPORT
Approyal of Work: Public Works Committee Act
Mr McVEIGH (Darling Downs-Minister for Housing and Construction) (9.12)-1 move:
That, in accordance with the provisions of the Public Works Commillee Act 1969, it is expedient to carry out the following proposed work which was referred to the Parliamentary Standing Committee on Public Works and on which the Committee has duly reported to Parliament, namely: Redevelopment of Brisbane International Airport-Further dredging and reclamation.
The proposal is for the execution of further sand dredging and site reclamation associated with the balance of phase one works, including the provision of a cross runway and general aviation areas. In reporting favourably on the proposal the Committee has made certain other recommendations and comments. The Committee indicated that it holds serious doubts as to the quality of the planning co-ordination between the Department of Transport technical divisions involved and was critical of the failure by the Department of Transport during 1979 to perceive the evident major disadvantages in the simultaneous operation of the two airports at Brisbane. Although there is no doubt that the two airports could be operated together, it was only during the design development stage during 1980 that some earlier assumptions regarding operating and equipment costs were found to be too low and the full costs of such an operation were realised. The Department of Transport moved immediately to rectify the situation and proposed to the Government in June 1980 that a cross wind runway should be provided and the existing airport closed when the redeveloped airport is commissioned.

Brisbane International Airport

17 November 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 2895

The Committee also concluded that a completion date of 1986 rather than 1990 for the full phase one works has not yet been clearly justified and went on to state that evidence to be put before the Committee on the remainder of phase one must provide such a justification. The Government commitment is to a 1986 commissioning date for the redeveloped airport. The Committee noted in its report that aircraft noise disturbance will be removed from 3,500 houses at that time. The Government is keen to take advantage of this noise reduction at the earliest opportunity in the interests of the people living in the noise affected area and this is one of the many benefits to be derived from the 1986 target commissioning date.
While the Committee has noted that it was satisfied with the broad master plan concept, it expressed concern that the public information program did not provide information on the master plan as put before the Committee. The public information program referred to was directed at the provision of the cross runway and hence did not cover the aspect of the full airport master plan layout. The basic airport master plan was first shown to the public in the media in 1971 and has been demonstrated on many occasions since. The Department of Housing and Construction and the Department of Transport are about to open a public information centre on the airport site and I can assure the House that the airport master plan forms part of the display in that centre.
The Committee suggests that the Department of Housing and Construction monitor the effects of dredging on the environment and on fishing and prawning. Such monitoring is being carried out and will continue. The estimated cost of the proposed work examined by the Committee was $16m at February 1981 prictls. The Committee recommended construction of the work in the reference. If the House agrees to support this motion, detailed planning can proceed in accordance with the recommendations of the Committee.
Question resolved in the affirmative.

STATES GRANTS (CAPITAL ASSISTANCE) BILL 1981
Second Reading
Debate resumed from 27 October, on motion by Mr Howard:
That the Bill be now read a second time.
Mr WILLIS (GelIibrand) (9.25)-The States Grants (Capital Assistance) Bill now before the House authorises the payment of capital grants to the States for the current financial year and for the first half of the year 1982-83. The first six months of next financial year are included to allow time for the Parliament to pass similar legislation next year. The capital grant which the Commonwealth has to pay to the States for this financial year as a result of this Bill is $436m, which is the same amount as last year despite an estimated inflation rate of 10.75 per cent for this financial year and which is almost the same money amount as the Commonwealth paid to the States in 1975-76 despite the fact that there has been rapid inflation throughout the period since then. In fact the real value of this grant has fallen by nearly half since 1975-76, that is, the grant the Commonwealth is proposing to make this year will buy only about half as much this year as it would have in 1975-76. Is it any wonder, therefore, that the States are protesting? However, I will come back to their reaction in a few minutes after discussing the Commonwealth's payment to the States more fully.

The second reading speech of the Treasurer (Mr Howard) began by explaining that capital grants by the Commonwealth to the States were introduced in June 1970 by a Liberal-National Country Party government. This ritual selfcongratulation appears in the Treasurer's speech on the States Grants (Capital Assistance) Bill every year. In fact I have the impression, on reading this speech, that the Treasurer has simply taken out last year's speech, changed the dates and the amounts-he has not had to change the amounts very much-and read it out for, as usual, the speech is repetitively self-justifying and misleading. Perhaps it is not surprising that the Treasurer wants to get the credit for making a grant to the States-even a small one-but he neglects to say by how much this grant has fallen in real terms over the last few years. It is certainly the task of the Opposition to provide this perspective.
This Bill authorises payment of the grant component of the Loan Council program for State Governments in 1981-82. The total funds available to State governments for capital works this financial year is $1,307m, of which the capital grant I previously referred to is one-third. The real value of these capital funds has fallen by 44.6 per cent since 1975-76, a very considerable decline indeed. the police often work in archaic buildings, and so on.
In his second reading speech on this BilI, the Treasurer tended to argue that this sharp fall in the real value of general purpose capitaI' funds for the States did not matter because other funds for the States have been increasing. He mentioned, for example, that total funds to the States from the Commonwealth are estimated to increase by 8.5 per cent this year in comparison with 1980-81. However, he neglected to mention that; in his Budget Papers, he presented an estimate of the rate of inflation for this year of 10.75 per cent. This means that the real value of total funds for the States from the Commonwealth is expected to falI by 2.25 per cent this year, that is, the 8.5 per cent increase in funds compared with the 10.75 per cent inflation rate. Further, this neglects the effect of population growth. During the last 12 months, the population grew by 1.6 per cent. There were 240,000 more people in Australia in June of this year than in June of last year. When the population is growing funds for government services have to expand or the quality of those services declines. The Government has made no allowance at all for this in its payments to the States. The real value of total Commonwealth payments to the States this year is almost 4 per cent per person less than in the last financial year. The Treasurer's comments are, therefore, severely misleading. Is'it any wonder that the States are having trouble balancing their Budgets and are being forced to raise their taxes and charges?

The Treasurer also tries to get some mileage out of the fact that the relative importance of specific purpose payments has fallen. Specific purpose
payments are allocated to a particular expenditure purpose and the States must spend them in that area. Whilst it is clear that the States want maximum control over what funds are available to them, the decline in the relative importance of specific purpose payments is completely irrelevant if it is not matched by a compensating increase in general purpose payments. the important reasons for the real fall in the value of Commonwealth payments to the States. Another ploy used by the Treasurer to cloud the issue is to talk about the increased borrowing approvals given to the States' semi-government authorities for infrastructure projects. Infrastruc-

Mr WILLIS-I thank the House. This table is very interesting. It shows the allocations in the 1975-76 Budget and the 1981-82 Budget, the one which operates for this financial year. It compares the amounts in real terms for the allocations to capital expenditure under various headings for the Commonwealth sector. The allocation from the Commonwealth Budget has declined by 58 per cent in real terms. The allocation in the Commonwealth sector for statutory authorities has declined by 16.6 per cent in real terms. The total Commonwealth public sector capital expenditure in real terms has decreased by 30.7 per cent. These are not just minor adjustments to the accounts. They are dramatic cutbacks in the size of public sector capital expenditures. They are enormous cutbacks which have horrendous effects on the abilities of the Commonwealth and the States to provide the level of services which was provided previously. They have enormous implications for the social infrastructure in particular. I will say a little more about that in the near future.
When we look at the expenditure by the States in this table we see that general purpose capital funds-the funds which are the subject of the Bill now before the House-have declined in real terms by 44.6 per cent, the figure I gave previously. That is an enormous reduction. Specific purpose capital payments have declined by 56.7 per cent, a reduction of more than half in real terms. The total payments to the States in the capital area, excluding infrastructure, have declined by 39.9 per cent. If we take into account infrastructure borrowings which began in 1978 and which have enabled the States to spend in particular areas in relation to resource infrastructure projects, we see that the total capital funds available to the States have declined in real terms by 29.4 per cent. That is an enormous reduction in the total amount of funds available to States for capital expenditures, including infrastructure borrowings. If we put all those areas together and

look at the total capital expenditures for the Commonwealth and State sectors and at the real movement in capital expenditures over the sixyear period we see a reduction of 28.5 per cent. That amounts to absolute sabotage of the public sector and its ability to maintain an appropriate level of capital works for this nation. They are quite devastating figures. They bring to light the enormous damage that this Government is bringing about in this country through its continued ideologically based cutbacks and slashes in the amount of real capital funds being made available to the public sector.
It can be said, of course, that the table shows only what has happened to funds which have been specifically earmarked for capital expenditure and that other funds are available to the State governments, for instance, through the general revenue grants which can be spent on anything although they are normally spent on recurrent expenditures. It is argued by the Government, not least the Minister for Housing and Construction (Mr McVeigh) who is now at the table, that these funds are available to the States to spend on capital works if they so wish. To this let me make two comments. Firstly, the real value of total funds made available to the States by the Commonwealth from its Budget-not just total capital funds but total funds-this financial year is, as I mentioned before, 2t per cent less in real terms than the last financial year. If there is less money from the Commonwealth Budget as well as markedly less money for capital works specifically, it is impossible to argue that the States can easily maintain capital expenditures by utilising their general revenue grants unless the Commonwealth is saying to the States that they should spend much less on recurrent expenditures, including salaries for school teachers and workers in the public sector generally, which are the main areas of recurrent expenditures. If that were to happen it would clearly have implications for the level of services provided by the States.
Furthermore, if one looks at the total funds from the Commonwealth Budget to the States over the period from 1975-76 to 1981-82 one finds that there has been a substantial real decline in the level of funds made available. It has declined by approximately 10 per cent. I stress this figure to the House. There has been a 10 per cent reduction in real terms in the total amount of funds made available to the States by the Commonwealth in the period 1975-76 to 1981-82. In the period of this Governmerit's control of this country since the end of 1975 the real level of funds has been reduced by 10 per cent when the population has increased by 8 per cent. On a per

2900 REPRESENTATIVES 17 November 1981

States Grants (Capital Assistance) Bill

head basis, therefore, there has been a decline of 18 per cent in the real level of Budget funds made available by this miserly Government to the States. So much for the argument that lesser funds to the States from capital allocations in the Budget can be made up from other Budget payments not allocated to any particular purpose. It is clearly an absolute nonsense given the size of the total cutback in funds to the States of 18 per cent in real terms on a per capita basis. Furthermore, when one takes into account not only funds from the Budget but also funds available to the States and the Northern Territory from outside the Budget, that is through semi-government borrowing programs and the so-called infrastructure borrowing program, it is clear that there has still been a substantial real fall in funds made available to the States. Between 1975-76 and 1981-82 there has been a fall in the real level of all funds made available to the States from all sources in the Budget or outside the Budget of 5.3 per cent. Even if we include the borrowing programs of the States there is a 5 per cent reduction in the real amount of funds made available to the States. When we take, as we must, account of the fact that there has been a population increase in the period of 8 per cent we see that there has been a 13 per cent real decline in funds made available to the States by the Commonwealth from all sources inside the Budget and outside it.
Another point that has to be made in this regard is that because of this reduction in funds to the States, especially the drastic cutback in funds for capital expenditures, there has been a consec quential severe decline in the overall level of government investment in the country. The national accounts for the year 1980-81 show that the real level of public gross fixed capital expenditure in Australia-in short, government investment-during the period 1975-76 to 1980-81, the last financial year, declined by 11 per cent. That is a very substantial reduction in the real level of government investment in this country in that six-year period. Furthermore, we are saying that there is 11 per cent less government investment in to to in real terms than for 1975-76. If one takes into account the 6t per cent rise in population in those years, one sees that on a per capita basis the decline was 17t per cent.
This is a very severe reduction in the totalleve1 of government investment in this country. There has been a 17t per cent decline since the Labor Government was in office in the levels which operated last financial year, and they will presumably be down further in this current financial year. These are quite staggering figures. They give the absolute lie to Government arguments that

cutbacks in capital expenditures can be offset by the use of other expenditure. This Government knows from its own experience that it is far more difficult politically to reduce recurrent expenditure than it is to reduce capital expenditure. The result of this Government's actions in cutting back its own capital expenditures and slashing capital funds to the States is that government investment has been devastated by this Government. That process is continuing in this current financial year. The Bill before the House is clear proof of my assertion.
Four points flow from this fact. The first point is that the impact of this severe reduction on the economy as a whole is substantial. If one cuts back on the level of government investment-which is an important part of the economy-in such severe terms, then each year a negative growth factor is being built into the economy and the possibilities of obtaining substantial economic growth are to that degree lessened. In terms of the overall rate of economic growth and employment growth we see that such cutbacks are absolutely adverse. Secondly, there is the impact of these continuing reductions on the quantity and standard of services provided by government. If one continually slashes the real level of government investment, as this Government does year by year ahd continues to do in the Bill now before the House, inevitably one will find it more difficult to provide government services of a capital nature and of a recurrent nature. There is a cut back for the whole array of services provided through what is known as the social infrastructure; that is, the array of services which are needed by people if they are to live decent lives in this community such as in the area of education, decent hospitals, roads, public transport, welfare housing, child care and so on. All of these areas must be affected if the Government continually cuts back on the level of government investment.
Thirdly, by these cutbacks in government investment we have a severe unbalancing of the economy because within government investment that is occurring the growth area is resource industries infrastructure; that is, power stations, coal loaders, railways and so on. The social infrastructure, the areas that I have just mentioned a moment ago, are the areas which are taking the burden of the reduction. There is an overall reduction of government expenditure which is absolutely severe. Within that level of investment is a growth area of resource investment. So the social infrastructure, which is the rest of that investment, is where the burden of reductions is being felt. We are reaching the stage where that reduction is becoming thoroughly intolerable.

States Grants (Capital Assistance) Bill

17 November 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 2901

Fourthly, there is the irony of the situation that despite this sabotage of public sector investment the Government still argues that government expenditure, particularly government capital expenditure, is the cause of increased interest rates. After today's report perhaps interest rates are very much in the news. I refer to the Government's argument that the cause of increased interest rates is the high level of demands for funds in this community and, therefore, what we need to do is to cut back government demand for funds. This has been the' continuing refrain of this Government and is a justification for the cutbacks in government investment in this -country. It hangs on the thread that interest rates are determined by the demand for funds in the capital market. Quite clearly, the increase in the demand for funds has not come from the public sector. How can it be said that the increase in demand for funds has come from the public sector when government investment has been slashed heavily?
I am sure that honourable members are all aware that the public sector borrowing requirement has been considerably reduced in the last year or two. How can it be said in that situation that the government sector is causing this rise in interest rates and that the appropriate response to that situation is to slash the level of government expenditure even further? It is an absolute absurdity to argue in that way. If we look at where the demand for funds is coming from, we need only to look at what has happened to business investment. Business investment last year went up by 23 per cent which is an extraordinary incre~se very much related to the resources boom. That Increase must be where the rapid increase in demand for funds has come from and to which one must describe the interest rate pressures if one says that interest rates are determined by the demand for funds in the capital market. In these circumstances it is absurd to argue that government investment is the cause of rising interest rates or that an appropriate response to rising interest rates is to further slash government investment. Such investment has already been sabotaged by this Government, as I have clearly shown. I move:
That all words after 'That' be bmitted with a view to substituting the following words:
'whilst not opposing the Bill, the House is of the opinion that the Bill, by further sharply reducing the real level of funds for State government capital works, is-
(I) making it impossible for the States to provide an adequate capital expenditure program;
(2) reducing the extent and standard of community services;
(3) undermining the efficiency and productive capacity of many industries especially the building industry;

(4) producing unbalanced development by facili.tating expansion of infrastructure for the resource mdustries while severely contracting expenditure on the social infrastructure;
(5) increasing unemployment, and
(6) intensifying pressure on the States to introduce a second income tax'.
