Mr Howe-I second the amendment.
Mr CHAPMAN (Kingston) (9.48)-The remarks we have just heard from the honourable member for Gellibrand (Mr Willis) demonstrate that the Australian Labor Party is irretrievably wedded to big government and, more than that, to big spending government and, the~efore, either i~ evitably spiralling taxation or an lncreased deficit and hence increased government borrowing and spiralling interest rates. The Bi1l .that we. are debating, the States Grants (Capital Assistance) Bill, provides non-repayable capital grants to the States of $435.75m. This grant component of the Australian Loan Council program for State governments of $1,307.25m was agreed to at the June Loan Council meeting. These grants were initiated by a Liberal Country Party government in 1970.
Although the States are free to apply the grants as they choose, the major purpose at that time was to help finance schools. It is therefore appropriate in debating this legislation to discuss the relative responsibilities of Federal and State governments in the sphere of education. This is especially so in the light of the growing practice of gross misrepresentation of the attitude of the Federal Government towards education, especially as regards funding. The facts on the Federal funding of education do not support the hostile reaction that has been generated through teacher organisat.i~ns, academic staff associations and student poittIcal bodies in concert with the Labor Party. I therefore welcome the opportunity this debate provides to set the record straight. Over nearly six years since the Fraser Government came to office it has effectively fulfilled the proper role of the Federal Government in education by maintaining adequate funding. This was again demonstrated in the recent Budget with the provision of $3,254.7m for education this financial year. This represents an increase of nearly one per cent in real terms; that is after allowing for inflation over the period 1979-80. That, in turn, follows a one and a half per cent increase in real terms in that year as against 1978-79. When combined with funds provided by the State governments which in fact have the major constitutional responsibility for education, over $7000m per year or $20m every day is

2902 REPRESENTATIVES 17 November 1981

States Grants (Capital Assistance) Bill

being spent by governments on education in Australia. That takes 6.1 per cent of Australia's gross domestic product. So one could be excused for treating the criticism of the Federal Government's education policies and programs with some scepticism. That scepticism is as valid of criticism of its approach to the school sector as is its approach to other sectors of education.

would preclude this necessary and beneficial variation. Similarly, the decision to administer the Special Education Program through a nongovernment committee supervised by the Schools Commission rather than as previously by State education departments, removes a source of disagreement and recognises variations in need depending on the type of handicap involved.

One source of criticism of the Government's policy in regard to the school sector has been the minority statement by two members of the Commonwealth Schools Commission. It should be noted that this was a minority statement not supported by the other nine members of the Schools Commission. An examination of the minority statement reveals those criticisms to be totally without substance. The minority statement criticises other members of the Commission for not condemning the Government over guidelines given to the Commission. This completely ignores the fact that the annual report is not the appropriate vehicle for such comments. The role of the annual report of the Commission is to carve up the financial resources provided by the Federal Government following its consideration of the recommendations contained in the triennial report of the Commission. If any comment on the guidelines is justified, the triennial report is the appropriate vehicle, not the annual report.
The minority statement also criticiser the Government for its selective acceptance or rejection of single recommendations in the triennial report of the Commission. But surely that is exactly what the Government should do. The role of the Commission is to give public advice in its triennial reports to government as to what the Commission sees as needs in education. The role of the Government is to assess that advice in the light of its own policies and available resources and to determine priorities for action. If the Government merely gave blanket acceptance to advice by the Commission, in reality the Commission and not the Government would be governing.
The minority statement also criticises the removal of some discretionary powers from the Commission following recommendations of the Review of Commonwealth Functions, the so called razor gang. But every change made in this regard has been to the benefit of those to whom programs are directed. For example, the Government prevented the Schools Commission from laying down detailed guidelines for the Severely Handicapped Children's Program. This is because great variety exists in preferred methods of providing for the educrtion of severely handicapped children. Detailed guidelines of the Commission

Criticism of the Federal Government's allocation of financial assistance to schools in general and to government schools in particular is quite unjustified. The Government has provided $1,008.1 m for Schools Commission programs in 1982-an increase of 4.6 per cent in real terms after allowing for inflation. This provides for significant real increases in some programs and maintenance in real terms of most. The cuts in funding involve a modest reduction of $6.3m in a total Commonwealth expenditure on government schools of some $400m. This is quite justified in the light of declining numbers in schools due to demographic trends. The overall real increases are to the Government's great credit. They have been provided despite continuing restraint in Government spending overall and cuts in a number of other areas of Government spending. Thus the Government, in providing $269,174,000 in recurrent grants to government schools has maintained funding in real terms. When related to declining student numbers, this means that Commonwealth funding per student place in government schools has never been higher. It makesnon-. sense of criticisms of this Government's attitude to the government school sector. There has been consistent maintenance of effort by this Government with regard to government schools.
Mr Howe-You want to go around and tell that to a few teachers in your electorate.
Mr CHAPMAN-I have been going around telling teachers in my electorate exactly that. The result of this maintenance of effort has been that all government schools have reached ahead of target, and many have exceeded, the equipment and staffing goals set as desirable by the Karmel Interim Committee for the Australian Schools Commission. Federal Government assistance has achieved this, despite the fact that the major responsibility for funding government schools lies with the States, a responsibility which the untied grants provided by this legislation help to fulfil. The States provide some 89 per cent of capital and recurrent funding whereas the Federal responsibility is II per cent. The real responsibility of the Federal Government is to identify national needs which have been neglected by the States or forgone. The Federal Government thus has an

States Grants (Capital Ass,'tal'.ce) Bill

17 November 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 2903

overseeing role. It must retain flexibility to move funds to fulfil identified national needs,
In the recent past, resource levels were a national need so a significant additional injection of funds to that area was justified, But, as I have just mentioned, the Karmel goals have now been achieved; so while it is important to maintain effort in that area, as has been done, it is quite wrong to criticise the Government for not providing further large increases in that area because the current major identifiable national need in education is that of transition from school to work, That is therefore the area to which any additional available funds should first and foremost be directed. The Fraser Government is fulfilling its responsibility with regard to school to work transition with a new program of $250m in real terms over five years initiated in 1980. Funds for this program have been increased by 26,6 per cent to $35,2m this year, a real increase of some 15 per cent. This large increase is a clear demonstration of the Fraser Government's commitment to areas of identified need and makes nonsense of the critics, This commitment is further demonstrated by increased funding to other areas of continuing national need-for example, the Special Education Program which is designed to supplement recurrent funding for special schools and schools with special classes to meet the needs of handicapped children and to provide support for the integration of handicapped children into regular schools. By providing $17,768,000 for 1982, the Fraser Government has provided a real increase of 5,7 per cent this year compared with 1981. Similarly, the English as a Second Language Program provides funds to improve the English language competence of students whose first language is not English. Funding has increased by 4.9 per cent in real terms for that Program.
The Federal Government has also been criticised in the minority statement of the Schools Commission and by others for its allegedly favourable treatment of non-government schools. But funding of non-government schools is predicated on a number of factors. Firstly, the Fraser Government believes it has a responsibility to encourage freedom of choice and diversity in education. This does not relate only to the nongovernment school sector. The Government has also provided funding for a program developing choice and diversity in the government school system. But the non-government school sector is the most significant element of the education system providing opportunity for choice and diversity, Of course, this is a basic tenet of liberalism,

Secondly, those who exercise this choice are entitled to some reimbursement of the taxation revenue they provide to the total education budget, but which they do not use through the government school system, Taxation has some income redistribution effects, not the least in education, but its major role is to pay for community services. It is therefore reasonable that the taxpayer, using an alternative service, receives some reimbursement for that. He or she still contributes to the bulk of the cost of using that alternative service through the payment of fees. To give effect to these elements of choice and partial reimbursement, the Federal Government provides minimum final).cial assistance per non-government school student of 20 per cent of the assessed average per student government school standard costs. The most recent data for these costs is $1,480 per student for government primary schools and $2,402 for government secondary schools, thereby providing minimum assistance of $296 per nongovernment primary school student and $480 per non-government secondary school student. It is quite dishonest to claim that resources are being transferred from the government school sector to the non-government school sector in this way. Clearly, grants to the non-government school sector go up only if costs and therefore resources of the government sector go up. Such grants go up by only a small proportion of that government sector increase.
Federal Government assistance to the nongovernment sector is also predicated on the fact that about 80 per cent of the non-government schools have recurrent resource levels about 30 per cent below those of the average government school. One reflection of this is that primary school student-teacher ratios are 24.4 : 1 for Catholic schools compared with 20.3 : 1 for government schools. Since 1975 the ratio for Catholic primary schools has come down from 27.4 : 1, whereas for government schools it has come down from 22.7 : 1. The comparison at secondary level is: Catholic schools 17 : 1 as against 12.3 : 1 for government schools, having reduced from 19.7: 1 and 14,2: 1 respectively in 1975. So, despite being in a significantly better position in student-teacher ratios initially, government schools have not been significantly outpaced in the improvements since then, retaining the significant advantage that they have over Catholic schools. That is why, for these schools, the Federal Government has provided per student grants greater than the basic 20 per cent over recent years,
To ensure that. the greatest funding goes to those non-government schools in greatest need the previous six resource categories have been

17 November 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 2905

The Commonwealth has allocated a total of $121m to Western Australia in general purpose capital grant funds for 1981-82, comprising $80.677m in approved Loan Council borrowings and $40.33m in interest free capital grants. Compared with the amount of $120.951m in 1981-82, the allocation to Western Australia this financial year represents a reduction in real terms of around $14.5m. The borrowing program for the State's larger authorities, excluding infrastructure financing, has been cut from a total program of $1l7.358m in 1980-81 to an estimated $101.6m in 1981-82. This represents a reduction in capital funds from this source amounting to around $31.4m in real terms. Approved Loan Council borrowings for special additions for infrastructure amount to $121.2m in 1981-82. Although Western Australia recevied the most favourable treat-
or ment in the total amount of approved borrowings,
the level approval represents around 65 per cent of the State's scheduled borrowings for infrastructure in 1981-82.
Notably, the Loan Council agreed to the States determining the allocation of the approved total between the projects for which the funds were sought. Sir Charles Court, the Premier of Western Australia, has always regarded the infrastructure financing as his project. In 1978-79 he referred to his formal submission to the Loan Council on the subject and went on to say that the new arrangements are a sensible and logical development in Commonwealth-State financial arrangements. In 1979-80 he again took credit for what he called the breakthrough that followed his submission. However, by the 1980-81 Budget, this new tier of borrowings had begun to backfire on Western Australia. Sir Charles Court expressed surprise and regret that general loans funds were being cut to compensate for infrastructure loan funds. This was a virtual admission that he had been conned by the Fraser Government into supporting a move that would clearly hurt Western Australia. The damage is already clear. The level of noninfrastructure loan funds has deteriorated to the point where it is now only slightly more than half the 1975-76 allocation in real terms, that is half the allocation effectively made in the last Labor Budget.
Time does not permit me to discuss the specific uses that the infrastructure funds are to be put to or what could have been done with more general works funds. Some of the proposed infrastructure expenditures are of doubtful value but the general situation of squeezing existing government services on the ground that it is more important to fund the great resources boom can clearly be seen as bad planning now so that even Government

Ministers try to dampen any talk of boom or any expectation of benefits for the people of Australia.
To understand the impact of these changes in the allocation of loan funds, it is necessary to outline the broader sphere of Commonwealth State financial relations and particularly this Government's so-called new federalism. After the Premiers Conference of 1976, at which the financial and administrative framework of the new federalism was established, Sir Charles Court stated:
I am confident that the scheme will now operate successfully and to the advantage of the States.
He went on to say:
These are history making developments and I belive the Premiers' Conference last week will be seen in future years as a turning point in Federal-State financial relations and the beginning of greater State independence and increased self reliance.
He was certainly right about it being a turning point. The direction has been around the bend ever since as far as Western Australian Budgets and the resultant State charge increases on the Western Australian population are concerned.
In 1975-76, total Commonwealth payments and Loan Council borrowings to or for the six States represented 39.1 per cent of total Commonwealth outlays. In 1981-82, the proportion of Commonwealth outlays allocated to the six States is estimated to be 33.4 per cent, even below the level of support in the last year of the McMahon Government at the turn of the decade-l 970-71. The net financial effect of the new federalism on Western Australia since 1976-77 and including 1981-82 is a total revenue loss amounting to over $350m. This figure includes the revenue loss of $49.1m which resulted from the abolition in 1980-81 of the guarantee that the State would not be worse off than it was under previous arrangements. This was the guarantee that Sir Charles Court had pinned his hopes on. New federalism for the people of Western Australia has meant higher State taxes and charges, a lowering of living standards and cutbacks in the standard range of essential community services such as health and education. In fact it has been a disaster.
The other factor that must be considered when assessing the impact of these Loan Council allocations over the next year is the Commonwealth Grants Commission review of tax sharing entitlements. The effect of the State factors recommended in the report of the Commonwealth Grants Commission, based on a six-State distribution, is a reduction in Western Australia's share of Commonwealth tax collections from the existing 12.284 per cent to around 9.826 per cent. The revenue effect amounts to an annual loss of

2906 REPRESENTATIVES 17 November 1981

States Grants (Capital Assistance) Bill

$160.7m, based on the amount available for distribution in 1981-82 and estimates of State population at 31 December 1981. The report of the Grants Commission has been referred back to the Commission for review. It is to be hoped that it will produce an upward revision in Western Australia's State factor, despite whatever incompetence there may have been in the presentation before it of the Western Australian case.
It must be admitted that the Commission's revenue needs assessment for Western Australia is basically accurate and sound. In particular the implicit c~iticism by the Commission of Western Australia's comparatively low revenue raising effort in the area of mining royalties is vindicated by the fact that the State Government has had immediate recourse to increasing royalties in its 1981-82 Budget. On the other hand, the Commission's assessment of expenditure and business undertaking needs does require drastic reconsideration. The review should expand the field under consideration to include a wide range of basic services such as power, water, housing and other essential services provided by business undertakings in the States. In addition, a more comprehensive analysis of the cost disabilities of providing services of an equal range and standard in remote areas of Western Australia must be undertaken.
The Commission report stated that, in conducting its examination of Western Australia several areas of interest were overflown at low altitude. I suggest that in future the Commissioners might decide to land and have a look at what is going on on the ground; perhaps then they might come to different conclusions about what ought to be done in Western Australia. The Western Australia Government's submissions to the Commission were, however, generally weak and uncoordinated. This could be a partial explanation, I believe, for Western Australia's relatively harsh treatment by the Commission. Clearly Sir Charles. Court failed to impress the Commissioners with his case for Western Australia.
Sir Charles Court attempts to convey the view to the Western Australian community that'he has been a solid, hard fighter for Western Australian interests. If Western Australians could only read that extraordinarily lengthy document-I do not blame them if they do not-they would see throughout it evidence of the failure of the Western Australian Government to press the points that it wished to make on matters of key interest to Western Australians. The effective exclusion of power, water and housing from the calculation of State need factors-and in respect of everyone of those items Western Australia is at a very severe

disadvantage-is an indication of the extent to which the Western Australian Government, despite the fact that these items were within the framework of the Commission's reference, failed to press Western Australia's relative disadvantage in these matters.
To sum up this tale of woe, these Loan Council allocations are but one part of a disastrous situation arising in Western Australia. They express the commitment to the mythical boom that affects the Commonwealth and Western Australian governments. Financial relations between the two have been extraordinary. In the name of States rights, Sir Charles Court has supported major moves by the Fraser Government to slash State spending. He has been conned time and again. It is only now when Western Australia is hundreds of millions of dollars worse off and facing the probability of another disaster from the Grants Commission that Sir Charles Court is beginning to wake up. If he had joined with the progressive State governments, such as that in New South Wales, in opposing these moves from the beginning, these attacks on the States may have been held off. If he had not been so persistent in attempting to bring down the Whitlam Government, his own situation and that of Western Australian consumers of electricity, water and State housing commission homes or whatever would have been far better off than they are now. What State rights have meant in Western Australian is the destruction of the Western Australian State Budget. It is interesting that Liberal governments, one by design and one by incompetence" have done more than any other government since Federation to cripple the State financially, all in the name of State rights.
Mr HOWE (Batman) (10.19)-In the past five years, we have seen a massive reallocation of <:apital spending in this country. The Government has sought to .redistribute negatively capital spending from the areas of social expenditure and social need-that is, education, health, transport and housing-providing more and more supp.ort for the corporate sector. As the honourable member for Gellibrand (Mr Willis) pointed.out, there has been in real terms a cut of 29 per cent.in capital moneys made available to the States, including funds for infrastructure borrowing during the six years of this Government's period in office. The social effects of these transfers of resources are now apparent right across the country. They mean poorer services and reduced standards of living for ordinary people. The Government had undoubtedly hoped that, as a result of these transfers in the distribution of wealth, there would be a recovery in private capital expenditure; that is,

States Grants (Capital Assistance) Bill

17 November 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 2907

companies encouraged by various incentives and tax reductions would embark upon an expansion of activity which would result in an increased rate of economic growth.
Central to this argument was the assumption that it would be possible to encourage a resources boom-indeed, a resources led recovery. There is no doubt that in part this strategy has had its successes. We have had-albeit unevenly-higher periods of economic growth. We have had an upsurge of investment in certain resource projects. However, this growth has not been subject to any careful planning and would appear to be faltering now with a sharp decline occurring in international markets for resources especially metal markets. The result that is now occurring is a scaling down of resource projects with a number of corporations pulling out of plant projects or threatening to pull out of them. There is a serious danger that, in the pattern of development that has been fostered by this Government, it will be the government sector which will suffer the risks implicit in it. It is quite clear that the public sector-that is, social expenditure but in the final analysis the people-has had to bear the sacrifices. This is apparent in many of the areas to which I have referred.
However, other risks exist to which the same attention has not been given. Much of the public investment which is accompanying resource based development will, in fact, be at risk because of the failure of major projects to go ahead. In 1978 the Government encouraged the States, through their semi-government authorities to go ahead with vastly expanded loan programs especially for electricity generation and other energy-related projects. The States did not need a great deal of encouragement from the Commonwealth and embarked on an enthusiastic program of borrowing. It appears that at this year's Loan Council meeting the Commonwealth indicated that it had had second thoughts about this program. However, we now have, in almost every State of Australia, major public works projects centred on producing more and more energy. Much of this additional electricity will be to service the aluminium industry.
Over the past few months we have seen a massive reduction in the expected levels of development of that industry. So far this year we have had abandoned projects which would have added 600,000 tonnes to aluminium production capacity by 1985. This is without the possible cancellation of the 'Portland smelter of Alcoa, which is under some doubt. In my view these problems flow from the failure of the Government to interest itself in fundamental policy issues. For example, at the

time in which the Federal Government was encouraging the States to embark on a massive expansion of loans for the accelerated development of electricity generation, it was also promising to produce a national energy policy, or at least a Green Paper designed to canvass the relevant issues. In fact the Prime Minister (Mr Malcolm Fraser) never permitted that Green Paper to see the light of day.
The Government achieved what it wanted out of its energy policy which was a massive infusion of revenue from petrol taxes or excise. It has no interest in looking to ensure that the Commonwealth developed an approach to energy. policy which related all of the sources of energy or the means of ensuring that epergy was distributed to consumers in an equitable manner, orthat in the long term we had policies which we would ensure for future generations security of supply. In my view this is an amazing situation. On the one hand we are seeking to conserve liquid fuels by pushing up prices, and on the other hand we are encouraging the use of other fuels, such as natural gas and coal, at a rate as fast as the international market will bear.
But surely the lesson of the last few years is that energy policy cannot be separated in the final analysis into liquid and solid fuels as if they bear no relation to each other. The price of energy is tending to rise domestically whichever fuel one is talking about,.. with the possible exception of uranium, because of the impact of the Organisation of Petroleum Exporting Countries crisis on overall national and international energy policies. I think we are beginning to see the result.
When the Federal Government moved from a cheap oil policy, it was moving towards a dear energy policy across the board. It did not admit this. But that is the direction in which we are being takenas we seek to make energy resources avail" able to the developed world. We cannot imagine for a moment that those companies with an interest in oil are keeping out of the solid fuel areas. Of course they are not. They are buying into alternative sources of energy to ensure that they will continue to dominate the supply of energy throughout the developed world. They have their corporate plans for Australia and it suits them for the Australian Government to continue to talk about the need to leave such matters to the so-called market.
In these circumstances a number of basic issues arise. One of those issues is the matter of energy pricing. With respect to oil we have moved to import parity pricing but with other fuels we have not taken international prices into account. Thus

2908 REPRESENTATIVES 17 November 1981

States Grants (Capital Assistance) Bill

it has been argued by the Department of the Treasury that we need at least to consider opportunity pricing in relation to electricity. Why should we export coal as aluminium at a price which bears no relationship to the price which it might bring as coking coal sold in Japan?
Of course, this is not a simple matter but at least the Government should have addressed itself to it. Similarly, the Government has not sought to require the States to reveal power prices to electricity consumers, such as aluminium producers, before giving loans for expanded electricity generation to meet the needs of those producers. I would have thought that was quite fundamental to the assessment of the viability of such projects.
The Government has made no study of the changing cost structure of the electricity generation industry and what the implications might be for the future. Thus it has been caught unawares by the exponential increases which are now beginning to take place in power prices in a number of States. Indeed, it was recently argued that if further funds are to be drawn by the authorities from tariffs for capital works, this can only mean further massive increases in tariffs. We have seen increases of the order of 40 per cent occur In Victoria over the past 18 months. However, the alternative measure has problems because it will result in the accumulation of a massive debt against future generations of taxpayers.
The Government has consistently taken the view that the planning of energy and energy intensive industries is a matter for the private sector. But there is a real danger that this will mean that the risks are increasingly borne by the public sector while the security and the profits will flow inevitably to the private sector. This in my view, is especially the case in relation to the aluminium industry.
The Aluminium Company of America is currently negotiating with the Victorian Government concerning its proposed smelter at Portland. The company, in the face of a difficult short term market situation, has been threatening to pull out of the project despite the $70m already invested. In the event that it does pull out the State Government will be left with massive commitments to power lines to Portland, and the construction of the Loy Yang power station, the costs of which have been escalating at the rate of $lm a day. The company argues that the enterprise will be only marginally profitable given the present state of international metal markets, especially the first two stages. However, this company is hardly operating in a true market situation, controlling as it does a substantial share of world production. It can

afford to plan over a very long period with some confidence that it will eventually be able to wield substantial control over world prices when we have another international economic upturn.
The Victorian Government, faced with an election in a matter of months, is undoubtedly being influenced to make a decision on the terms and conditions offered to the company, under the pressure of a threat to close down yet another major project in the State. For these reasons it is crucial that national policies be determined with respect to electricity and electricity pricing. As the Department of the Treasury has warned there are serious dangers that electricity is being offered to aluminium producers at rates which do not reflect the national economic interest. In part this reflects the fact that we have never faced up to the implications of a policy of electricity for export.
In the present circumstances it is not simply a matter of paying for costs, but a matter of getting a return on Australian resources. Given that we are expanding supply to take advantage of a changing international energy situation the marginal costs are also more relevant than they have ever been. But the marginal cost of supplying electricity is going up dramatically for a variety of reasons. There are the unprecedented high interest rates, the increased demand for expensive anti-pollution devices to be added to power stations, and the diminishing economics of scale associated with the very large stations which are more complex and appear to operate at relatively low levels of efficiency.
In summary, it is then a time in which we need to do some clear thinking. What are our national objectives with respect to energy? To what extent should we give consideration to the long term? Are we simply interested in exploiting resources at the fastest possible rate in a policy which seeks to define national energy objectives and then utilise our resources in conformity with those objectives?
I believe that we are moving along a path of development which poses extreme dangers in the future. I doubt that a country as geographically dispersed as Australia needs to have an energy policy which must mean expensive energy in the longer term. Also, given our resources it ought to be possible to guarantee relatively cheap energy for all our people. Cheap energy flows, in my view, not from rapid development of our resources but from attention to both the most effective forms of supplying energy and also the most effective and efficient forms of the utilisation of energy; that is, attention to energy conservation.

States Grants (Capital Assistance) Bill

17 November 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 2909

The development of a comprehensive national energy policy is not an optional extra. It is absolutely fundamental. This is a government that has little or no interest in policy. It is interested only in the short term. It identifies the interests of the international corporate sector inevitably with the interests of Australia. It is not even interested in business in the broadest sense. It does not understand the inter-relationship between energy and employment policy. It does not understand, for example, that in a situation of surplus labour and diminishing energy resources different policies need to be adopted. The Government's relentless drive towards restructuring the economy around capital and energy intensive industries orientated towards uncertain international markets has built into it fundamental contradictions.
We as a nation need to make choices. Those choices need to be rational. But they should be made in the light of a comprehensive range of factors including the fundamental priority of maintaining and improving standards of living in Australia for all Australians. What this debate does is to provide an opportunity to point to the fact that the Government is sacrificing in the short term the well-being of ordinary people for the sake of a dream which I believe is not rooted in economic reality. We have no future as an advanced country if we develop around nonrenewable resources, owned by transnational companies, shipped overseas in foreign ships and not transformed past the semi-processing phase in Australia.
There is no doubt that industry in this country has had to be subsidised and supported by consumers and tax payers. Before we rush in to provide massive subsidies to resource giants we should carefully assess what their contribution
will be to the Australian economy and what they will do to advance our standard of living. The record of these companies in other countries, particularly in the Third World, would suggest that they would not make a great contribution. That is why we need to look for examples of where governments have been prepared to set objectives and to force these corporations to meet those objectives.
If the Victorian Government, and/or the Australian Government wishes to continue to encourage Alcoa to go ahead at Portland it should make absolutely certain that there is no degree of subsidy involved. The private inquiry carried out by Professor Cochrame does not give one a great deal of comfort that that, in fact, will be the case. These powerful companies which dominate the industry ought not to require subsidies. One would have thought that many members of the

Government parties would agree to that. It is what many of them argue. But if these big boys of industry require subsidies how can one criticise the competitive sector of the economy asking for assistance from the Government to ensure that it does not go to the wall?
In this mixed economy, for the most part, industry will not be able to pay its own way, particularly in times of flat demand and international recession. The question is: Who gets subsidies for what reason?
The States Grants (Capital Assistance) Bill reflects the fact that people are getting less and less support for basic services. The share of capital resources going to meet social needs is constantly being diminished. We should ensure that the funds that are available in this country, particularly in the public sector are not being transferred out of the country in the form of subsidies to international resource giants.
Finally, I speak in support of the amendment, particularly as it relates to clauses 2 and 4. I emphasise that clause 4 refers to producing unbalanced development by facilitating the expansion of infrastructure for the resource industries while severely contracting expenditure on the social infrastructure. This, indeed, has been the trend through the life of this Government. It isa trend which this amendment condemns. That has been the principal aim of my remarks with respect to the Bill.
Mr Hurford-Mr Deputy Speaker, I would like to explain that the Opposition is not dividing the House on this issue not because it does not feel strongly about it but because of the lateness of the hour and the amount of business that needs to be carried out before the House adjourns.

NITROGENOUS FERTILIZERS SUBSIDY AMENDMENT BILL 1981
Secona Reading
Debate resumed from 27 October, on motion by MrMoore:
That the Bill be now read a second time.

291 0 REPRESENTATIVES 17 November 1981

Nitrogenous Fertilizers Subsidy Bill

Mr CUNNINGHAM (McMillan) ( 10.37)-The Nitrogenous Fertilizers Subsidy Amendment Bill increases the period for the payment of the nitrogenous fertilisers subsidy scheme and is of itself not one of the more important Bills introduced in this House. We on this side of the House do not oppose the extension of the period of payment for a further six months. However, the legitimacy of this form of subsidy requires some comment. In addressing myself to the Bill I fully realise that fertilisers of all sorts, particularly nitrogenous fertilisers, are one of the very important elements in food production in Australia. The production of food has reached a rather critical situation throughout the world. The use of fertilisers is vitally important to the well-being of millions of people.
There is a new development in the use of land which I would like to raise. It has some impact on the use of nitrogenous fertilisers and the use of subsidies. I refer to the approach throughout the world of using food-growing land and fuel crops for fuel. National programs to divert agricultural resources to produce fuel crops come at a time when efforts to expand world food output are losing momentum. Between 1950 and 1971 per capita grain production world wide increased by some 30 per cent leading to substantial improvements in nutrition not only in industrialised countries but also in much of the Third World. However, since 1971 growth in world output has slowed scarcely keeping pace with population growth.
The failure of the world grain output to match the increases in both population and income led to rising food prices during the 1970s. Throughout the 1960s and early 1970s the world had two major food reserves-large, often burdensome stocks of grain and a vast area of crop land idle under the United States of America farm programs. Together, these provided a period of unprecedented stability in world food supplies and prices. As recently as 1972 over 24 million hectares of United States' crop land was idle in the farm management programs. Since then most of that idle farm land has been returned to production. Even with this addition, the production of food in some years has not matched the growth in world demand.
As the 1980s begin the world has only one food reserve left, and that is its stocks of grain. These are at as Iowa level as they were in the 1972-73 period which led to savage increases in prices in 1974, creating problems for many people in the poorer parts of the world. In addition to the decline in reserves production has become more erratic. Efforts to double food output over the last

decade pushed agriculture into areas of marginal rainfall and on to marginal soils. The former is dramatically evident in the Soviet Union, where a crop failure every third year or so is commonplace in the virgin lands-that is, in the new areas brought into cultivation since the 1950s. Over the past decade world demand for grain has been expanding by some 30 million tonnes a year and of this total the bulk is needed to cover the increase in human numbers, leaving very little to upgrade diets.
While the world population growth has slowed, the absolute year to year increase remains at 70 million. In effect, the gradual decline in the rate of population growth has been offset by the increase in the size of the population. Rising incomes have also contributed to the increase in world food demand, particularly in areas where the consumption of livestock products and the grain to feed livestock has been rising rapidly. On the supply side, growth in the world food crop land base has slowed markedly since mid-century, expanding at only one-fifth the rate at which the population is expanding. Amongst all of this we have a new competitor seeking the use of land and fertilisers, and hence the use of nitrogenous fertilisers. I refer to the potential to produce alcohol to drive motor cars as against the use of the same land for food production. Even without the competition from energy crops, the world will be hard-pressed to avoid a decline in per capita food production during the decade ahead if the projected increase in population continues. As against this, we must look at the emergence of energy crops which will compete actively for the land available for food.
We have seen a large inflow of capital into this country, much of which in the last 18 months has gone towards buying up broad acres of land in Australia. It is interesting to note that much of the land that has been bought up is what I would term energy related land-land that eventually could be used for the production of fuel for motor cars as against fuel for human beings. We need only to look at what is happening in some other countries. For instance, the Brazilians signed a protocol in 1979 with the National Automotive Association confirming their intention of producing in the early 1980s 250,000 vehicles that would run entirely on alcohol. They have in effect planted some 200,000 hectares of crop land for sugar cane. Brazilian officials claim that the production of energy crops will be undertaken in addition to, rather than in competition with, other food crops. But energy crops compete not only for land but also for agricultural investment, water, fertilisers, farm management, fuels, farm market roads, agricultural credit and technical advisory services.

Nitrogenous Fertilizers Subsidy Bill

17 November 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 2911

The immediate consequences of the Brazilian energy program may be more internal than external but the more recently launched effort in the United States has broader ramifications. If the United States crop land is shifted to energy crop production to fuel automobiles on a massive scale it will be at the expense of the exportable surplus of grain. Over the past generation the entire world has come to depend heavily on the North American grain export, with just over four-fifths of the total being for the United States, and all but
a handful of countries now import grain, mostly from North America. A year or so ago the United States Government set a target to produce 500 million gallons of fuel alcohol a year by the end of 1981. It looked to be hopelessly optimistic. Now that quantity appears likely to be exceeded. As more and more alcohol distilleries are built in the United States to meet the official goal of produc-
ing 7,500 million litres of ethanol by the mid-1980s, the exportable surplus of grain will be reduced accordingly.
In addition to the traditional buyers of the North American grain market-the flour millers and the feed lot operators-in the grain importing countries there will be a fourth potentially large group. That will be the distilleries. Exactly how high the price of grain could be driven by this approach is difficult to say but any high increase in the price of grain would cause problems. Obviously, the higher petrol prices go the more the distillers can afford to pay per bushel of grain. When petrol prices reach $2 a gallon, given the existing gasahol subsidies in the United States, those United States distillers could easily afford to pay $5 a bushel for maize and, should the price of gasoline reach $3 a gallon, distillers would be able to pay close to $6 a bushel for maize without any subsidies or credits. If the phase-out of oil price controls in the United States proceeds on schedule and the oil price moves to the world market level by the end of 1982, gasolene prices will rise accordingly. Under these circumstances United States distillers will be in a very strong position to bid for a growing share of the grain harvest.
This has implications for Australia. We have vast acres of broad land that could be used for growing cassava, sugar cane and grain for the production of alcohol. If we proceed in an unregulated way and government subsidies are unwisely used to encourage production it may not be in the best interests of this country. It would not be in the best interests of the production of food and it could lead to a lot of instability. I urge this warning on the Government: It should carefully assess just what the position is in regard to energy related land-land where crops will be

grown in the future for the production of alcohol fuels and thus will compete directly with food production. As I have said, both Brazil and the United States have major programs now to convert agricultural commodities to alcohol. Countries such as Australia and New Zealand, and to a lesser degree South Africa, are considering the conversion of crops to alcohol for fuel on a commercial scale. This new interest in fuel crops could be an historical landmark. If more and more oil refineries are replaced by alcohol distilleries the world agricultural economy will be transformed. This will have a dramatic effect on Australia and Australia would be attracted to producing crops for alcohol production. I know that at the moment very many problems are to be sorted out in relation to the economics and technology of producing alcohol but in 1981 we could see the start of what might be a crisis situation in regard to the use of land and the use of our fertilisers.
This leads me to ask a couple of questions in regard to the use of subsidies, particularly those which apply to these fertilisers. Fertiliser subsidies can be used to affect production in one, two or perhaps three ways. The first is to assist in raising incomes at times of non-profitable prices. for agricultural products. This is not a very good measure to be used because any subsidy used to assist one industry automatically goes across to the very profitable industries, and hence we pass public funds across to help people who are doing very well. The second point is that subsidies could be used to encourage production in the particular direction in which a government wishes agricultural products to develop. This could involve the legitimate use of a subsidy of this nature, namely, to direct production. But we in Australia are at present far past this stage where we need to use a subsidy of this nature right across the board to direct the production of agricultural products. The second reading speech of the Minister for Business and Consumer Affairs (Mr Moore) reads:
The question of the future operation of the nitrogenous fertilisers subsidy is currently under inquiry by the Industries Assistance Commission.
The reporting date on the inquiry which was 16 October 1981-
This is the report on nitrogenous fertilisers-
has been extended to coincide with the reporting date of 28 February 1982 for the associated lAC inquiry into phosphatic fertilisers. This action was taken because of the commonality of the issues involved in both references.
The Minister referred in his speech to the fact that the subsidies on both phosphatic and nitrogenous fertilisers have a commonality, and the Industries Assistance Commission report which will come down in February will report on that matter. This raises a couple of interesting points, particularly

2912 REPRESENTATIVES 17 November 1981

Nitrogenous Fertilizers Subsidy Bill

in the light of a policy-we should bear in mind that the Campbell report on the Australian Financial System was brought down todaybased very much on free enterprise philosophies. We have the Campbell report to look at and later in the year the Government will have the lAC report to look at. To counterbalance that I suppose we will have National Country Party pressures. Those pressures, particularly in regard to subsidies, will be in stark contrast to what the Campbell report says and possibly to what the lAC report will say.
Mr Braithwaite-How much is this subsidy worth?
Mr CUNNINGHAM-The nitrogenous fertiliser subsidy will cost some $2m for the next six months. Any subsidy paid to an industry which could be very profitable is an expenditure of funds out of the public purse which perhaps could go to other more needy people in the community. We .are finding at present that this Government is only too happy to reduce the funding for many needy sections of the community. We have only to look at many of the things that have happened in the last six months as a result of the razor gang to see that this Government has two standards when it comes to this type of thing. I am not one of those who say that there are not many reasons why the rural sector should not be encouraged and assisted, particularly when, as at present, this Government's policies are absolutely tearing the heart out of the value of the money that it is expecting to receive from exports. Many industries at present using this nitrogenous fertiliser receive money in one hand from the Government and have it completely taken off them by the Government's economic policies. There is not much point in putting nitrogen on a fruit tree when the product cannot be sold overseas simply because the Government's policies have virtually destroyed the value of our currency.
I conclude by saying that we will need to assess within the next six months the whole situation of the fertiliser subsidies. I hope that, when the lAC report on the nitrogenous fertilisers and the phosphatic fertilisers comes down, this Government will take a common-sense approach. We do not want to see nitrogenous fertiliser subsidies being placed in the hands of people who perhaps would develop an industry not in the best interests of world food production. Nitrogenous fertiliser subsidies could quite easily be spent, at the expense of food production, on the development of the production of sugar cane or cassava for alcohol fuels for the motor car. This sort of policy direction is something that the Government must look at

seriously and it must take it into consideration before subsidies are put forward. There may be a good case for a complete change in direction after the lAC report is brought down in February.
I am very concerned that over many years, particularly the 1970s, this superphosphate subsidy issue has been used more for political reasons than for good economic sense. I know that many members of this Government, particularly in the Cabinet, receive large sums from the public purse, simply because they use large tonnages of fertiliser. I cannot see the common sense in that approach, because in other countries there is usually a means test approach to subsidies on fertilisers. I have heard many speakers on the Government side push that approach only too willing when they are talking about pensioners, but we do not hear too much of it when they are talking about the tonnages on which fertiliser subsidies can be paid. I suggest that a limit of 50 tonnes would be ample. If it were really necessary for the existence of farmers for there to be a $12 a tonne subsidy on superphosphate or a $20 a tonne subsidy on nitrogenous fertiliser, a cut-off point should be put into the arrangement. In the first place, we have to be sure that a subsidy is justified and, secondly, we have to have a cut-off point.
I strongly oppose any across the board subsidy, because a very profitable enterprise could be using up to 500 tonnes of fertiliser and getting a $6,000 hand-out from the public purse when there is no need for it at all. Of course, the argument then is that such enterprises making big profits would be paying fair taxes and that some of that money would be going back to the Government anyway.
Even if they are paying 46c in the dollar in tax, 54c in the dollar is going from the public purse into the pockets of people who are doing very well without handouts from the public purse. While we support the proposal for another six months, I hope that at the end of those six months there will be a big adjustment by the Government to this type of approach and that it will put into operation some of the practices that it is so keen to im-
pose on other sections of the community. If it finds, after the lAC report is handed down, that there is justification for the continuation of some sort of subsidy, at least it should be directed to those in need of that subsidy and should not be paid across the board to big corporations and big farmers who are returning only half of it in tax and pocketing the rest at public expense.
Mr BRAITHWAITE (Dawson) (10.54) -'.The honourable member for McMillan (Mr Cunningham), who preceded me in this debate, was going well during his set speech but, when he got to the impromptu areas of his speech, I am

afraid that he missed the point altogether. He was talking of massive subsidies. I remind the honourable member that the total amount of extra nitrogenous fertiliser subsidy is between $2m and $2.5m.

More than anything else I support this measure because of the cost to the Government

and to the consumer of protective subsidies and grants. I believe that industries in Australia receive too much protective support which prohibits innovation, initiative, restructuring and the use of modern technology. Might I say that that can apply to only one set of industries. The industries I represent, particularly the rural and mining industries, are innovative, they are prepared to restructure, and they use modern technology.
I spoke on similar legislation on 14 September 1978 and 11 October 1979. If we are to use grants and subsidies I believe they should be meaningful and appropriate. I agree with the previous speaker that they have to have a purpose. This legislation is being extended so that the lAC can complete a fresh inquiry to enable a decision to be made by 30 June 1982.
I mentioned the appropriateness of a subsidy. For instance, the rate per tonne of nitrogenous content has reduced from $78.74 per tonne in 1975 to $60 in 1976, $40 in 1977 and $20 since 1978. Mr BRAITHWAITE~This schedule shows an increase in the basic costs of nitrogenous fertilisers since 1974 of 136 per cent for urea, 107.5 per cent for aqua ammonia, and 106 per cent for ammonia nitrate. This increase has occurred over a period in which the subsidy has decreased by 75 per cent.
The increases in the prices of fertiliser reflect the increase in world crude oil prices. Since 1974 prices have gone from $US11.25 a barrel to $US32 at June 1981, an increase of 84 per cent. For these reasons, farm costs~and farms are the

great users of fertilisers and fuels-have escalated at figures well above the consumer price index. Farm products are sold on world markets where more competition and changing exchange rates are depressing the market prices for those products. I mentioned before the $3m cost of the subsidy for all of 1981-82. This is insignificant in amount but important in principle, particularly in regard to the changes in other costs.
Could I for a moment refer to the fuel pricing

2914 REPRESENTATIVES 17 November 1981

Nitrogenous Fertilizers Subsidy Bill

arrangements instituted many years ago. Basically, 1976-77 was the year in which the real impact of the policy took hold. The increase from $USI4.53 per barrel in 1979 to $US24 per barrel in 1980 was significant and resulted in severe criticism for many people, particularly from the Opposition. The present price of $32 per barrel shows a decline in that increase and a relative stabilising of the price. The results achieved by this policy, including decreased usage of fuel, a lessening dependency on overseas supplies, greater exploration and development, and a greater use of alternative energy resources, today stand as evidence and testimony to the wisdom and correctness of the crude oil policy.

The National Country Party fully endorses this Bill, the six months extension and the lAC review, particularly if undertaken with a view to comparing the assistance given to manufacturing industries and the limited assistance available to rural industries. Statute Law Bill

17 November 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 2915

Australian Capital Territory Evidence (Temporary Provisions) Act should be included in legislation of this kind is wrong. In 1971 the Senate disallowed an evidence ordinance for the Australian Capital Territory. The Act was passed to provide evidence laws in respect of criminal trials for the Territory. I do not believe it to be consistent with what occurred in 1971 for the Act, which gives effect to the ordinance, to be able to be amended or repealed by another ordinance. Of course, ordinances are made by the Governor General in Council.
At present the matter of evidence in Federal and Territorial matters is being considered by the Law Reform Commission. This legislation will allow the evidence law applicable in the Australian Capital Territory to be changed without any reference to the Parliament. If the Government wishes to give an undertaking that the ordinance to be passed in the future must be approved by the House of Assembly, we will find that acceptable. However, if, as at present, the recommendations of the Assembly are basically to be ignored we will insist on parliamentary approval of a new evidence law for the Australian Capital Territory. The only power that the Houses of Parliament have in relation to ordinances is the power of disallowance, not the power of amendment. If the Parliament were to disallow an ordinance providing for a new law of evidence for the Australian Capital Territory, that would lead us into precisely the same position as we were in in 1971; that is, there would be a vacuum in the law. That is a position in which the Parliament should not be placed.
Probably the major amendment contained in the legislation is that proposed to the Commonwealth Electoral Act. On 7 May the Minister for Immigration and Ethnic Affairs (Mr Macphee) announced that in future the Government would treat citizenship as the appropriate basis for voting rights. As the honourable member for Port Adelaide (Mr Young) pointed out, one must have residency in Australia for three years before one can apply for citizenship. We certainly do not believe that British subject ought to be given special rights. However, some consideration ought to be given to reducing the qualifying period for citizenship to, say, 12 months. As the honourable member for Port Adelaide pointed out also, the term 'British subject' appears in over 70 Acts of Parliament, quite apart from the Commonwealth Electoral Act. If a provison such as this, which we

welcome, is to be included in the Statue Law Revision Bill, which we find dubious, the opportunity ought to be taken in this legislation to remove the discrimination which continues to exist in other pieces of Commonwealth legislation.
We have no objection to the transfer of jurisdiction in bankruptcy, to the proposed amendments to the Designs Act or the proposed amendments to the Patents Act. The proposed amendments to the Petroleum Products Pricing Act, which was passed earlier this year, are interesting in that an earlier statutes revision Act has been misnamed, even though that Act was passed only this year. The amendment referred to the Commonwealth Functions (Statutes Revision) Act, whereas it should have referred to the Commonwealth Functions (Statutes Review) Act. It is perhaps a little ironic that statutes revision legislation should have to correct earlier statutes revision legislation. We hope that in future all statutes revision Bills carry the same title as the present Bill and that if more than one such Bill is introduced in a particular year those Bills simply be numbered, as was the case with the tax Bills-for example, tax Bills (Nos 1 to 9). If that is not done the titles of amending Bills will be extremely confusing and it will become very difficult to locate the revising Acts.
The proposed amendments to the Trade Union Training Authority Act simply recognise the fact that the Council of Australian Government Employee Organisations is no longer in existence, it having merged with the Australian Council of Trade Unions. The representation on the National Labour Consultative Council previously given to CAGEO will now be taken up by the ACTU. The Trades and Labour Council representation on the State councils for union training is to be correspondingly increased by one. Another proposed amendment of substance is that to the Aboriginal Councils and Associations Act 1976. Whilst that is a piece of legislation passed by the Fraser Government, I should point out that it is a piece of Australian Labor Party legislation which was introduced in the Parliament in 1975 and which would have been passed were it not for the Governor-General's intervention. The proposed amendment will allow the bodies incorporated under the Act to dispose of their assets to non-Aporiginal bodies or individuals. However, ministerial approval is still required. I deem that a little paternalistic.
While the amendments in themselves are welcome in that they improve the present position, ministerial approval is still required. Why Aboriginal councils and associations cannot deal with their own assets in the same way as anybody else I

2916 REPRESENTATIVES 17 November 1981

Statute Law Bill

am at a loss to understand. The reason for land tenure being under what is called an inalienable freehold is not because there is distrust of what land councils or land trusts might do with the land but simply because of the relationship between Aboriginals and land which is quite different from the European concept which simply regards land as an asset which is readily transferrable. Provided that an Aboriginal councilor association is not acting ultra vires or in some way vexatiously, there is no reason in principle why the disposal of assets or interests should not be dealt with in any way that the councilor association thinks fit.
We support the amendments to the Australian Capital Territory Supreme Court Act. These amendments will enable judges to make their oaths or affirmations of allegiance before superior court judges, that is, High Court or Federal Court, instead of the necessity for this to be done to the Governor-General. The amendment has a slightly republican tone which we thoroughly support. I do not know whether it will get the ringing support of the Minister for the Capital Territory (Mr Hodgman) in view of his comments in this place. The amendment to enable reserved judgments to be delivered by any member of the court is quite sensible and we support it. It places the Australian Capital Territory in its proper place as a supreme court. The same considerations apply to clause 16 of the Bill which enable the Attorney General rather than the appointee of the Governor-General to bring indictments for offences. Again there is a republican tone in this amendment. The other amendment to be made to the Australian Capital Supreme Court Act relates to the question of interest being payable on judgments for money. We support this amendment which is necessary particularly at this time of inflation.
The Opposition does not support the amendments to the Australian Overseas Projects Corporation Act. We find it difficult to understand why this legislation is being introduced in this fashion. The Government must be under the mistaken belief that the Corporation, established in 1978, is achieving some effective work. We cannot see that it has been able to do anything because of the strictures placed on it in the original Act. The Corporation cannot buy or sell goods either as a principle or an agent and it cannot compete with private enterprise. The Government continues to have a hang-up about the overseas trading corporation legislation which it rejected in the Senate when in Opposition. The question of an overseas trading corporation has nothing to do with communism or socialism. It is simply a fact of life that

there is a need for trading contracts to be established on a government-to-government basis. It is in the interests of private enterprise as a whole for this to be done.
However, the Government seems intent on protecting some sectional interests. The amendment states that the Corporation is not to take any actions which would result in a project being carried out where there is any government involvement. This is a ludicrous provision which will result in the loss of trading contracts. The Government seems to be unaware that we live in a mixed economy where the private and public sectors are intertwined. The building industry is in a slump due very largely to government cut-backs. Public sector involvement is vital to the Australian private industry particularly in the area of trade. While some individual private concerns may benefit from the amendments being moved Australian industry as a whole will not, and the Australian public certainly will not.
Subject to the reservations that I have stated we are prepared to support the Bill other than provisions of Part VII which we would ask the Government to delete. Those provisions relate to amendments to the Australian Overseas Projects Corporation. I make the point again that the original Bill really bound that project corporation hand and foot from the ability to operate in any competitive fashion. It could neither operate as a principal nor as an agent. If one looks at the profit and loss statement contained in the annual report for 1979-80, is it any wonder that we get into the position that all the report can show is a loss? We have a situation now where the accumulated losses to date are over $250,000. If one compares the Corporation's balance sheet with that of the Snowy Mountains Hydro-Electric Authority one will find the Snowy Mountains Authority operating at a profit. Why is this so? It is so because it can get out and compete both as a principal and agent. One has the ridiculous situation here that taxpayers' money is being given to an overseas projects corporation which is heavily in the red after two years and is unable to get out of it. How can it possibly start to earn money when it is not able to act either as principal or agent? If we look at the amendments now proposed it is prevented from even getting involved in any area where there is deemed to be an Australian authority. There is a specific prohibition in Part VII preventing the Corporation from getting involved in any public consortium or competing against any Australian private organisation or consortium. Proposed new section 8A provides that where the Corporation is aware that an Australian private organisation or an Australian private consortium

Statute Law Bill

17 November 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 2917

is able and willing to carry out an overseas development project the Corporation is unable to take part in the project. But there has to be some logic in what we are about. It is ridiculous to think that this Bill provides that if a department of the Australian Public Service or a department of a State Public Service is involved the Corporation cannot assist. I think that is where we of the Opposition find this provision to be ridiculous.
I make the point again that the Corporation is operating at a loss. We want to see the Corporation get into the act of making a profit. In so doing it will readily accelerate the opportunity for the private sector to do the work. Surely the task of the Corporation is to assist with and to find the work. But to have it in a situation in which it cannot ever be a principal or an agent for another organisation and it has to drop out because some organisation allegedly is in the private sector or comes within the definition of an Australian authority which virtually bars the Corporation by statute is quite counter-productive. I know the House wants to complete this Bill this evening and the Opposition will co-operate in that regard but we will need to go into Committee just to deal with Part VII of this legislation.
Question resolved in the affirmative.
Bill read a second time.
In Committee
The Bill.
Mr LIONEL BOWEN (Kingsford-Smith) (11.17)-1 refer to Part VII of the legislation which deals with amendments to the Australian Overseas Projects Corporation Act 1978. It can be seen from this Part that the Australian Overseas Projects Corporation cannot go near any organisation coming within the definition of 'Australian authority' in terms of being involved in development. The definition of'Australian authority' also includes a department of the Australian Public Service and a department of the Public Service of a State, but does not include the Corporation. Clause 24 seeks to insert proposed new section 8A, which reads:
Where the Corporation is aware that an Australian private organisation or an Australian private consortium is able and willing to carry out an overseas development project or a part of an overseas development project, the Corporation shall not take any action that would result in that project or that part of the project being carried out, in whole or in part, by an Australian authority or by an Australian public consortium.
There is a similar provision set out in proposed new section 8A (2). We of the Opposition find it quite ridiculous to think that the Corporation is so hamstrung. I have already made the point in my speech on the second reading that we do not think

the Corporation is doing as well as it should be doing. The taxpayers' money will not be wisely expended if the Corporation is to be hindered by legislative strictures. Accordingly, we oppose this amendment; that is, the whole of Part VII. We do noUntend to press it to a division because of the lateness of the hour.
Mr VINER (Stirling-Minister for Industrial Relations) (11.19)-1 would like to take the opportunity to respond briefly to the honourable member for Kingsford-Smith (Mr Lionel Bowen) who has argued the opposition to Part VII of this Bill. I think the role which the Government has set for the Australian Overseas Projects Corporation will be seen quite clearly in the explanation I am about to give. I think the public will understand that it is a proper role which carves out for the Corporation an ability to assist Australian organisations to compete for contracts for development projects overseas. The Government has deliberately limited the powers of the Corporation so that existing Australian commercial organisations cannot be displaced by the Corporation in their normal activities. The actual execution of projects is a function of the private sector and the supply of goods needed to fulfil a particular project would be the responsibility of the Australian firms associated with the project.
I believe, then, that the Committee will ~ee that the role of the Corporation is complementary to that of private enterprise and existing Australian commercial organisations. It is intended that the Corporation assist those organisations without intruding into their normal commercial activities and without seeking, as I have said, to displace existing Australian corporations in their normal activities.
Bill agreed to.
Bill reported without amendment; report adopted.

Mr DEPUTY SPEAKER (Mr Millar)-

Mr Dawkins-Tricks?

Mr MILTON~Yes, well named, as the expired.

honourable member for Fremantle has implied. Mr COLEMAN (Wentworth) (11.28)-As

Mr Brent Potts, partner in the broking firm the Standing Committee of Attorneys-General

Rivkin and Co., predicted a stock marketcrash if meets in Perth on Friday I want to draw the

Labor won. The Fraser Government encouraged House's attention to the unsatisfactory position in

Adjournment

17 November 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 2919

which private enterprises wishing to establish computerised legal information retrieval systems still find themselves. Honourable members will recall recent discussion in the media on the subject of difficulties public libraries and universities are having in complying with the amendments to the Copyright Act and in particular in relation to the copying of statutes, regulations and court judgments. No doubt honourable members have received numerous representations on the subject.
Less well publicised but equally important is government failure so far to allow electronic dissemination of primary legal source materialsstatutes, regulations and cases. It is this which is seriously impeding the establishment in Australia of the computerised legal information retrieval systems that I have referred to, whether it be by Computer Law Services Pty Ltd, Butterworths Pty Ltd or others. At the heart of both these problems is the Crown's copyright claim in respect of these legal source materials-cases, statutes and regulations.
Every person is presumed to know the law and this is indeed his duty. Such an onerous duty must surely carry with it the right of unimpeded access to primary sources of the laws. Governments should not have, or even claim, the right to impede the free dissemination of primary legal source materials, especially given their well known unsatisfactory record in providing up to date consolidated statutes and regulations. If private enterprise can produce a better access to the law then it must be in the public interest that it be able to do so in whatever media-electronic. or otherwise-it finds most convenient. It is for this reason that the Committee on Copyright and Law in the United Kingdom-the Whitford Committee-observed:
The statutes and Judgments, embodying as they do the law, should, on the face of it, be free of any possible copyright restrictions.
The House is no doubt aware that in this respect Australia is out of step with every other major common law countries. In the United States all statutes, regulations and judgments have, since the case of Wheedon v. Peters in 1832, been in the public domain and may be disseminated without government interference. In the United Kingdom the Crown, which has rights in relation to legislative materials but not judgments, does not assert those rights save in exceptional circumstances. In Australia the report of the Committee of Review of Commonwealth Functions has recommended a relaxation of copyright; yet to date there has been no indication of any relaxation of copyright with respect to computerised electronic media. The reasons for this are not clear. The responses to

questions before the Estimates Committee C inquiry into the Attorney-General's Department's estimates for 1981-82 were not entirely satisfactory and indicated that discussions between the States and the Commonwealth with respect to the establishment of computerised legal data retrieval systems were far from resolved, particularly as far as the Commonwealth's position is concerned. Furthermore, details of discussions of and reports to the Standing Committee of Attorneys-General have been given very little public airing.
I believe that this position cannot be justified either on the basis of the Government's commitment to private enterprise or on the basis of my earlier comments with respect to the important right of unimpeded public access to the law. We need to facilitate that access. With the increasing importance ofcomputerised electronic media as a means of rapid dissemination of the law in the major common law countries, it is in Australia's national interests for the Government to forgo what could now be claimed to be a monopoly and to allow capable organisations which wish to disseminate information through computerised electronic media to do so. Again, noting that the Standing Committee of Attorneys-General is to meet in Perth on Friday, I urge that steps in that direction be taken forthwith.
Moti.on (by Mr Hodges) agreed to:
That the question be now put.
