Answers to Questions

The following answers to questions were circulated:

Commitment to Youth Program
(Question No. 968)
Mr Hawke asked the Minister for Employment and Youth Affairs, upon notice, on 31 March 1981:
(I) How many young persons does the Government anticipate assisting under each of the 5 new initiatives of the Commitment to Youth Program announced by him on I October 1980.
(2) How many young persons have been assisted under each of these new initiatives as at 30 March 1981.
(3) How many extra Commonwealth Employment Service staff have been allocated nationally to cope with the increased workload involved in implementing these new initiatives and how have these staff been distributed in each State.
(4) What was the basis on which this staffing figure was calculated.
(5) Were relevant staff associations involved in the calculations of extra staff requirements; iF not, why not.
(6) Were these staff associations satisfied with the number of extra staff allocated to cope with the new initiatives; iF not, what was the basis For their dissatisFaction.
(7) What is the current $ituation concerning the Review to seek improved opportunities For youth jointly announced with the Minister For Education on 16 January 1981 and, in particular, the consideration of ceasing the availability of unemployment benefits For persons under 18 years of age.
Mr N. A. Brown-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I) and (2) Two of the five initiatives relate to the payment of a Transition Allowance. The allowance, equivalent to Unemployment Benefit plus $6 per week, is payable to young people aged 15-19 years who have been unemployed and away From Full-time education For at least four months in the last twelve months and who take part in approved full-time Commonwealth Funded School-to-Work Transition Program courses in T AFE. The same allowance is payable to all young people under the age of 25 years, unemployed and away from Full-time education For eight months out of the last twelve months and who participate in an approved Full-time T AFE course of up to twelve months' duration which is likely to lead to employment.
The Government anticipated that, in a Full year, up to 10,000 young people would be paid a Transition Allowance. The initiative came into effect on I February 1981 and by the end of March 1981, payment of an allowance had been approved to 2,722 young people.
A third initiative was the introduction of higher subsidies under the Special Youth Employment Training Program (SYETP). The new subsidies, set at $80 per week For the first 17 weeks and $55 per week For the remaining 17 weeks, are payable to employers who agree to take on and train young people aged 18-24 years who have been unemployed and away From full-time education For eight months out of the last twelve months.
The Government expected that up to 10,000 young people would receive subsidised on-the-job training under this initiative in a Full year. The new subsidies were also effective from I February 1981 and by the end of March 1981, payment had been approved in respect of 2,686 trainees.

A Fourth initiative involved a variation to the eligibility criteria for training under standard SYETP conditions. Eligi-
bility to such training has been extended to all young people who have completed a Full-time course in TAFE under the Commonwealth Funded School-to-Work Transition Program and who are in need of Further on-the-job training.

This variation in SYETP eligibility became effective at the end of November 1980 and to the end of March 1981, 63 young people had benefitted.

The fin;!1 initiative was the introduction of a special counselling and advice service within the CES. The Government intends that every young person under 25 years of age who has been continuously registered as unemployed for eight months will benefit From this initiative. ACOA bans on the collection of statistics in some States prevented the compilation of an accurate statistic For Australia.

(3) An extra 52 staff, allocated as follows:

(5) and (6) A meeting was held with the Administrative
and Clerical Officers' Association on 23 December 1980 prior to the introduction of the programme and at that meeting the Association was advised of the details of the programme, its
target and of the staffing arrangements which the Department proposed to make.
Subsequent to that meeting, the ACOA's Federal Execu-

recommendations. (7) The outcome of the Review was announced by the Min-

the age of 18 years.

Capital Territory Health Commission Grant to Family Planning Association
(Question No. 1721)
Mr Bungey asked the Minister for Health, upon notice, on 9 April 1981:

Family Planning Program-to assist with the provision of non-clinical services, i.e. education, training and information; and

Private Health Funds
(Question No. 2020)
Dr Blewett asked the Minister for Health, upon notice, on 5 May 1981:
(I) Further to the answer to question No. 377 (Hansard, 28 April 1981, page 1675) what are the individual organisations referred to in his reply to part (I) of that question.
(2) For each fund mentioned in part (I), what are the discriminating rules in each case.
(3) Has he considered using the powers of deregistration, to enforce compliance with this, and any other matter, such as repayment of moneys owing to Government or contributors, by the funds concerned.
Mr MacKellar-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(1) There are only two organisations, the Medical Benefits Fund of Australia Limited (M.B.F.)-operating in New

2924 REPRESENTATIVES 17 November 1981

Answers to Questions

South Wales, Queensland and Tasmania-and the Mutual Health Association (M.H.A.) South Australia, that have aspects of rules that are not completely acceptable.
(2) M.B.F. There are four unacceptable areas:
(i) a pre-existing exclusion on supplementary benefits may be applied for two years;
(ii) hospital benefits could be excluded where in the view of the organisation, hospitalisation is not 'essential';
(iii) supplementary benefits may not be payable to persons with Repatriation entitlements;
(iv) the rule referring to basic hospital benefits is incomplete in relation to benefits for outpatient services.
M.H.A. Two aspects are unacceptable:
(i) a pre-existing ailment exclusion and a waiting period of nine months for obstetric cases, in respect of basic benefits where a contributor falls more than two months in arrears on contributions;
(ii) a'pre-existing ailment exclusion could be applied to a new dependant of a contributor for supplementary benefits.
(3) Negotiations with these organisations are continuing.
Industrial Disputes and Accidents
(Question No. 2434)
Mr Hayden asked the Minister for Industrial Relations, upon notice, on 4 June 1981:
(I) What was the (a) number of workers involved, (b) number of working days lost and (c) estimated loss in wages, as a result of industrial disputes in each State and Territory, and in Australia as a whole, for each of the last 10 years.
(2) What was the (a) number of workers affected, (b) number of working days lost, (c) estimated loss in wages and

(d) compensation and other payments made to victims of, industrial accidents and injuries, and occupational diseases, in each State and Territory, and in Australia as a whole, for each of the last 10 years.
Mr Viner-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
Regarding information in respect of Part (1) the attached table sets out details of (a) number of workers involved (b) number of working days lost and (c) estimated loss in wages as a result of industrial disputes in each State, the Northern Territory, the Australian Capital Territory and Australia as a whole, for each of the last 10 years.
These statistics refer only to working days lost by workers directly involved in stoppages, and those indirectly involved at establishments where the stoppages occurred. They do not include time lost by workers indirectly affected at other establishments, nor the effects of bans and limitations.
Regarding information in respect of Part (2), (a), (b) and (c), uniform and comprehensive statistics of industrial accidents, injuries and occupational diseases for the past 10 years are not available. However, the Australian Bureau of Statistics is proceeding with initiatives to extend statistical coverage to include the Territories and Commonwealth Government employees and to standardise the existing statistics for the States. It is hoped then that reliable State, Territory and national statistics can be produced.
Calculations prepared for the Departments of Labour Advisory Committee on Industrial Accidents indicate that each year, there are at least 300 fatalities, and 300,000 disabling injuries (causing time off work of one day or more) and 1,000,000 weeks lost from work. These estimates do not include cases of occupational disease, or journey to or from work.
Complete information in respect of Part (2) (d) is not available.

Care and Use of Animals in Research
(QuestioJl No, 2455)
Mr Humphreys asked the Minister for Health, upon notice, on 4 June 1981:
(I) Has the National Health and Medical Research Council drawn up a Code of Practice in respect to the care and use of animals in research.
(2) If so, does this Code of Practice apply to (a) Government laboratories, (b) industry and (c) educational institutions.
(3) Does his Department carry out inspections of research laboratories to ensure compliance with the code; if so, how often are inspections carried out.
Mr MacKellar-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I) The National Health and Medical Research Council (NH&MRC) has, in conjunction with the Commonwealth Scientific and Industrial Research Organisation (CSIRO), drawn up a 'Code of practice for the care and use of animals in research in Australia'. It is an extensive document, covering aspects in relation to the manner in which experiments are conducted, the care and maintenance of animals and the training of laboratory personnel in the care of animals.
(2) The code of practice is a voluntary code and therefore, it is at the discretion of individual laboratories as to whether it is followed. However, it is a requirement that NHMRC grantees comply with the Code and compliance is generally monitored by the relevant Ethics Committee of the institution where the research is being conducted. In those institutions where there is no appropriate Ethics Committee, the grantee is required to abide by the Code of Practice, as a condition of the grant. Government laboratories such as the Commonwealth Serum Laboratory comply with the code which is administered through an Animal Experimentation Ethics Review Committee.
(3) My Department does not inspect research laboratories to monitor compliance with the code. It is the responsibility of State and Territory Departmental authorities to ensure the legislation is appropriately administered.
Job Training Programs ,
(Question No, 2504)
Mrs Darling asked the Minister for Employment and Youth Affairs, upon notice, on 9 June 1981:
(I) Is (a) cabinet-making excluded from the building category for which $1,000 rebates are made available and (b) panel beating excluded from the metal trades category for which $1,000 rebates are made available under his Department's job training programs; if so, why.

(2) Has his Department received complaints concerning these exclusions.
(3) Will he consider amending the conditions of the training programs to include these trades; if not, why not.
Mr N. A. Brown-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I) (a) and (b) Yes. As cabinet-making is a furniture trade and panel-beating is a vehicle trade, they are outside the metal, electrical and building trades to which the CRAFT $1,000 rebate applied in the period I December 1980-30 June 1981.
(2) Yes.
(3) No. The metal, electrical and building trades were identified and agreed by Commonwealth and State Ministers of Labour as the areas where the greatest shortfall of skilled tradesmen may apply as a result of demands from the major development projects. The Government thereFore gave priority to increase the supply of skilled tradesmen in these areas.

International Labour Organisation Conventions: Ratification

o Convention which has not yet received the required number of ratifications for entry into force.
- Convention revised by a subsequent Convention.
+ Convention no longer open to ratification as a result of the entry into force of a revising Convention.
X Conventions which have not received the required number of ratifications for entry into force and which are no longer open to ratification as a result of the entry into force of a revising Convention.
A Conventions which merely revise the final articles'.
R Conventions which Australia has ratified. P Conventions which are precluded from ratification by Australia as they are unsuited to Australian conditions.
I Conventions which are irrelevant or of limited relevance to Australia. U Conventions which are no longer seen as setting desirable objectives. NM Conventions which apply to non-metropolitan territories only.
C Conventions which are wholly within the competence of the Commonwealth.

Answers to Questions

Human Rights: International Labour Organisation Conventions
(Question No. 2948)
Mr Hayden asked the Minister for Industrial Relations, upon notice, on 8 September 1981:
(I) Does the International Labour Office list International Labour Organisation Conventions No. I 35-Workers' Representatives, 1971, No. 141-Rural Workers' Organisations, 1975 and No. 151-Labour Relations (Public Service), 1978 among the 10 conventions which deal with basic human rights.
(2) What have been the dates and form of the periodical consultations with the States which the Australian Government is obliged by Article 19 (7) (b) (ii) of the Constitution of the ILO to arrange with a view to promoting co-ordinated action to give effect to the provisions of such conventions.
(3) What attitude has each State expressed to the ratification of each convention.
Mr Viner-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I) There are 11 International Labour Organisation Conventions which the International Labour Office lists as basic human rights Conventions. They are:
Convention No. 11: Right of Association (Agriculture), 1921
Convention No. 87: Freedom of Assoc1ation and Protection of the Right to Organise, 1948
Convention No. 98: Right to Organise and Collective Bargaining, 1949
Convention No. 135: Workers' Representatives, 1971 Convention No. 141: Rural Workers' Organisations, 1975 Convention No. 151: Labour Relations (Public Service), 1978 Convention No. 29: Forced Labour, 1930 Convention No. 105: Abolition of Forced Labour, 1957 Convention No. 100: Equal Remuneration, 1951 Convention No. III: Discrimination (Employment and' pccupation),1958 Convention No. 156: Workers with Family Responsibilities, 1981
(2) Convention No. 135-Workers' Representatives, 1971: Apart from correspondence between the Commonwealth and State Departments of Labour, and a request in January 1972 by my Department for State Department reports on the extent to which law and practice in their respective jurisdictions complied with the Articles of the Convention, Convention No. 135 has been considered at Technical Officers' meetings in November 1973, May 1974, December 1974, July 1975, December 1975, February 1977 and August 1979, Departments of Labour Advisory Committee in August 1973, February 1974, September 1976, September 1978, the Conference of Commonwealth and State Labour Ministers in September 1976 and February 1979.
Convention No. 141-Rural Workers Organisations, 1975: Convention No. 141 applies particularly to developing countries where organisations representing rural workers have not been established or, if they have, are in the formative stages of development only. The approach adopted in the instrument does not lend itself to adaptation to the Australian legal frameworks and institutional arrangements in the labour area and ratification would not be possible without quite fundamental changes to the Australian industrial relations system. Nevertheless this Convention has been discussed at Technical Officers' meeting in 1971 and 1977 and the position will be reviewed from time to time.

Convention No. 151-Labour Relations (Public Service) 1978: In addition to correspondence between the Commonwealth and State Departments of Labour, State Departments of Labour were requested by my Department in 1978 to provide detailed information on the extent to which law and practice in their respective jurisdictions complied with the Articles of this Convention. My Department is still awaiting those detailed responses. Convention No. 151 has also been discussed specifically at Technical Officers' meetings in October 1978, August 1979 and August 1980 and generally at the Departments of Labour Advisory Committee in September 1978 and at the Conference of Commonwealth and State Labour Ministers in February 1979.
(3) No State has agreed to the ratification of either Convention No. 135 or Convention No. 141. Western Australia has agreed to the ratification of Convention No. 151.
Western Australian Department ofAgriculture
(Question No. 2971)
Mr Campbell asked the Minister representing the Minister for Aboriginal Affairs, upon notice, on 8 September 1981:
(I) Will the Minister provide a detailed breakdown of the funds allocated by the Department of Aboriginal Affairs to the Western Australian Department of Agriculture for (a) Ord River Station, (b) Kununurra Experimental Farm and (c) Fox River Station, during 1980-81.
(2) Have the funds been allocated for these same projects in 1981-82; if so, will the Minister provide a detailed breakdown of this allocation.
(3) How many Aboriginal people were trained under these projects in 1980-81 and over what individual periods were they trained.
(4) What did this training involve.
(5) Are any of these Aboriginal people now employed in the area in which they received training.
(6) Did the Department allocate funds for training programs on any Aboriginal-held pastoral leases in the Kimberley region of Western Australia during 1980-81; if so, which Aboriginal groups were funded and what was the level of funding involved.
(7) Are any Aboriginal-held pastoral leases in the Kimberley region of Western Australia to receive training funds in 1981-82; if so, what level of funding is involved in each instance; if not, why not.
Mr Viner-The Minister for Aboriginal Affairs has provided the following answer to the honourable member's question.
(1) and (2) The breakdown of allocations for projects in 1980-81 and proposed for 1981-82 are as follows:

Agriculture Department Ord River Station
Wages for up 9 Aboriginal trainees including oncosts
Workers Compensation Materials

(3) 47 persons were trained under these projects in 1980-81. Their periods of training are as follows:

In some cases training was not completed due to trainees making personal decisions to move on to another location.
(4) Training on these projects involved instruction on maintenance, operation and repair of farm machinery, recognition of warning signs, preparation of virgin soil for production, irrigation, crop rotation and use of fertilisers and the recognition of plant diseases and their subsequent treatment.
(5) An extensive follow-up survey of all persons trained has not been undertaken. However, a sample indicates that approximately 25 per cent of the trainees are now employed in areas relevant to those in which they received instruction. Three former trainees are now members of the permanent Department of Agriculture staff.
(6) The Department of Aboriginal Affairs provides funds for the employment of a specialist pastoral adviser for Aboriginal communities in the Kimberleys. Part of his role is to assist in training Aboriginal workers. The allocation for this position in 1980-81 was $43,600 and approved funding for 1981-82 is $58,300.
(7) Two Aboriginal-held pastoral leases receive allocations from my Department under the Western Australian Region's Grant-in-Aid Training program. Kadjina Community Inc. which operates Millijidee station has an approved allocation of $2,700 and the Bidyadanga Community which operates Frazier Downs will receive $7,400 in 1981-82 for training. In addition, the Ngangganawili Community has a special lease over the agricultural land and $17,500 has been approved under the training program for 1981-82.
The Aboriginal Development Commission has recently approved a grant of $116,000 for the establishment of a Kimberley and Pilbara Advisory Service. The role of this agency will be to provide advisers to Aboriginal-held pastoral operations with a view to encouraging and training Aboriginal workers in the interests of the successful self-management of enterprises.
South Australian Airfields Committee
(Question No. 3008)
Mr Jacobi asked the Minister for Transport, upon notice, on 9 September 1981:
(I) Further to his answer to Question No. 71 (Hansard,24 February 1981, page 55) has the South Australian Airfields Committee, which he expected to commence activities early in 1981, been established.

(2) If so (a) who are the members of the Committee and which organisations do they represent and (b) on what dates has the Committee met.
(3) Hit has not yet met, what is the reason.
(4) Why did he state that an early resolution of the question of a new airport site is not expected.
(5) Can he now say when the land will be purchased; if not, why not.
(6) Did a forward planning document prepared for him propose the purchase of land near Two Wells for $55m, as reported in the Adelaide Advertiser earlier in 1981; if so has any sum been set aside for this purpose in 1981/82.
Mr Hunt-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I) Yes.
(2) and (3) (a) The Committee comprises representatives of the Commonwealth (Department of Transport) and the State (Departments of Transport, Environment and Planning and Local Government).
(b) The Committee met on 13 July 1981 and 22 September 1981.
(4) Resolution of the question of a new airport site will require extensive work by Commonwealth and State Departments under the guidance of the South Australian State Airfields Committee. The magnitude and complexity of the task, and the limited planning resources available, preclude this work from being completed at an early date.
(5) It will not be possible to purchase land for a new airport until the completion of the State airfields Committee's task of identifying the site required.
(6) No. No funds for this purpose have been set aside in 1981-82.
Tenosynovitis: Department ofIndustry and Commerce
(Question No. 3012)
Mr Les McMahon asked the Minister for Industry and Commerce, upon notice, on 9 September 1981:
(I) Have any cases of tenosynovitis been reported to date, in the Department or statutory authorities under the Minister's jurisdiction.
(2) If so, will the Minister state (a) how many cases have been reported, (b) in what types of work the affected persons were engaged, (c) of the cases reported how many were in respect of (i) female and (ii) male employees and (d) what age brackets appear to be most affected, in the Department and each statutory authority.
(3) Will the Minister provide similar information in respect of carpal tunnel syndrome.
Sir Phillip Lynch-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I) Yes.
(2) (a) 69 cases of tenosynovitis.
(b) Repetitive work mostly, such as that performed by operators, process workers, production machinists and typists, but classifications include aircraft mechanic, painter, clerk, storeman, carpenter, crane driver, floorman, inspection viewer and laboratory assistant.
(c) (i) 39 females; (ii) 30 males.
(d) From 17 years to 53 years.

2934 REPRESENTATIVES 17 November 1981

Answers to Questions

(3) (a) 22 cases of carpal tunnel syndrom'e.
(b) Mostly involving machinists, process workers or operators, but classifications include general duties hand, spray painter, toolmaker and furnaceman.
(c) (i) 16 females; (ii) 6 males.
(d) from 30 to 56 years.
Tenosynovitis: Department of Primary Industry
(Question No. 3016)
Mr Les McMahon asked the Minister for Primary Industry upon notice, on 9 September, 1981:
(I) Have any cases of tenosynovitis been reported to date, in the Department or statutory authorities under the Minister's jurisdiction.
(2) If so, will the Minister state (a) how many cases have been reported, (b) in what types of work the affected persons were engaged, (c) of the cases reported how many were in respect of (i) female and (ii) male employees and (d) what age brackets appear to be most affected, in the Department and each statutory authority.
(3) Will the Minister provide similiar information in respect of carpal tunnel.
Mr Nixon-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I) Yes-in the Department of Primary Industry and in the Australian Wool Testing Authority.
(2) (a) six cases since July 1980.
(b) four persons were employed as meat inspectors and two as laboratory assistants.
(c) (i) two; (ii) four.
(d) the meat inspectors involved were 25, 37,44 and 51 years of age and the laboratory assistants were 27 and 48 years old.
(3) Of the reported cases of Tenosynovitis, two involved a carpal tunnel injury. The persons involved were a 44 year old male meat inspector and a 27 year old female laboratory as-' sistant. There was a further case of carpal tunnel injury in the Australian Wool Testing Authority involving a 43 year old female laboratory assistant.
Australian Gem Industry
(Question No. 3109)
Mr Jacobi asked the Minister for Trade and Resources, upon notice, on 15 September 1981:
(I) Is it a fact that the Australian Minerals and Energy Council and the Commonwealth/State Industry Ministers' Conference established a Joint Study Group to inquire into the Australian gem industry.
(2) Is it also a fact that its report, dated June 1980, at paragraph 12, contained 10 recommendations to encourage the further processing ofgemstones within Australia.
(3) Has the Government taken action to implement these recommendations; if so, when; if not, why not.
Mr Anthony-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I) The Commonwealth-State Joint Study Group on Raw Materials Processing was established in 1978 following agreement between the Commonwealth, the States and the Northern Territory. The Joint Study Group reports to the Australian Minerals al'1 Energy Council and the Commonwealth/State Industry Ministers' Conference. A

number of studies on a range of raw materials processing industries have been undertaken by the Study Group and one of the published reports concerns the potential for expansion of the Australian Gemstone Processing Industry.
(2) Yes.
(3) The report was presented to the Australian Minerals and Energy Council in April 1981 and to the Commonwealth State Industry Ministers' Conference in August 1981. The recommendations cover responsibilities of both the Commonwealth and States and various authorities and Departments at both Commonwealth and State levels which have responsibility for matters referred to in the recommendations have been approached seeking their reaction to the relevant recommendations. On receipt of advice from these Departments and authorities, the Joint Study Group will be reporting to Government canvassing possible action on these recommendations.
Australian Gift Cheques: Business Practice
(Question No. 3123)
Mr Humphreys asked the Minister for Business and Consumer Affairs, upon notice, on 16 September 1981:
(I) Has the Minister's attention been drawn to the business practice of the firm known as Australian Gift Cheques; if so, is he able to say whether a book of gift cheques sold by that firm cost $29.95, but purports to offer goods and services to the value of $826.
(2) Is he satisfied that the business practices of the company are completely in accord with section 52 of the Trade Practices Act.
Mr Moore-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I) My attention has been drawn to the activities of the firm known as Lonsdale Promotions Pty Ltd, trading as Australian Gift Cheques, through a complaint lodged with the Trade Practices Commission on behalf of a garage proprietor. The complaint concerned a contract between Australian Gift Cheques and the garage proprietor whereby the proprietor was to provide services upon receipt of coupons from promotional booklets distributed by Australian Gift Cheques. I have no knowledge of the activities of the firm beyond what has been revealed by this complaint, and in particular I am unaware of the cost of the booklets and the claimed total value of any goods and services mentioned therein.
(2) With respect to the complaint mentioned in (I), I am advised the Commission was not able to establish that a breach of the Trade Practices Act had occurred. No other complaints concerning Australian Gift Cheques or Lonsdale Promotions Pty Ltd had been made to the Commission as at 26 October 1981, and I have no information that the business practices of these enterprises involve any contravention of section 52 of the Trade Practices Act.
Brisbane Airport: Environmental Impact Statement
(Question No. 3128)
Mr Humphreys asked the Minister for Transport, upon notice, on 16 September 1981:
Is his Department's Draft Environmental Impact Statement of March 1974 on Brisbane's Airport Redevelopment methodologically unsound in that it failed to:
(a) justify the need for the project, for example, by suggesting that the airport was expected to reduce the pressure of air traffic through Sydney.

Answers to Questions

17 November 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 2935

(b) establish the benefits that would accrue to the Australian or local community should the project go ahead and
(c) take account of planning parameters in regard to passenger and freight traffic to and from the airport.
Mr Hunt-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
A Draft Environmental Impact Statement (EIS), covering the airport development works, was prepared by the Department of Transport in 1978 and released for public comment in accordance with the Commonwealth's Environmental Protection Administrative Procedures. The final EIS was published in February 1979 following review of public comments on the draft EIS.
In relation to the particular issues which the honourable member has raised, the answers are:
(a) No.
(b) No.
(c) No.
By way of amplification, the EIS outlined the need for the project, and benefits to the local community. Exhaustive descriptions of these elements were not included in the EIS as such information had been provided to the community through various announcements in the news media and other public forums over a long period of time since 1971.
The EIS clearly indicated that in relation to access proposals, State Government environmental assessment procedures would apply.
Sydney International Airport: Immigration Clearance Booths
(Question No. 3130)
Mr Humphreys asked the Minister for Business and Consumer Affairs, upon notice, on 16 September 1981:
(I) Is it a fact that 12 Immigration Clearance Booths at Sydney's International Airport were unattended on 12 July 1981, causing inbound passengers to queue for over 2 hours, and several passengers to miss onward connecting flights.
(2) If so, why have additional staff not been assigned to Clearance Booths to spare travellers and tourists from protracted delays.
Mr Moore-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I) and (2) On 12 July 1981 eighteen primary officers were rostered for duty to cover arriving as well as departing passengers. To spare travellers and tourists from protracted delays a further eight officers were rostered, increasing the primary officer working strength to twenty six. Sixteen officers were deployed to process arriving passengers and crew and ten officers to process departing passengers and crew.
Between 9.30 am and 10.15 am, 1,640 passengers disembarked from 7 aircraft, 5 of which were off-schedule by periods of between 14 and 50 minutes. Passengers from these aircraft did wait for periods ranging up to 115 minutes to be cleared through the Customs/Immigration primary check.
Passenger overloads at the inwards primary line are caused largely by circumstances over which the Department has no control, such as the arrival of off-schedule aircraft which is what occurred on 12 July. I have been assured that all officers on that morning were utilised to the maximum advantage.
I have been advised that the Department has received no reports of passengers missing connecting flights.

Qantas Airways Ltd: Facilities in Mauritius
(Question No. 3136)
Mr Morris asked the Minister for Transport, upon notice, on 16 September 1981:
(I) What funds have been expended by Qantas Airways Ltd in the acquisition or installation of facilities for airline purposes in Mauritius in the last 2 years.
(2) What was the date of the expenditure and what'use has been made of the facilities since the expenditure was incurred.
(3) What facilities were utilised by Qantas Airways Ltd in Mauritius during the provision of services to South Africa abandoned in recent years, what was the cost of those facilities and when were the facilities disposed of by Qantas.
(4) What proceeds were received by Qantas from the disposal of the facilities in Mauritius.
Mr Hunt-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I) Nil.
(2) Not relevant.
(3) Qantas ceased operations to Mauritius in September 1977. The company shared a rented facilities building with Air Mauritius. Permanently stationed staff resided in rented houses. Some of the aircraft handling equipment owned by Qantas in Mauritius was returned to Sydney, but the following items were sold locally between 19 October 1977 and 8 February 1978: water replenishing unit; toilet unit; aircraft servicing van; aircraft servicing stand; Douglas tractor; Carrier air conditioner and two motor vehicles. The initial cost of the assets sold was $A40,642 and at the time of disposal they had a net value of $A8,940.
(4) Proceeds from sale of aircraft handling equipment in Mauritius was $27,454.

Secondary School Libraries
(Question No. 3153)
Dr Blewett asked the Minister for Education, upon notice, on 16 September 1981:
I. What extra costs will be inciJrred by secondary schools to expand their libraries to cover the increased demand for books and journals as a result of increasing restrictions on the use of photocopying facilities by secondary teachers requiring sufficient duplicates for class course material.
2. Does the Government intend to provide financial support to those schools lacking the finance to provide the necessary extra books and journals.
3. Have there been any discussions with State Education Ministers or Departments regarding the effects on standards of education resulting from restrictions to photocopying; if so,

what was the outcome of the discussions; if not, will there be discussions.
Mr Fife-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
I. It is not possible at this stage to estimate what extra costs, if any, schools will incur in expanding their libraries to meet 'any needs arising from the operation of the Copyright Amendment Act 1980. Such costs will depend in part on determinations to be made by the Copyright Tribunal in regard to the rate of royalty payable to copyright owners for multiple copying of their work.
The Act does not require schools to stop photocopying.
a What it does require is that schools should examine more
closely the amount they propose to mUltiple copy from particular work. Broadly speaking, a school may multiple copy up to I per cent of a work free of payment to the copyright owner. Up to 10 per cent may be mUltiple copied if the school is prepared to pay a royalty for the pages copied. When multiple copies of more than 10 per cent of a work are required, the school is expected to buy rather than copy, if the work is commercially available as a separate publication.
2. School funding is basically a matter for the State Governments and other bodies which operate schools. If, in due course, such bodies choose to document cost increases arising from the changes to copyright law, in their routine submissions to the Schools Commission, the Government will be in a position to decide whether or not those costs should

Answers to Questions

17 November 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 2937

influence the level of funds to be provided for schools by the Commonwealth.
3. Proposed and actual changes to copyright law have been regularly discussed over a period of several years by the Commonwealth and State Ministers for Education, and by officers of their Departments. Some outcomes of the discussions have been:
(a) the establishment, in 1978, of an Australian Educational Council working partly on copyright law;
(b) representations to the Attorney-General's Department to ensure that the needs of education were met as far as possible when the new legislation was being. prepared;
(c) co-operation among the Education Departments in the task of preparing teachers and administrators for the new legislation; and
(d) -continuing discussions with the Attorney-General's Department on the needs of schools in regard to their use of audio visual materials, which in an area where further amendments to copyright law are envisaged.
A report on the current situation will be presented to the next meeting of the Australian Education Council which is to be held from 19 to 23 October.

Special Youth Education Program
(Question No. 3177)

(2) What form have the abuses of the program taken.
(3) Have any of the abuses resulted in legal action being taken against employers.
Mr N. A. Brown-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I) The South Australian office of my Department has records of 16 alleged cases of employer abuse on file in respect of the Special Youth Employment Training Program (SYETP).
(2) Of these, 15 relate to various breaches of the training agreement and in the other case the employer appeared to be attempting to use the program as a means of obtaining a continuous supply of subsidised labour.
(3) No legal action was taken in any of these cases. However, rriy Department has refused to accept any further training vacancies from 7 employers, and terminated the training subsidy in another 7 cases. In the case of the remaining 2 employers acceptance by the CES of training vacancies was suspended until the employers agreed to abide by the provisions of the program including the training agreement.

Australian Tobacco Leaf Exports
(Question No. 3335)
Mr Campbell asked the Minister for Trade and Resources, upon notice, on 15 October 1981:
(I) Has his Department obtained a legal opinion on whether arrangements for the export of Australian tobacco leaf by firms which import manufactured tobacco breach the General Agreement on Tariffs and Trade provisions on export subsidies.
(2) Has the United States of America or any other country protested about those arrangements.

2940 REPRESENTATIVES 17 November 1981

Answers to Questions

Mr Anthony-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I) On 26 March 1981 the Government announced its decision to provide a duty concession of 10 per cent ad valorem on one kilogram of manufactured tobacco imported for each kilogram (greenweight) of Australian leaf purchased. The concession is available contingent upon importers making specified purchases of Australian leaf.
The Department of Trade and Resources, under the direction of the Minister for Trade and Resources, has primary responsibility for Australia's participation in the General Agreement on Tariffs and Trade (GATT). This includes the negotiation of any agreements or understandings in the GATT, the handling of any representations by other GATT contracting parties on any Australian measures or practice which affects their rights under the GATT or under any agreement administered under the auspices of the GATT, and advice to the Government on the GATT implications of proposed courses of action.
Normally, any GATT contracting party, jUdging a practice of another contracting party to be harmful to its interests and not in conformity with the GATT, raises the matter under formal GATT procedures. In the case of tobacco, no such action has been initiated by any contracting party.
The only grounds for questioning Australia's action would be in terms of the GATT export subsidy provisions which, as far as agricultural products are concerned and as Australia itself is well aware, are not especially onerous. It is, in any case, widely accepted within GATT that concessional import duties, whether or not applied to agricultural products, are not regarded as subsidies. Accordingly, it is not surprising that Australia's actions on tobacco have not been questioned in GATT.
In terms of GATT practice, it was neither appropriate nor necessary for the Department of Trade and Resources to seek a further opinion concerning the consistency of the concession on manufactured tobacco with GATT provisions on export subsidies.
(2) Bilateral representations were, in fact, received from the United States concerning Australia's actions on tobacco shortly after they were announced. However, these representations were postulated with insufficient precision to permit a specific response. Since going back to the United States they have not pursued the matter further.
No other country has raised the matter.

(Question No. 3336)

(I) What quantities and grades of Australian tobacco leaf were exported between March and September 1981.

Australian Dairy Corporation
(Question No. 3396)
Mr Kerin asked the Minister for Trade and Resources, upon notice, on 22 October 1981:
(I) With reference to the Report by the Senate Standing Committee on Finance and Government Operations on the Australian Dairy Corporation and its Asian Subsidiaries presented on 17 September 1981, when did he become aware of the alleged breach of the General Agreement on Tariffs and Trade arrangements.
(2) Did he or his Department ever question the legality of the original agreement negotiated by Mr McCartney of the Department of Primary Industry.
(3) If so, was he satisfied that the contract was legal and could not have been successfully challenged in court.
(4) If neither he nor his Department checked the legality of the contract, what was the reason for not doing so.
Mr Anthony-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I) I first became aware of an alleged breach of the GATT Arrangement Concerning Certain Dairy Products as a result of a report published in the Australian Financial Review in September 1977. At that time, advice was provided to my Department that when the Australian Dairy Corporation became aware of the possible breach, the Corporation took steps to terminate it.
As explained to the House on 22 September 1981, my Department was -epresented at a meeting with the then Chairman of the Australian Dairy Corporation on 13 October 1976 who stated that when skim milk powder prices reached the minimum specified under the GATT Arrangement, the Corporation had invoked the majeure clause in the contract in order to discontinue sales at prices which would have been inconsistent with the Arrangement.
(2), (3) and (4) The original agreement of December 1974 for the sale of skim milk powder to the Philippines was never referred to my Department and there would be no reason for the agreement as such to be so referred. At no time was Mr McCartney an officer of the Department of Primary Industry.
Meat Exports
Mr Nixon-On 9 September 1981 (Hansard, page 1058) Mr Wallis, the honourable member for Grey, asked a question without notice concerning the number of allegations of improprieties or breaches in meat inspection in the meat export industry which have been investigated by the Australian Federal Police and the results of these investigations. I undertook to provide a detailed list of the allegations that have been referred to the police over the years.
Since the Government's decision to establish a royal commission to investigate, inter alia, allegations of malpractice made over the past ten years, my Department has been identifying all relevant papers. Meat Species Testing Program
Mr Nixon-On 13 October 1981 (Hansard, page 1863) Dr Everingham asked the following question without notice:
(I) Can the Minister for Primary Industry inform the house of any results of the meat species testing program, including firstly, the number of firms and individuals detected substituting buffalo for other meats and secondly, the identities of fi rms or individuals involved in meat substitution and/or the use of adulterated meat for domestic consumption, export or both?
(2) Finally, can he say whether any of the adulterated meat was buffalo and whether some of this meat has been exported?
The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I) (a) To 13 October it is estimated that in excess of 12,500 species tests had been carried out as part of the testing program for export meat.
As the Commonwealth only has responsibility for meat produced for export, only export meat has been incl uded in the species testing program established by the Department of Primary Industry.
(b) No instances of substitution of export beef with buffalo meat have been detected by the monitoring program.
(c) Premises which have been deregistered as a consequence of the detection of substituted meat, and the firm in whose name the premises were registered, are as follows-
571 Victoria Street, Abbotsford, Victoria, registered in the name of Jason Meats Pty Ltd
leased portion of Protean Enterprises Pty Ltd, Barkly Avenue, Richmond, Victoria, registered in the name of Profreeze
84 Bastings Street, Northcote, Melbourne, Victoria, registered in the name of Steiger's Meat Supply (Aust) Pty Ltd.
(2) The Department of Primary Industry is unaware of any substitution of export beef with buffalo meat or of any suggestion by overseas authorities that this may have occurred.
Capital Territory Health Commission Grant to Calvary Hospital
(Question No. 1719)
Mr Bungey asked the Minister for Health, upon notice, on 9 April 1981:

(I) How was the 1979-80 grant of $6,439,339 by the Capital Territory Health Commission to the Calvary Hospital determined.
(2) In 1979-80 how many patients of each classification were admitted to Calvary Hospital and what was their average stay in the hospital.
Mr MacKellar-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I) The 1979-80 grant to Calvary Hospital Australian Capital Territory incorporated was determined according to an agreement between the Corporation of the Little Company of Mary, the Commonwealth of Australia, Calvary Hospital Australian Capital Territory Incorporated and the Capital Territory Health Commission. The agreement provides for the Commission to pay to Calvary Hospital a grant for operating expenses equal to the difference between income and expenditure for the financial year concerned based on a submission to the Commission of estimates of income and expenditure for the coming year. Calvary Hospital is also required to provide information and statistics relating to their estimates as required by the Commission. Calvary Hospital is entitled to participate in discussions on the funding and budget estimates for all public hospitals in the Australian Capital Territory. The Commission has the right to vary, after consultation witli the hospital, the approved budget at any time during the year.
(2) In 1979-80, 2507 patients were admitted to acute beds in Calvary Hospital. These admissions represented 849 hospital service patients with an average length of stay of 8.0 days and 1,658 private and compensation patients with an average length of stay of 8.3 days.

Aluminium Smelter Proposals, Queensland
(Question No. 2684)
Dr Everingham asked the Minister representing the Minister for National Development and Energy, upon notice, on 18 August 1981:
(1) Have feasibility studies for Woongarra Shire and Bundaberg City Council, Queensland, concerning aluminium smelter proposals been disclosed to governments but witheld from the public; ifso, why.
(2) Is the Minister able to say whether the Queensland Coordinator General intends to disclose his recommendations to the Australian Government; if so, will these recommendations be made public; if not, why not.
(3) Will non-permanency rules be enforced in caravan parks housing smelter workers.
(4) Have any of the environmental reports referred to Walkerva1e School; ifso, will these references by made public.
(5) Are names of nominee land purchasers and developers published or disclosed to each level of government while objectors are required to present their cases against aspects of development in public documents to local authorities who then present these to the Co-ordinator General for determination without disclosure of his reasons to the objectors, discussion with them or checking of his decisions by the Australian Government.

Answers to Questions

17 November 1981 REPRESENTATlVES 2945

(6) Will Wide Bay-Burnett Electricity Board buy power from the State grid at about 3 cents per kilowatt hour (kWh), representing 1.068 cents for coal and 1.932 cents for capital costs, then sell to consumers at 6.48 cents/kWh to cover subtransmission costs, while Alcan pays 1.8 cents/kWh.
(7) Is the Minister able to say whether the aluminium industry paid (a) 3 cents in the United States of America, (b) 4 cents in Europe and (c) 8 cents in Japan per kWh in 1978.
(8) At the marginal export production cost of coal, does it cost $459 to smelt one tonne of aluminium.
(9) Are returns to Queensland from Alcan estimated at $252 per tonne.
(10) Is the Minister able to say whether there is any substance to the report in the Brisbane Courier Mail of 17 March 1981 that the Treasurer opposed State governments selling electricity below cost to attract smelter development.
(II) Will Alcan be required to reveal electricity prices to the (a) Commonwealth Government and (b) Parliament.
(12) Is the Minister able to say whether the relaxation of the demands of the Queensland Premier for health funds in May 1981 occurred in the context of relaxation of Federal monitoring of development projects.
Mr Anthony-The Minister for National Development and Energy has provided the following answer to the honourable member's question:
Parts (I), (2), (3), (5), (6), (8) and (9) concern matters which come within the responsibility of the Queensland Government and various of its agencies.
(4) A draft Environmental Impact Statement was prepared and was available to the public for examination. I understand that the draft statement did not refer to the Walkervale School.
(7) There is no uniform price for electricity supplied to smelters in particular countries. It is also generally the practice for the price of electricity to smelters to be commercially negotiated and it is therefore not possible to determine the range of applicable prices. An industry spokesman did refer in 1979 to prices of the order cited. However, the precise connotation was not clear from the text. It would appear that the estimates referred to the incremental costs of electricity from new capacity and not to the average price of electricity to the then existing smelters which could well have been significantly lower.
(10) A search of the Courier Mail of 17 March 1981 revealed no reference to any views of the Treasurer on the appropriate selling price for electricity. There was, however, a report in Courier Mail of 7 March 1981 apparently reporting on a submission by the Treasury Department to the Senate Standing Committee on National Resources: the Development of the Bauxite, Alumina and Aluminium Industries.
(II) Major development projects typically, require some Commonwealth Government approvals. The approval process, particularly in relation to foreign investment guidelines, can involve provision, on a confidential basis, of a wide range of data relevant to the assessment of national benefits. Nevertheless, the determination of electricity prices is a responsibility of the State Government.
(12) There has been no relaxation of Commonwealth monitoring of development projects.
Moreton Bay: Site Reclamation Procedures
(Question 2728)
Mr Humphreys asked the Minister for Home Affairs and Environment, upon notice, on 19 August 1981:

Has his attention been drawn to the site reclamation procedures of hydraulically pumping sand from Moreton Bay to accommodate the 02/20 runway in conjunction with the redevelopment of Brisbane's International Airport; if so, have any investigations been carried out into (a) possible adverse ecological consequences to micro-organisms in Moreton Bay fishing grounds which may produce ciguatoxin and hence conta'minate locally caught fish and (b) the effects of dredging on adjacent beaches,
Mr Wilson-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
Yes, my attention has been drawn to the reclamation procedures.
With regard to investigations, my Department has been advised by the Department of Housing and Construction as follows:
(a) Expert advice provided since 1972 has never identified ciguatoxin. as a potential problem and consequently detailed investigations have not been undertaken. Ciguatoxin is associated with the destruction of coral substrate and is generally restricted to tropical waters. Dredging in Moreton Bay for the re-development of Brisbane International Airport is being conducted at Middle Banks, which is an area devoid of coral and not within tropical waters. Thus ciguatoxin is not likely to result from Middle Banks dredging,
(b) Yes. Investigations of the effect of dredging at Middle Banks on the beaches of Moreton Island by specialist advisers concluded that the effects will be be negligible, on the currently unstable Moreton Island beaches. Records existing since the mid ninteenth century confirm the unstable nature of these beaches. Direct monitoring of the western Moreton Island beaches commenced prior to dredging to identify any -changes.

Foreign Language Films
(Question No. 2823)
Mr Jull asked the Minister representing the Attorney-General, upon notice, on 25 August 1981:
(I) What provision is made by the Film Censorship Board for the classification of foreign language films.
(2) How many members of the board, or employees of the board, speak a foreign language, so that classification of imported feature films can be undertaken.
(3) Are provisions made to employ freelance interpreters to undertake classification of feature films.

2946 REPRESENTATIVES 17 November 1981

Answers to Questions

Mr N. A. Brown-The answers to the honourable member's questions are as follows:
(I) The registration and classification of foreign-language films can generally be based on synopses, sub-titles, dubbed dialogue and visual content or some combination of those elements.
(2) All nine members of the Film Censorship Board have a working knowledge of at least one foreign language encompassing French, Italian and Spanish. Two Deputy Censors are fluent in German and Serbo-Croatian respectively.
(3) Normal provisions exist for the employment of Government and freelance interpreters should this be considered necessary.
Repatriation Review Tribunal
(Question No. 2936)
Mr Les Johnson asked the Minister representing the Minister for Veterans' Affairs, upon notice, on 27 August 1981:
(I) How many cases to be determined by the Repatriation Review Tribunal have been waiting for review for (a) more than and (b) less than 2 ye&rs.
(2) How many formal representations has the Minister received in the last 10 months expressing concern with the delay in the repatriation determining system.
(3) What action does the Government propose to take to overcome these problems.
Mr Thomson-The Minister for Veterans' Affairs has provided the following answer to the honourable member's question:
(I) On I September 1981 there were 7,310 applications awaiting decisions by the Repatriation Review Tribunal. Of this total 1,116 were lodged before I September 1979 and 6,194 were lodged on, or after, I September 1979.
(2) Approximately 50 formal representations have been received during the last 10 months expressing concern about delays in the Repatriation determining system.
(3) The Government is fully aware of the problems being encountered with delays in the repatriation determining system and changes have been made to improve the position. Three additional Repatriation Boards have been established with the object of lessening the backlog in cases awaiting determination. The number of Repatriation Commission delegates has been increased and approval has been sought for further delegates to be appointed. In addition, the Repatriation Act 1920 has been amended to provide the Repatriation Review Tribunal with a discretion whether or not to refer to the Repatriation Commission cases where additional evidence is presented.
While the above, and other, measures have assisted in reducing the average time taken to process claims and appeals, the delays in the system need to be reduced further.
The Government is concerned that some veterans are experiencing lengthy delays in establishing entitlement to Repatriation benefits and preliminary discussions on the problems have already been held with veterans' organisations to seek their views on further measures to streamline the determining system.
Public Service: Staff Reductions
(Question No, 2959)
Mr Lloyd asked the Minister Assisting the Prime Minister, upon notice, on 8 September 1981:

Further to his answer to Question No. 2158 (Hansard, 10 June 1981, page 3560) which revealed a dramatic increase in the percentage of 2nd and 3rd Division officers in -the Commonwealth Public Service since 1975, will he confer with the Public Service Board to ensure that (a) any further reductions in the Public Service are at least applied uniformly between the various divisions and (b) as positions become vacant in the 2nd or 3rd Divisions they will be abolished or left unfilled until the 1975 ratio is restored.
Mr Viner-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
While the Government decides the level of total staff resources to be employed in the Public Service, Permanent Heads of Departments and the Public Service Board, in the exercise of their statutory functions under the Public Service Act, have primary responsibility for the classification and Divisional status of positions.
As mentioned in my answer to Question No. 2158 there are many continuing factors which are leading to changes in the distribution of staff between the Second, Third and Fourth Divisions and there is no reason to expect that a distribution between those Divisions applying in anyone year is appropriate in subsequent years.

(3) Yes. Notwithstanding this, two potential trainees were identified, on separate occasions, to undertake training in the honourable member's office. One of these decided not to pursue the opportunity. The second person had agreed to commence until it became apparent that the honourable member's electorate office was to be relocated to Brisbane's northern suburbs. As the potential trainee resided in the southern suburbs it was agreed between the honourable member's electorate office staff and officers of my Department that it would be inappropriate to proceed with the proposal. The person in question was subsequently placed in the office of Senator Bonner.
(4) Delays may occur before training opportunities offered by parliamentarians are taken up. The issue is one of matching eligible trainees to the vacancies. In this my Department endeavours to ensure that the vocational needs of individuals receive primary consideration.

Mr Holding asked the Minister representing the Minister for Aboriginal Affairs, upon notice, on 13 October 1981:

Persons Classified as Profoundly Deaf
(Question No. 3268)
Mr McLean asked the Minister for Health, upon notice, on 13 October 1981:
How many persons in Australia are classified as being profoundly deaf.
Mr MacKellar-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
No exact census of profound deafness has been undertaken in Australia. On the basis of available evidence it is estimated that there are approximately 15,700 persons in Australia who can be regarded as profoundly deaf.
Commonwealth Medical Benefits: Fitting of Contact Lenses
(Question No. 3288)
Mrs Darling asked the Minister for Health, upon notice, on 13 October 1981 :

Answers to Questions

17 November 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 2949

(I) Does the Commonwealth medical benefits schedule include an item for specialist consultations.
(2) Is it a fact that no item in the Commonwealth medical benefits schedule explicitly covers the fitting of contact lenses by specialists.
(3) Does the fitting of contact lenses require a full consultation with a specialist.
(4) Is it normal practice for the cost of fitting contact lenses to be the same as that of a specialist consultation.
(5) Does the Commonwealth medical benefits schedule also include an item covering follow-up specialist consultations.
(6) Is the cover afforded for this item half that provided under the item for specialist consultations.
(7) Does Medibank cover the fitting of contact lenses under the item for follow-up specialist consultations.
(8) If specialists regard the fitting of contact lenses as a full consultation and not a follow-up consultation, for what reason is the fitting of contact lenses covered under the follow-up specialist consultation item.
(9) Will he act to correct this apparent anomaly; if not, why not.
Mr MacKellar-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I) Yes.
(2) No. As from I September 1981, a new item was included in the Medical Benefits Schedule with a fee in all States of$49.00 for-
'Attendance by a medical practitioner for the investigation and evaluation of a patient for the fitting of contact lenses, with keratometry and testing with trial lenses and the issue of a prescription-one attendance in any period of 36 consecutive months'.
Benefits are not attracted under this item unless the lenses are prescribed at this attendance. Evaluation and fitting without the issue of a prescription do not qualify under the item.
Benefits are payable for an initial referred consultation rendered in association with the fitting and prescribing of the lenses.
Subsequent follow-up attendances attract benefits on a consultation basis.
In addition, Commonwealth medical benefits are payable for the prescribing and fitting of contact lenses by optometrists who have undertaken to participate in the Government's Optometrical Benefits Scheme.
Unlike the new item covering the evaluation of a patient for fitting of contact lenses by a medical practitioner, the item covering the prescribing and fitting of contact lenses by an optometrist covers all attendances subsequent to the initial consultation in a single course of attention. This bulk item attracts a Schedule fee of $1 08.
(3) to (8) See (2).
(9) There is no anomaly.
Defence Service Home Loans: Female Members of Services
(Question No. 3271)
Mr Scholes asked the Minister representing the Minister for Veterans' Affairs, upon notice, on 13 October 1981:

(1) Has his attention been drawn to statements by former members of the Australian Women's Army Service who ser-
ved during the Second World War, that they were enlisted for active service outside Australia because they were members of
Military Forces engaged in operations against an enemy and any of them could have been sent at any time to war zones outside of Australia.

(2) Is there any discrimination with respect to recognition of war service of female members of the Services when deter-
mining eligibility for Defence Service Home Loans.

(3) Under what circumstances are female members of the Defence Forces eligible to receive Defence Service Home
Loans.

(4) Does nursing qualify as operations against the enemy when nurses served in forward areas.

Mr Thomson-The Minister for Veterans' Affairs has provided the following answer to the honourable member's question:

(I) Yes.

(2) Consistent with the basic principle adopted when the
Defence Service Homes Scheme was introduced in 1919, benefits under the Scheme in respect of service in World War
II have been available, in general, only to men and women who were enlisted in, or appointed to, Forces raised for service
outside Australia or who actually served outside Australia.

Accordingly, service in the wartime Citizen Military Forces

and Women's Services, which were not raised for service out-

side Australia, is not qualifying service for Defence Service

Homes benefits unless the member, man or woman, served

outside Australia.

--

(3) Female members of the Defence Force are eligible for a Defence Service Homes loan on exactly the same basis as are male members.

Eligibility for a loan was extended in 1973 to members of
the Defence Force who were serving on or after 7 December 1972 and who complete 3 years' effective full-time service.

In 1978 a separate eligibility test was introduced to apply to members whose service commenced on or after 17 August
1977. Those members are required to complete a period of 6 years' effective full-time service and to enter into a commitment to render further full-time service before becoming eli-
gible for a loan.

A member of the Defence Force who served after World War II and whose service terminated before 7 December 1972 is eligible for a loan if he or she:

(a) was allotted for duty in connection with the warlike operations in Korea between 27 June 1950 and 31 August 1957 (inclusive) or in Malaya between 29 June 1950 and 31 August 1957 (inclusive) and left the last port of call in Australia or other part of the Queen's dominions before I September 1957 to serve in connection with those operations;
(b) served on land betwen I September 1957 and 27 May 1963 (inclusive) in the area of Malaya or in the area of the Island of Singapore and its then dependencies other than Christmas Island; or
(c) served on 'special service' as defined in the Repatriation (Special Overseas Service) Act in a prescribed 'special area'.

(4) This is not a consideration in determining eligibility under the Defence Service Homes Act.

(Question No. 3308)

Education: Commonwealth Training Program
(Question No. 3343)
Mr Beazley asked the Minister for Education, upon notice, on 15 October 1981:
(I) Prior to the establishment of the Commonwealth Transition Program what activities and courses had each State developed which the State authorities regarded as coming within the area of transition education.
(2) What impact on Commonwealth funding to each State for the Commonwealth Transition Program did the prior .existence of these activities and courses have.
Mr Fife-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I) Prior to the establishment of the Commonwealth Transition Program each State has developed activities and courses which they regarded as coming within the area of transition education. These included such activities as provision of pre-apprenticeship, pre-vocational and preemployment courses in TAFE, alternative courses for secondary students likely to experience difficulty in the transition to work, work experience programs for secondary students, link courses involving secondary students' participation in programs including elements of T AFE courses, and career education and guidance programs.

The extent to which specific activities had been developed varied from State to State.
(2) The allocation between States of the funds available under the Commonwealth's five year $150m School to Work Transition Program was made on the basis of the numbers of 15-19 year olds in the population in each State; the level of pre-existing activities in transition education in each State was not taken into account in that allocation in view of the wide range of activities concerned and the difficulty in making equitable comparisons of effort.

benefits on the one hand against the long-term detriment on the other and (e) in reaching its decisions on these complex matters, the Commission will need not only all the expert advice it can muster but even more importantly it must ensure that all relevant interests and persons are recognised and have a chance to put their case.

Mexico 22 Nation Summit Conference
(Question No, 1069)
Mr Barry Jones asked the Prime Minister, upon notice, on 7 April 1981 :
(I) What were the reasons for Australia being excluded from the proposed 22 Nation Summit Conference to be held in. Mexico in October 1981 to discuss North-South problems, following publication of the Brandt Report on International Development Issues.
(2) Were efforts made to secure an invitation; ifso, what.
(3) Did he receive the support of the Administration of the l)nited States of America or the Government of the United Kingdom in his efforts to secure an invitation.
(4) What reasons were given for Australia's rejection.
Mr Malcolm Fraser-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
For reasons which are well understood, the number of Heads of Government to attend the Conference at Cancun in Mexico in October 1981 was limited. Australia did not attend, but the Australian Government had long been concerned to do what it could to contribute to a successful outcome from the Cancun proceedings. North/South matters were prominent in discussions at the Commonwealth Heads of Government Meeting in Melbourne, as Australia had hoped, and both CHOGM and the Cancun meeting were notable for their constructive spirit. Because of the great importance of these issues and my Chairmanship of the Commonwealth meeting,l had discussions earlier this year both with Commonwealth leaders and with other world leaders who would be attending

the Cancun Summit, including both co-chairmen. These discussions proved extremely useful in registering Australia's views on North/South matters and as a preparation for the Melbourne Commonwealth meeting. The Melbourne meeting achieved a substantial consensus on North/South matters and, in the Government's view, made a significant contribution to the deliberations at Cancun.
Co-ordination of Recurrent Grants
(Question No. 1714)
Mr Bungey asked the Minister for Health, upon notice, on 9 April 1981:
(I) Is there any co-ordination of recurrent grants administered by the Capital Territory Health Commission and grants from other Commonwealth Departments.
(2) If so, did the Commission recognise the $8,000 grant in 1979-80 from the Family Support Scheme to the Canberra Women's Refuge when determining its recurrent grant of $57,800 for the same period.
Mr MacKellar-The answer t,o the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I) Yes. An interdepartmental committee has been set up in the A.C.T. to co-ordinate funding of community organisations. Commonwealth departments and authorities represented on the IDC are:
(i) A.C.T. Schools Authority (ii) Attorney-Genera!'s Department (iii) Capital Territory Health Commission (iv) Department of the Capital Territory (v) Department of Home Affairs and Environment (vi) Department of Immigration and Ethnic Affairs (vii) Department of Social Security
The Committee meets annually. Additional meetings are held whenever required.
(2) Yes. The Capital Territory Health Commission grant to the Canberra Women's Refuge is for salaries, administrative and operative costs.
The Family Support Scheme funded by an $8,000 grant from the Office of Child Care, Department of Social Security, is for the purpose of providing support and budget counselling for single parent families. Both the Capital Territory Health Commission and the Department of Social Security are aware of the total grant monies received by the Refuge and the purposes for which those moneys are provided.
Antarctica: Preservation of Mawson's Hut
(Question No. 1746)
Mr Bungey asked the Minister for Science and Technology, upon notice, on 9 April 1981:
(I) What is the current condition of Mawson's Hut, Commonwealth Bay, Antarctica.
(2) What work has been done by (a) Australia and (b) other nations on the hut during the last 10 years.
(3) What are the current plans in relation to the preservation, maintenance and location of the hut.
Mr Thomson-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I) In January/February 1978 a party from the Antarctic Division visited Commonwealth Bay to inspect Mawson's Hut, to assess the feasibility of returning it to Australia and to do some restoration work. The party found that the condition of the hut reflected the severe weather conditions it has had to withstand. They reported that the outer timbers had been . reduced to half their original thickness and had shrunk leaving

Answers to Questions

17 November 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 2953

gaps between the boards. The inner and main timbers were generally in good condition; however, some repairs and replacement would be necessary.
The party recommended that because of the condition of the hut and the variable weather conditions no attempt should be made to return it to Australia and that it should be restored on site.
(2) Only Australia has worked on the restoration of Mawson's Hut. Restoration commenced in the 1975-76 austral summer when roof hatches were repaired, but the main work was carried out in January 1978 when four expeditioners spent over a month at the site.
A number of tasks were undertaken by the 19711 party and they include:
temporary weather sealing of roof and outer timber lining of the hut;
excavation of workshop hut;
approximately 120 cubic metres of ice removed from the inside of the hut;
hut photographed inside and out and a film of restoration work made;
all items of historical importance catalogued and photographed.
In addition, meteorological data was collected to compare with that collected by Mawson's expedition.
(3) The Government has decided that Mawson's Hut should be restored on its present site. This decision has been endorsed by the Australian Heritage Commission which fully supports the arguments which led to this decision (these arguments are summarised on page 1124 of Senate Hansard 2 April 1981).
The next step is weatherproofing of the outer timber lining so that the hut can be cleared of ice and snow. Under present arrangements, restoration work will not recommence for several years. This is because of the present heavy commitment to the rebuilding of our Antarctic stations.
My Department has reviewed its program to see if restoration work can be brought forward. I have been advised that the demand on logistics for the rebuilding program will continue for several years. As the rebuilding program passes its peak, it will be possible to send vessels to Commonwealth Bay to continue the restoration work.
Mawson's Hut is a monument of great importance to Australia and it is my intention that restoration work will continue at the earliest possible date.

(3)(a) The ABS does not provide a breakdown of the data on income of trading and financial enterprises into the income

attributable to foreign-controlled enterprises and to Australian-Controlled enterprises. There are available data on profits and dividends payable abroad; these data enable a measure to be derived of the proportion of company income, after payment of tax, that is payable abroad. My press release no. 35 of 31 March 1980 provided details of the method of calculating this proportion and outlined some qualifications about its use. In particular, the proportion is subject to a good deal of short-term fluctuation, as evidenced by the fall in the proportion in 1978-79 and the subsequent increase in 1979-80 (after preliminary data had indicated that the proportion had stabilised). Apparent trends in the proportion may be subject to reversal as revised and more recent data become available.
The proportion is not a reliable measure of the extent of foreign. participation in the economy and may also be affected by various influences in addition to the level of foreign ownership. These include a tendency for the profitability of foreigncontrolled enterprises to differ from the Australian-controlled enterprises, and for the profitability and taxation payments of sectors in which foreign ownership is relatively high to vary relative to other sectors. For these reasons considerable care must be excercised in drawing any conclusions relating to foreign participation from the proportion.
(3) (b) and (c) Data that would enable these proportions to be derived are not available.
