Tax Deductions: Maintenance Payments
(Question No. 2741)
Mr Beazley asked the Treasurer, upon notice, on 19 August 1981:

(I) Is he able to say in which countries tax deductions are permitted for maintenance payments by divorced persons,
(2) If so, what amount is deductible in each case.
(3) Has the Australian Government considered a similar course.
Mr Howard-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I) and (2) I am not able to list all of the countries in which tax deductions are permitted for maintenance payments by divorced persons, or the amount deductible in each case, and it would be misleading to give an incomplete list since the many countries that would not be included might or might not allow such deductions. It is, of course, known that a good many countries do allow deductions and while I am unable to speak with authority on the taxation laws of other countries, I can say that in some cases a deduction is allowed for the whole of the maintenance paid while in other cases there is a limit on the amount that may be deducted. In many cases allowance of a deduction is subject to various conditions being satisfied. The maintenance payments are often taxable in the hands of the recipient, at least up to the amount allowable as a deduction to the payer.
(3) The Government has, from time to time, considered representations received from persons proposing that tax deductions be allowed for alimony or maintenance payments by divorced persons to their former marital partners. However, to amend the law as has been proposed would be contrary to the basic principle of the income tax law that a deduction is only allowable for expenditure incurred in gaining or producing assessable income, Expenditure of a private nature is precluded from deduction. Furthermore, such a proposal would place divorced persons who pay alimony or maintenance to their former partners in a more advantageous position than a married taxpayer who contributes to the maintenance of his orher spouse during a year and who is limited to a maximum concessional rebate of $830 in respect of that maintenance.
Peace Studies in Schools
(Question No. 2752)
Dr Everingham asked the Minister for Education, upon notice, on 19 August 1981:
(I) Has the Minister's attention been drawn to the article entitled 'the dove in the classroom' in The Guardian, London, 21 April 1981, and circulars from Parents and Teachers Against Violence in Education.
(2) What moves has the Minister's Department made towards planning and/or supporting a widening of peace studies in schools and colleges, including military training establishments, in the public service and elsewhere, in accordance with UNESCO principles and other international conventions and commitments.
(3) How do these moves compare with those of other countries and non-government school systems, including Quakers' schools, in Australia.
Mr Fife-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I) Yes.
(2) As a Member State of UNESCO, Australia shares a concern that mutual understanding between peoples, respect for human rights and access to education and information are important among the foundations upon which a lasting peace may be built. The Australian National Commission for UNESCO has in recent years sponsored two seminars on the topics of 'Teacher Education for International Understanding' (1977) and 'Teaching of Human Rights' (at the tertiary

2956 REPRESENTATIVES 17 November 1981

Answers to Questions

and adult education levels) (1980). These seminars drew participants from a wide range of educational interests, and were infiuential in promoting consideration and study of the teaching issues involved.
In keeping with the obligations of its membership of the United Nations, Australia supports the inclusion in the whole school curriculum of studies, which will contribute to the promotion of peace and international understanding. The Commonwealth's national role in curriculum development has been carried out by the Curriculum Development Centre (CDC). In June 1980, the CDC published a discussion paper entitled 'Core Curriculum for Australian Schools'. Two of the eight areas of knowledge and experience nominated were 'social, cultural and civic studies', and 'moral reasoning and action, value and belief systems'. Studies related to peace and international understanding could be encompassed within these areas. The CDC paper has been considered by State and non-government education authorities, which are primarily responsible for the determination of curriculum matters.
(3) There is insufficient information available to enable .valid comparisons to be made.
Spouse Rebate
(Question No. 2763)
Mrs Darling asked the Treasurer, upon notice, on 19 August 1981:
(I) Does a family with 2 wages have 2 tax-free thresholds and thereby save $2648 in tax.
(2) Does a family with one wage have one tax-free threshold plus an $830 spouse rebate thereby saving only $2172 in tax.
(3) If so, do families with one pay packet therefore pay nearly $1 0 a week more pro rata than a 2 income family on the same combined income.
(4) Would an increase in spouse rebate from $830 to $1342 redress this anomaly.
Mr Howard-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I) If each wage was $4195 or more for 1981-82 the tax-free threshold of $4195 would apply in each case.
(2) The tax-free threshold is available to all individual taxpayers. Where a taxpayer wholly maintains his or her spouse a rebate of $830 is also available.
(3) The difference in tax in the two situations would depend on the level of the combined income and the proportions in which it was shared.
(4) The effects of increasing the spouse rebate to $1342 would also depend on the level of the combined income and the proportions in which it was shared. One effect would be that, under existing arrangements for shading-out entitlement to the spouse rebate by reference to the spouse's separate net income, a taxpayer whose spouse had a separate net income equal to the $4195 tax-free threshold would be entitled to a spouse rebate of$364.
Brisbane Airport: Development
(Question No. 2793)
Mrs Darling asked the Minister for Housing and Construction, upon notice, on 18 August 1981:
(I) Have work projects undertaken in respect of the development of Brisbane Airport involved the extensive use by heavy vehicles of Kingsford Smith Drive; if so, during which

hours and on what days do the trucks use Kingsford Smith Drive.
(2) Has his attention been drawn to the fact that an increase in the volume of traffic along Kingsford Smith Drive due to the development of Brisbane Airport, has been a source of annoyance and concern to residents living next to the road.
(3) In connection with which projects involved in the Brisbane Airport development will trucks be using extensively (a) Kingsford Smith Drive (b) Sandgate Road (c) Gympie Road and (d) Nudgee Road from January 1981 to January 1982.
(4) In those cases where tenants residing in flats adjacent to roads which experience a significantly increased volume of traffic due to the airport development, vacate those flats because of an increase in noise, will any form of compensation be made to the owners of those fiats.
Mr McVeigh-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I) Work projects undertaken as part of the redevelopment of Brisbane International Airport have. involved delivery of gravel and fill material by truck to the site both along Kingsford Smith Drive and via the Sir James Holt vehicular ferry. Trucks using the ferry would account for about 10-15 per cent of the total operations. These operations commenced in mid October 1980 with the delivery of about 500 truck loads over 10days.
-Subsequent hauling operations in November and December 1980 of filling material for construction of bunds for the rehandling basin peaked at about 200-250 truck.loads per day.
Except for the trucking of approximately 2,000 cubic metres of gravel during February and March 1981, haulage operations did not resume again until May 1981. These operations have continued to the present with a maximum hauling rate of about 120 truck loads per day.
Some works at the existing Eagle Farm Airport have also involved trucking operations with approximately 50 concrete trucks and 1,000 gravel trucks proceeding to the existing Airport via Kingsford Smith Drive and approximately 550 trucks via Nudgee Road during the period from 30 June 1981 to 30 Septem ber 1981.
Trucking operations have been confined to daylight hours on six days per week, generally not earlier than 7.00 a.m. and not later than 6.00 p.m. on weekdays and 2.00 p.m. on Saturdays. These operations have been in accordance with Brisbane City Council and State Government regulations.
(2) I understand the Member for Lilley's office sought information from the Department of Housing and Construction on 24 November 1980 following complaints from Kingsford Smith Drive residents. It was advised that two airport projects involving improvements to Sugarmill Road, and the construction of a bund for the rehandling of spoil had generated some truck traffic along Kingsford Smith Drive.
Further information was sought by your office on 21 May 1981 as to the trucking traffic. The Department advised that contractor's trucks were restricted to daylight hours and to legal load limits. I have been advised that at the time of this enquiry, a considerable number of trucks were also using Kingsford Smith Drive for trucking coal for export.
(3) Further projects on the airport redevelopment will involve the haulage of construction materials to the site. At this stage it is not possible to predict the traffic distribution between Kingsford Smith Drive, Sandgate Road, Gympie Road, Nudgee Road and the vehicular ferry as the routes to be trafficked will depend on the location of source material and other factors determined by contract bidding at the time.

Answers to Questions

17 November 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 2957

(4) The various non-airport developments taking place in the lower reaches of the Brisbane River and in the north- /north eastern suburbs of the city are generating a steadily increasing volume of traffic on Kingsford Smith Drive.
The current traffic volume along Kingsford Smith Drive during daylight hours is understood to be about 11,000 to 14,000 vehicles in each direction including 2,600 to 2,800 heavy vehicles. Undoubtedly this volume will increase as a result of the widening of the road from three to four lanes by the BrisbaneCity Council, from grants provided by the Commonwealth for developmental roads.
Despite increased usage with widening and improvements to Kingsford Smith Drive, which will be completed shortly, it is expected that these measures will smooth out the flow of traffic and contribute to a reduction in traffic noise.
The extra truck traffic generated along Kingsford Smith Drive by the Brisbane Airport development represents only a relatively small proportion of the total trucking traffic and it is not considered to have been the specific cause of any deterioration in amenity for local residents.
Even at its heaviest, the airport trucking traffic only represents a small percentage of the total heavy vehicle movements along Kingsford Smith Drive. Furthermore, Kingsford Smith Drive is a public road, along which a high volume of heavy traffic has passed for many years.
Accordingly, I do not consider that compensation by the Commonwealth is appropriate to flat tenants along Kingsford Smith Drive who vacate because of noise.
Indian Ocean: Trident Submarines
(Question No. 2882)
Mr Beazley asked the Minister for Foreign Affairs, upon notice, on 27 August 1981:
(I) Has his attention been drawn to a report in the Australian of 24 August 1981 which quotes a report from the United States of America Department of Defence indicating that the Trident submarine would be deployed in the Atlantic and Pacific Oceans and not the Indian Ocean.
(2) Will he seek assurances from the US. Government that Trident submarines will not be introduced into the Indian Ocean, as it would extend the nuclear arms race in that area; if not, why not.
Mr Street-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I) Yes.
(2) No. Although the u.s. has stated publicly that it does
not routinely deploy its ballistic missile submarines through the Indian Ocean, to constrain those submarines from entering the Indian Ocean would simplify efforts to detect and track them and might thereby make vulnerable the principle element of the US. nuclear deterrent force. This would be prejudicial to international security.
Reyiew of Commonwealth Functions: Manpower Reyiews
(Question No. 2942)
Mr Humphreys asked the Minister Assisting the Prime Minister, upon notice, on 27 August 1982:
(I) Which Commonwealth Government Departments have completed their manpower reviews stemming from the recommendations of the Committee of Review of Commonwealth Functions.

(2) What staff movements have occurred in these Departments as a result of (a) natural wastage, (b) resignation, (c) redeployment and (d) retrenchment.
Mr Viner-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I) Over and above the staffing adjustments which will be required in particular departments in implementing specific Government decisions following the Review of Commonwealth Functions the decision to achieve a further 2 per cent reduction in staff ceilings over two years will affect departmental staffing generally. In respect of this latter decision and other decisions requiring progressive implementation over a period of years manpower review will be a continuing process.
(2) (a) Staff separations (,natural wastage') from Commonwealth Departments and other bodies staffed under the Public Service Act for the period I May 1981 to 30 August 1981 were as follows:
Permanent staff 4,562; Temporary and exempt staff 3,129; Total staff 7,691.
(2) (b) Comprehensive statistics on resignations for the period I May 1981 to 30 August 1981 are not yet available. Statistics on separations from the Australian Public Service during calendar year 1980 published in the Public Service Board's 1980-81 Annual Report indicate that resignations comprise about 76 per cent of separations of permanent staff.
(2) (c) Redeployment most commonly involves the movement of staff within a department from lower to higher priority activities and does not involve the use of the provisions of the Commonwealth Employees (Redeployment and Retirement) Act. The redeployment of staff in this way occurs on a day to day basis and statistics on such ll}jwements are not available.
However, as a result of decisions stemming from the recommendations of the Committee of Review of Commonwealth Functions a total of 62 staff have been declared under the Commonwealth Employees (Redeployment and Retirement) Act as being eligible for redeployment because they were surplus to the requirements of their departments. As at 15 October 1981, 18 of the 62 have been redeployed. Action is continuing in relation to all others with a view to effecting their redeployment as soon as possible.
(2) (d) No officers have been compulsorily retrenched; three officers of the Department of Defence and one from the Australian Electoral Office have elected, under the Public Service Arbitrator's Determination No. 509 of 1977, with the Board's approval, to have their employment terminated and have thereby qualified for the benefits associated with retrenchment.

Libyan Claim to the Gulf of Sidra
(Question No. 3002)
Dr Klugman asked the Minister for Foreign Affairs, upon notice, on 9 September 1981:

(2) Is the Gulf included in papers and memoranda on Historic Bays prepared by the United Nations Secretariat.
(3) [s he also able to say whether (a) Italy, (b) Malta, (c) Egypt, (d) Tunisia, (e) the Union of Soviet Socialist Republics or (f) any Mediterranean littoral countries recognise Libya's claim.

Mr Street-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:

Libya claimed that 'through history and without any dispute, the Libyan Arab Republic has exercised its sovereignty over the Gulf'.
(2) Neither the United Nations Secretariat's Memorandum of 30 September 1957 entitled 'Historic Bays' nor its study of 9 March 1962 entitled 'Juridical Regime of Historic Waters, including Historic Bays' make any reference to the Gulf of Sidra or to Libyan claims to the Gulf.
(3) We are not aware, on the information available to us, that any of the countries listed, or any of the littoral countries of the Mediterranean, has expressly recognised the Libyan claim to the Gulf of Sidra.

Superannuation Fund Contributions: Tax Deductibility
(Question No. 3079)
Mr Beazley asked the Treasurer, upon notice, on 10 September 1981:
(I) In assessing income tax payable, why are non-employer sponsored superannuation fund contributions deducted from net income (item 37 of form A) while employer sponsored superannuation fund contributions form part of the basic concession of $1 ,590.
(2) Are the maximum deductions of $300 for rates and $100 for funeral expenses related to average expenditure on these items; if not, how are the figures determined.
(3) Will he take action to raise these maximum deductions.
Mr Howard-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I) In introducing the income tax concession for personal superannuation contributions by self-employed persons and employees without employer-sponsored superannuation arrangements, the Government sought to match the taxation position of those taxpayers as closely as possible with that of employees who are members of employer-sponsored superannuation funds. The latter are able to benefit both from the fact that an income tax deduction is available to the employer for amounts contributed for the employee's benefit and by their own contributions qualifying as rebatable expenditure under the concessional rebate provisions of the income tax law. The new concession provides broadly comparable deductions for persons who do not have tax-subsidised employer contributions made for them.
(2) No; the maximum amounts of $300 for rates and $100 for funeral expenses are not related to average expenditure of these kinds by taxpayers and are not designed to offset the full cost of such expenditure. They represent the extent to which, with due regard to revenue commitments, it has been found possible to grant tax relief to taxpayers incurring such expenditure. They are expenses of a private or domestic nature and as such have no claim in taxation principle to constitute a deduction in determining taxable income. The concessional allowances made in respect of them is by way of a tax rebate to the extent that all reba table expenditure exceeds $1,590.

(3) Concessional tax allowances are reviewed in the context of the Budget.

Indexation of Personal Income Tax
(Question No. 3137)
Mr Robert Brown asked the Treasurer, upon notice, on 16 September 1981:
(I) If full indexation of personal income tax based on movements in the CPI and without any discounting had been applied each year, then instead of the tax free threshold being $4,195 in 1981-82 what would it have been using the tax free threshold for (a) 1976-77 and (b) 1977-78 as base.
(2) What is the estimated number of taxpayers in 1981-82 who will have a taxable income of (a) below $4,195 and (b) between $4,195 and the threshold which would have applied had full tax indexation been applied each year since 1976-77.
(3) What is the estimated total tax which will be paid by those taxpayers included in part 2(b).
Mr Howard-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I) (a) Effectively the tax-free threshold for resident individuals subject to tax at the general rates for the 1976-77 income year was $2,845, the maximum level of taxable income at which the gross tax payable was exceeded by the general concessional rebate of $610. [f this tax-free threshold had been increased in line with the changes in the CPI from the average of the 4 quarters ended March 1976 to the average of the 4 quarters ended March 1981, the threshold would have been $4,662.
(b) The tax-free threshold for the 1981-82 income year based on the 1977-78 income year figure of $3,402, moved up in a similar manner to I (a) by the full CPI, would have been $4,908.
(2) (a) The relevant income tax statistics of non-taxable individuals relate only to those who have occasion to lodge in-- come tax returns and exclude, for example, dependent wives, children and certain recipients of social security benefits. The estimated number of taxpayers who will lodge returns for the 1981-82 income year, but will not be subject to tax because their taxable incomes will be less than $4,195, is about 1,300,<)00.
(b) The number of individuals who will have taxable incomes in the range $4,196-4,662 for the 1981-82 income year is estimated to be about 150,000.
(3) The estimated total income tax payable for the 1981-82 income year by taxpayers referred to in part 2(b) is about $lOm. However, all other taxable individuals have part of their taxable income within the range $4,196 to $4,662. The total tax payable on all taxable income falling within this range in 1981-82 income year will be about $900m.
International Year of Disabled Persons: Sport and Recreation Needs of the Disabled
(Question No. 3141)
Mr Beazley asked the Minister representing the Minister for Social Security, upon notice, on 16 September 1981:
(I) What considerations were taken into account when the Government decided to Ret up the Committee on the sport and recreation needs of th~ disabled.

(2) [s this committee under the chairmanship of Mr [an Wilson, MP, and is it the only committee that the Government has decided to set up in this International Year of Disabled Persons; if not, what other committees have been established.
(3) What funding will be made available to implement decisions that are made by the Committee.
Mr Hunt-The Minister for Social Security has provided the following answer to the honourable member's question:
(I) and (2) The National Committee on Sport and Recreation for the Disabled was created as an advisory committee to the Government as part of a new Government policy on recreation and sport for mentally and physically disabled persons. It is indicative of the measure of concern of the Government for [YDP and is intended to extend beyond 1981. The policy of the Government is to assist and encourage physically and mentally disabled persons to participate in sport and recreation.
The objectives of the Committee are to assist the integration of disabled athletes with non-disabled athletes and to enable the best of the disabled athletes to compete at the international level. Its chairman is Dr Jeffrey Miller, Principal of the Cumberland College of health Sciences and a member of the Nationa[ Advisory Council for the Handicapped.
Other committees the Government has established to coordinate planning for IYDP in Australia are:
the Council of Commonwealth, State and Territory Ministers for IYDP which co-ordinates Australia's national response to IYDP;
an Officials Committee, chaired by the Director-General of Social Services, which supports the Council and consists of nominated representatives of each Minister on the Council,and
the IYDP National Committee of Non-Government Organisations, established in conjunction with ACROD (Australian Council for Rehabilitation of Disabled), which is responsible for co-ordinating the non-government sector's response to IYDP.
(3) The 1981-82 Budget allocated $200,000 to the Department of Home Affairs and Environment for programs and projects for sport and recreation for disabled people. Applications for funding were solicited through all major daily newspapers in mid-September. The National Committee on Sport and Recreation for the Disabled will be advising the Minister for Home Affairs and Environment of its recommendations for program and project funding in the near future.
Department of Social Security: Welfare Staff
(Question No. 3152)
Dr Everingham asked the Minister representing the Minister for Social Security, upon notice, on 16 September 1981:
(I) Has the Minister's attention been drawn to a recent circular letter from the Australian Social Welfare Union; Queensland Branch, claiming that recruitment freezes and staffing restrictions have allowed less than half of the Social Security offices in Queensland to fulfil their allocation of 2 welfare staff, even in districts where no other trained or trainee social workers are available, and that the 3 Aboriginal Welfare Officer posts, all in Brisbane, are unfilled.
(2) If so, is it a fact that problems, some with no solution in these circumstances, are constantly being referred in growing numbers to members and senators, and to voluntary and State bodies.

Answers to Questions

17 November 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 2961

Mr Hunt-The Minister for Social Security has provided the following answer to the honourable member's question:
(I) & (2) The circular letter from the Australian Social Welfare Union has been brought to my attention.
The situation is that as a consequence of the Review of Commonwealth Functions, the Government directed that the Public Service Board, in consultation with my department, review the activities of departmental social workers against the background that social work activity is to be confined to that directly related to the delivery of social security benefits, Commonwealth programs and rehabilitation services.
As to the specific references in the honourable member's question about the staffing levels for welfare staff in the offices of my department in Queensland, every attempt is made to ensure that each regional office has the services of at least one social worker.
In fact, with the exception of Bundaberg and Maryborough regional offices where recruitment action is currently underway, all regional offices in Queensland have at least one position of social worker staffed.
All 3 Aboriginal Welfare Officer positions in Brisbane are currently staffed.
Tax Deductions: Car Telephone Units
(Question No. 3154)
Mr Scholes asked the Treasurer, upon notice, on 16 September 1981:
Will luxury car phone units plus rentals, recently introduced by Telecom with costs exceeding several thousand dollars, be allowable tax deductions for vehicle users able to write-off car use, etc., as a business or company cost.
Mr Howard-The answer-to the honourable member's question is as follows:
I am advised that mobile telephones may be either purchased or rented from Telecom. Where a taxpayer establishes that a mobile telephone is used in the course of gaining or producing assessable income, an income tax deduction would, where the instrument is purchased, be allowable by way of depreciation based on its cost and, where the instrument is rented, in respect of the rent paid. The deduction would, however, be appropriately reduced if the telephone is also used to an extent for private purposes.
Social Welfare: Pension Income Test
(Question No. 3171)
Mr Humphreys asked the Minister representing the Minister for Social Security, upon notice, on 17 September 1981:
(I) Are interest payments derived from long-term deposits averaged out to arrive at weekly income when the pension income-test is applied by the Department of Social Security.
(2) If so, is the pension income-test on income derived from long-term deposits applied prospectively or retrospectively.
Mr Hunt-The Minister for Social Security has provided the following answer to the honourable member's question:
(I) Under the provisions of the Social Services Act pensions are payable at an annual rate and that rate is determined by taking into account the annual rate of a person's income, including the annual rate of income from long-term deposits. Pensions are paid in fortnightly instalments, the amount of

each instalment being one twenty-sixth of the determined annual rate of pension.
(2) As indicated above, the income test is applied having regard to a person's annual rate of income at the time of assessment. For example, a pensioner with $10,000 invested in long term deposits at a rate of interest of 10 per cent would be assessed as having an annual rate of income of$1 ,000.
Social Welfare: Liquidation of Income-producing Assets
(Question No. 3172)
Mr Humphreys asked the Minister representing the Minister for Social Security, upon notice, on 17 September 1981:
(I) Is it a fact that new provisions under the Social Services Act to guard agair.st deliberate deprivation of income on the part of pensioners sanction the liquidation of incomeprod;Jcing assets for the purchase of (a) a car, (b) a home or (c) holidays.
(2) If so, do these provisions require that these items must be for the immediate use of the pensioner concerned.
Mr Hunt-The Minister for Social Security has provided the following answer to the honourable member's question:
(I) and (2) As I announced in the Senate on \3 October 1981, there will be some delay in introducing the legislation to strengthen the provisions of the Social Services Act against schemes contrived to circumvent the income test. When the legislation has been introduced further announcements will be made as to its operational aspects. However, as I mentioned in my statement of 18 August 1981, it is not intended that the new provisions will operate to penalise people if they liquidate .income-producing assets for reasonable purposes, such as the purchase of a home or a car for their personal use or for a holiday.
Sales Tax on Bulk Cartage Grain Vehicles
(Question No. 3265)
Mr Kerin asked the Treasurer, upon notice, on 13 October 1981:
(I) What was the reason that 17.5 per cent sales tax was imposed on bulk cartage grain vehicles in the 1981-82 Budget.
(2) Were there widespread examples of abuse of the previous concession.
(3) Is it a fact that this imposition will be heaviest in areas where there is not a well developed or close location of silos.
(4) Is it also a fact that vehicles used by cartage contractors cost up to $80,000 and that the tax will add severely to wheat growers' costs.
(5) Does he expect that contractors and farmers will seek to exceed truck weight limits due to the imposition, thereby causing higher costs for local road maintenance.
(6) Is he concerned that the imposition will tend to discourage the production of grain in newer areas of agricultural activity.
Mr Howard-The answers to the honourable member's questions are:
(I) The provision under which exemption had been gained for bulk grain vehicles was not intended to cover such goods. It was designed to cover grain storage and handling plant at grain silos and terminals at railway sidings and ports. The wording of the provision was such, however, that bulk grain

2962 REPRESENTATIVES 17 November 1981

Answers to Questions

vehicles were included. This fortuitous exemption was contrary to the general policy in the sales tax law that road vehicles used in the transportation of goods, whether rural products or secondary products, are subject to sales tax. The taxing of bulk grain vehicles is consistent with this general principle.
(2) Yes.
(3) No. In the more developed areas trucks are required to cart the grain from the field to the silo.
(4) The tax is significant but it is no greater than that imposed on other similar transport vehicles which for many years have borne tax.
(5) No.
(6) It is not expected that the sales tax will, in any significant way, discourage the production of grain in newer areas because vehicles used to transport other primary products have always been taxable and there is no evidence that this has discouraged activity in these areas of primary production. Also, it is a fact that many general cartage contractors paid tax on vehicles used to cart grain before the unintended exemption began to be exploited and their cartage rates would have been based on a tax-inclusive purchase price for their vehicles. Similarly, general purpose trucks used to cart grain at harvest time are purchased at a tax inclusive price. Rates fixed by reference to this have not discouraged the opening up of new areas of grain production.

(1) Yes.
(2) Yes.
(3) Yes.

Repatriation General Hospital, Daw Park, South Australia: Operating Theatres
(Question No. 3302)
Mr Jacobi asked the Minister representing the Minister for Veterans' Affairs, upon notice, on 14 October 1981:
(I) Does the Minister recall my repeated questions relating to the urgent need for the replacement of the operating theatres at the Repatriation Hospital, Daw Park, South Australia.
(2) Did the Minister state in reply to question No. 2370 (Hansard, 27 August 1981, pages 960-1) that (a) the Specialist Advisory Committee had expressed concern to the Deputy Commissioner in South Australia about the theatres, (b) the Committee had also expressed concern about the future of the hospital if a decision on replacement of the theatres was not soon made and (c) the Minister was unable to provide any details about the date of commencement of the project.
(3) Did the Treasurer state in the 1981-82 Budget Speech that the Government has had before it a number of proposals .for capital expenditure at the repatriation Hospitals, one of which had been considered by the Parliamentary Public Works Committee and it was not possible to announce a decision on any of these proposals, which involve the expenditure of many millions of dollars, but the Government would be giving further consideration to Repatriation hospitals later in 1981.
(4) Were reports and administrative recommendations for the replacement of the operating theatres formulated as long as 4 years ago; if so, did the recommendations support the replacement of the theatres as a matter of urgency.
(5) Are approximately 4000 operations performed annually in these theatres; if so, would it be impossible for these operations to be performed in other hospitals in the Adelaide area during the same period.
(6) In view of the mounting concern of staff, patients and in particular the Returned Services League and the South Australian Consultative Council of Ex-Servicemen's Organisations who have actively supported the need to up-date not just the operating theatres but the Hospital as a whole, and in view of the undertaking contained in the 1981-82 Budget statement, when will the Minister announce his decision appropriating the funds necessary to ensure that these outmoded operating theatres are replaced as quickly as possible.
Mr Thomson-The Minister for Veterans' Affairs has provided the following answer to the honourable member's question:

The concern about the facilities at Repatriation General Hospital, Daw Park should be allayed by my Press Statement of I November 1981 indicating that the Government has decided that plans should be put to the Standing Parliamentary Committee on Public Works for the construction of four new operating theatres and surgical wards and associated facilities at the hospital, estimated to cost about $6.6m. These proposed new facilities would meet almost all the surgical needs of the veteran population in South Australia, both in terms of the number of operations and of the increasing complexity of modern surgical procedures.
The plans for re-developing part of the Repatriation General Hospital, Daw Park, were announced simultaneously with the Government decision to reaffirm its commitment to the maintenance of a separate Repatriation hospital system for the treatment of entitled veterans. The Government considers that adequate funds should be provided for capital and operational expenditure at these hospitals.
These announcements should dispel any doubts about their long term future.

(Question No. 3354)
Mrs Darling asked the Minister representing the Minister for Social Security, upon notice, on 20 October 1981:

(4) Are all announcements involving routine Funding allocations a matter of partisan privilege.

Soviet Union Military Presence in other Countries
(Question No. 3382)
Mr McLean asked the Minister for Foreign Affairs, upon notice, on 21 October 1981:
Is he able to say in which countries the Soviet Union maintains a military presence, provides military assistance and military advisers, or in some other way provides tangible deFence support.
Mr Street-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
The Soviet Union maintains a military presence in the Following countries whi~h are members of the Warsaw Pact:

Czechoslovakia, The German Democratic Republic, Hungary and Poland. (Although members of the Warsaw Pact, Romania has no significant Soviet military presence on its territory and Bulgaria has no Formed Soviet combat units.) Soviet combat units are also maintained in AFghanistan and Mongolia, and a small Soviet combat brigade is stationed in Cuba. The Soviet Union maintains a naval presence in Ethiopia, the People's Democratic Republic of Yemen and Vietnam.
In addition to the above countries, the principal recipients of Soviet military assistance in the Form of equipment and, in some cases, military advisers are as Follows: Algeria, Angola, Congo, Guinea, India, Iran, Iraq, the People's Republic of Korea, Libya, Madagascar, Mali, Mozambique, Nicaragua, Nigeria, Peru, Syria, Tanzania, the Arab Republic of Yemen, Yugoslavia and Zambia.

Department of Veterans' Affairs: Processing of Entitlement and Pension Claims
(Question No. 3417)
Mr Free asked the Minister representing the Minister for Veterans' Affairs, upon notice, on 22 October 1981:
(I) How many staff were engaged in the processing of entitlement and pension claims in the Sydney office of the Department of Veterilns' Affairs as at 21 October 1981.

(3) How many staff were engaged in these tasks during 1975.
(4) What was the (a) average and (b) maximum time lag during 1975.
Mr Thomson-The Minister for Veterans' Affairs has provided the following answer to the honourable member's question:
(I) As at 21 October 1981 there were approximately 339 staff involved in the processing and determination of pension and entitlement claims and appeals; distributed as follows:
8 Repatriation Boards each comprising 3 members; 3 Delegates of the Repatriation Commission; 4 Repatriation Review Tribunal (R.R.T.) panels, each comprising 3 members; approximately 300 staff were engaged in processing.
(2)entitlement claim (a) 286 days (b) 600 days-
Commission appeal on entitlement (a) 332 days (b) 500 days
R.R.T. application on entitlement (a) 495 days (b) 600 days-
application for pension increase (a) 192 days (b) 600 days-
R.R.T. application on assessment (a) 338 days (b) 600 days-
- Time taken is recorded on 0-600 day scale. It is not possible to give the time beyond 600 days without intensive file examination.
(3) During 1975 there were, on average, 383 staff involved in the processing of pension and entitlement claims and appeals; as follows:
3 Repatriation Boards each comprising 3 members; 2 Delegates of the Repatriation Commission; 2 Assessment Appeal Tribunals (W.P.A.A.T.), each comprising 3 members; 2 Entitlement Appeal Tribunals (W.P.E.A.T.), each comprising 3 members; approximately 360 staff were engaged in processing.

W.P.E.A.T. appeal on entitlement (a) 249 days (b) 300 days*
application for pension increase (a) 116 days (b) 300 days*
W.P.A.A.T. appeal on assessment (a) 122 days (b) 300 days-
- In 1975 the time taken to process was recorded on a 0-300 day scale. It would not be possible to specify the maximum time taken.

By way of general comment, it should be understood that the systems applying in 1975 and at present are not identical. The legislation has been amended and there is now a requirement that reasons for decisions be given.
Action is being taken to reduce delays in finalising claims and appeals (see answer to Senate Question No. 1408).
Uranium Advisory Council
(Question No. 3449)
Mr Scott asked the Minister for Trade and Resources, upon notice, on 28 October 1981:

McIntyre to him, in which the Chairman recommended that the Council's report on the review of the Atomic Energy Act and the submissions received be made available to other interested parties as early as possible, will he release this report and the submissions, as recommended; if not, why not.
Mr Anthony-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
I am able to advise the honourable member that the report of the Uranium Advisory Council on its review of the Atomic Energy Act and related matters, together with submissions which the Council received on this matter in response to its public call for submissions, are available to interested parties.
As is the usual practice with reports and advisings to the Government from the Uranium Advisory Council, the report of the Council in relation to this matter appears in its annual report in Appendix H. I refer the honourable member to page 2 of the annual report of the Council, under the heading 'Submissions by the Public', which indicates that 46 public submissions on the review of the Atomic Energy Act were received by the Council, some of them quite extensive. The report states that bound volumes containing copies of submissions are held by the Council library. They may be perused there by interested parties or may be borrowed for a limited period. Defence Service Homes Loam.
(Question No. 3458)
Mr Beazley asked the Minister representing the Minister for Veterans' Affairs, upon notice, on 28 October 1981 :
(1) What is the current waiting time for applicants for Defence Service Homes Loans.
(2) Is it a fact that the Defence Service Homes Corporation claims it cannot extend loans to servicemen already buying a home; ifso, is it because of the limited capital available.
(3) Is it also a fact that in 1980-81, an appropriation of $12m to augment the capital of the Defence Service Homes Corporation, was not drawn; if so, why.
Mr Thomson-The Minister for Veterans' Affairs has provided the following answer to the honourable member's question:
(1) A waiting time of 10 months applies to loans for the purchase of a home. The waiting period does not apply to loans for the construction of a home.
(2) In accordance with a Ministerial Direction issued in 1973 the corporation may not grant a loan for the discharge of a mortgage entered into without the prior approval of the Corporation unless the terms of the mortgage are considered

2966 REPRESENTATIVES 17 November 1981

Answers to Questions

to be onerous and payment of commitments under this mortgage is causing serious hardship. This Direction varied a previous Direction issued in 1951 by permitting consideration to be given to whether the terms of the mortgage are onerous by current standards. The policy applies not only to serving members of the Defence Force but to all eligible persons.
The cost of relaxation of the policy cannot be estimated with confidence, but it could be expected to be sufficiently high to require a substantial additional Budget allocation or an extension of the waiting period.
(3) At the time the 1980-81 Budget was prepared it was estimated that a capital appropriation of $12m would be required to meet the' shortfall between the cost of the estimated demand for loans ($111.5m) and the funds estimated to become available from the Corporation's operations, mainly repayments of existing loans. In the event, expenditure was lower than estimated and funds available for re-Iending were higher, principally because of the number of loans prematurely discharged. The estimation process was complicated in 1980-81 by the decisions to increase the maximum Defence Service Homes Loan by $10,000 to $25,000 and to reduce the waiting period from 14 months to 10 months.
Sales Tax
Mr Howard-On 8 September 1981 (Hansard, page 987) Mr Lloyd asked me, without notice:
Can the Treasurer clarify the present confusion, and the charges of discrimination between horses, dogs and cats, in the application of sales tax to manufactured pet food, saddlery and harness? [s a person who earns his living by breeding, training or racing dogs able to purchase food and other goods for those dogs free of sales tax while someone who earns his living from training or racing horses is not exempt from paying sales tax on their food or other goods such as saddlery? Does a breeder of horses, dogs or cats have to earn his total income from this pursuit to be exempt from sales tax on food for those animals? [f not, at what percentage of income does the tax apply? Are all breeds of dogs exempt if the other criteria are met or only breeds of working dogs? Finally, is the proprietor of a supermarket or other shop retailing pet food required to remove the sales tax on presentation of an exemption certificate number, or can the proprietor force the purchaser to buy his pet food only from a wholesaler?
The answer to the honqurable member's question is as follows:
(I) The tax on pet foods applies only to foods for animals kept as domestic pets. Horses are not regarded as domestic pets but dogs and cats are. Food for horses and for dogs and cats not kept as domestic pets is exempt. All saddlery and harness is now taxable except where it is purchased for use in agricultural industry exclusively, or primarily and principally, in carrying out agricultural operations.
(2) Persons breeding or training racing dogs or training or racing horses are entitled to purchase foods for the dogs or horses free of tax. Equipment for racing dogs, such as muzzles, collars and leads, is taxable as is saddlery and harness for racing horses.
(3) A horse or dog or ca t breeder does not have to earn his or her total income from such pursuits for exemption to apply for food for breeding animals. [t is a question of fact whether a person operates a breeding business, either full or part-time, as distinct from merely keeping the dogs or cats as domestic pets with any breeding being incidental to their role as domestic pets.
(4) No distinction is drawn between breeds of dogs.
(5) There is no requir~ment at law to compel a proprietor of a supermarket or oth~r retailer of pet food to remove the

sales tax on production of an exemption certificate. Whether or not a certificate is accepted is a matter between the retailer and the customer. This situation is not new; it has existed for many years and applies also in respect of all other taxable goods that are purchased at the retail level for use in exempt circumstances. It may be that in practice a person entitled to exemption will find it more convenient to deal with a pet food supplier than with a supermarket.
Sales Tax
Mr Howard-On 9 September 1981 (Hansard, page 1060) Mr Ian Cameron asked me, without notice:
Will the Treasurer ensure that, to save resources and red tape, the Treasury will allow primary producers and manufacturers to use their existing diesel certificate numbers to get an exemption from sales tax on oils and greases? They are different taxes but in the interests of good management they should be grouped on the same certificate.
The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
On 9 September 1981 Mr [an Cameron (Hansard, page 1060) asked me, without notice, whether primary producers could use their existing diesel certificate numbers to obtain exemption from sales tax on oils and greases.
[ indicated that this was an administrative matter which [ would take up with the Commissioner of Taxation. [ have since received further information from the honourable member that dealers and agents involved in marketi'ng oils and greases have expressed concern about the administrative procedures necessary to gain exemption.
The Commissioner has advised that, while a diesel certificate number may assist in establishing the bona fides of a person claiming exemption from sales tax, it would not be practicable from a legal viewpoint to use one certificate for two different taxes. The exemptions are not parallel and ther~ are different responsibilities under the relevant legislation involved. It would not be practicable or desirable to cover the requirements of two different sets of legislation with one certificate.
The Commissioner suggested that any supplier of oils and greases experiencing problems with exemption claims should contact the local taxation office where officers would be available to discuss the matter. The kind of problems likely to arise in this area have arisen previously in other situations and satisfactory solutions found.
[ mention also that the Government is at present reviewing the system of exemption certificates under the sales tax law. The information supplied by the honourable member will be taken into account in this review.
Quarantine: Landings by Foreign Fishermen
(Question No. 1699)
Mr Bungey asked the Minister for Health, upon notice, on 9 April 1981:
(I) How many reports of landings by foreign fishermen on the north-west coast of Australia and adjacent islands have been received since July 1977.
(2) Were any of these reports substantiated; if so, what are the details.
(3) Did any reports refer to animals accompanying the fishermen; if so, were they substantiated.
Mr MacKellar-The answers to the honourable member's questions are as follows:

Answers to Questions

17 November 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 2967

(1) Accurate records of the kind needed to answer the honourable member's questions have only been kept since July 1978 with the introduction of the Government's upgraded Civil Coastal Surveillance Program. Since then fifty-four reports have been received of foreign vessels being sighted in positions or situations from which it had to be assumed for quarantine purposes that landings on the north-west coast of Australia or adjacent islands had occurred or were about to occur.
(2) On investigation it was possible to verify that landings on Australian soil had occurred on twenty-five of those occasions. Details are as follows:
25.9.79-An Army Nomad aircraft sighted an Indonesian fishing vessel In the vicinity of Adele Island. The Transport vessel M.V. Cape Pillar responded and found that the food and water cache at the Adele Island lighthouse had been stolen and there was Indonesian writing on the lighthouse walls. The fishing vessel could not be relocated.
3.10.79-Littoral surveillance aircraft sighted two Indonesian fishing vessels anchored off Tapers Inlet with canoes heading from the beach to the vessel. A chartered helicopter response was mounted and the landing confirmed but the fishing vessel was not relocated.
18.10.79-Littoral surveillance aircraft sighted one Indonesian fishing vessel at Cartier Islet with two people on the beach tending fish drying racks. The fishing vessel departed on the aircraft's approach.
21.10.79-Aborigines from One Arm Point Mission reported landing of crews from two Indonesian fishing vessels on West Roe Island. An Animal Quarantine Officer, on investigation, confirmed the landings. The vessels could not be relocated.
27.10.79-Chartered aircraft on surveillance response task reported an Indonesian fishing vessel hidden in mangroves at Sir Frederick Island. The surveillance chartered vessel 'Collendina' responded, escorted the fishing vessel to sea and issued warnings.
29.10.79Littora I surveillance aircra ft sighted an Indonesian fishing vessel at Bedford Island with one person on shore and three canoes in the vicinity. The fishing vessel was boarded by officers from the Customs vessel 'Jacana' and then escorted to sea.
25.11.79-Littoral surveillance aircraft sighted an Indonesian fishing vessel moored in mangroves at East Sunday Island. The surveillance chartered vessel 'D.McD' could not relocate the fishing vessel.
27.11.79-The chartered vessel 'D.McD' located and boarded an Indonesian fishing vessel hidden in mangroves at Tide Rip Island. The fishing vessel departed the area overnight.
28.11.79-Littoral surveillance aircraft reported an Indonesian fishing vessel with three people having landed at High Island. The chartered vessel 'D.McD' located two Indonesian vessels high and dry on the beach. The masters of the vessels were warned and then escorted to sea.
7.12.79-Littoral surveillance aircraft reported an Indonesian fishing vessel hidden in mangroves at Bedford Island. Health and Fisheries officers from the surveillance chartered vessel 'Collendina' boarded the fishing vessel and escorted it to sea following a warning being given.
11.l2.79-Littoral surveillance aircraft reported an Indonesian fishing vessel hidden in mangroves at Mermaid Island. Officers from HMAS Adroit boarded the vessel and ordered it to sea.
16.12.79-Littoral surveillance aircraft reported an Indonesian fishing vessel hidden in mangroves at Pascoe

Island. Officers from HMAS Ardent confirmed the landing, boarded the vessel and confiscated a quantity of fish and trochus. The following day the vessel was escorted to sea and a warning issued.
17.4.80-Three reports were received and confirmed by littoral surveillance aircraft of Indonesian fishermen having made landings on various islands. Two vessels were seen at Island Reef, 25 miles north west of Cafferelli Island, one vessel on High Island, and another two hidden in mangroves at Sunday Island. In response to these reports the Customs vessel 'Jacana', with two Quarantine Officers and a State Police Officer on board, boarded four Indonesian vessels in King Sound between 17 and 19 April, whilst WA. Fisheries Inspectors on board HMAS Aware boarded a total of eleven vessels in the vicinity of Scott, Ashmore and Brue Reefs. All vessels were given warnings and departed the area.
20.4.80-Littoral surveillance aircraft reported two Indonesian fishing vessels at the west island of the Lacepedes group with canoes on the beach. Further investigation failed to relocate the vessels.
8.5.80-Littoral surveillance aircraft reported one Indonesian fishing vessel at Adele Island with a landing having occurred. Fisheries officers from the surveillance chartered vessel 'Collendina' confirmed the landing, boarded the Indonesian vessel and issued warnings. The fishing vessel departed the area.
13.5.80-Littoral surveillance aircraft sighted one Indonesian fishing vessel hidden in mangroves at Curlew Bay. The vessel could not be relocated on further investigation.
2.6.80-Littoral surveillance aircraft sighted one Indonesian fishing vessel anchored at North Maret Island with people foraging on the reef. Subsequent investigation failed to relocate the vessel.
9.6.80-Littoral surveillance aircraft reported one Indonesian fishing vessel beached at Adele Island. Officers from the chartered vessel 'Axion' boarded two Indonesian vessels in the area. The masters denied landing at Adele Island and were directed to leave the area.
14.6.80-Littoral surveillance aircraft reported one Indonesian fishing vessel camouflaged in mangroves at East Roe Island. This vessel was subsequenlly boarded by officers from HMAS Ardent and escorted to sea.
15.6.80-Littoral surveillance aircraft reported one Indonesian fishing vessel hidden in mangroves at Sunday Island. Officers from the chartered vessel 'Axion' investigated, towed the vessel clear of the island and HMAS Ardent escorted it to sea.
16.6.80-Littoral surveillance aircraft reported two Taiwanese fishing vessels aground on Churchill Reef. Fisheries officers on board HMAS Ardent investigated and found two licensed Taiwanese fishing vessels, one of which had accidently run aground. It was refloated at the next high tide.
22.7.80-Littoral surveillance aircraft reported one Indonesian fishing vessel hidden in mangroves at Gregory Island with the crew gathering trochus shells on reef. Officers on board HMAS Ardent investigated the report, apprehended the vessel and towed it to Koolan Island. Legal proceedings were instituted and the master was subsequently fined for illegal landing and the vessel was confiscated.
23.7.80-Littoral surveillance aircraft reported one Indonesian fishing vessel beached on Bedford Island. This vessel was apprehended by officers on board HMAS Ardent and towed to Koolan Island. Legal proceedings were instituted with similar results as in preceding incident.

Immigration: Settler Worker Arrivals
(Question No. 2474)
Mr Uren asked the Minister for Immigration and Ethnic Affairs, upon notice, on 5 June 1981:
How many settler worker arrivals in (a) Australia as a whole and (b) each State and Territory in each quarter since 1976 have been (i) skilled and (ii) semi-skilled in the building and construction trades.
Mr Macphee-The answer to the honourable member's question, set out in the table below, is derived from the Australian Bureau of Statistics Occupation Tables and is as follows:
Prior to July 1979, details required to compile these statistics were obtained from settlers' self-description of their occupations. This information was recorded in incoming Passenger Cards. Since then the necessary details have been obtained from the worker categories into which intending migrants are determined to fall, completed by overseas posts. This information is included in a tear-out tab which is placed in passports, at the same time as visa issue. On arrival in Australia these tabs are removed from passports by primary officers and attached to incoming Passenger Cards.
This latter method provides more accurate information than the earlier used self-description method. The tendency under the self-description method was for people to inflate their level of skill which would seem to be borne out by the higher proportion of semi-skilled revealed since the end of 1979.

Copyright: Audiovisual Recordings
(Question No. 2724)
Mr H.umphreys asked the Minister representing the Attorney-General, upon notice, on 19 August 1981:
(I) Is it a fact that the 1980 amendment to s. 183 of the Copyright Act removed from all educational institutions the protection afforded by the wording 'for the services of the Commonwealth or State' in relation to audiovisual recording.
(2) Will possible breaches of the amended Act by Members, Senators and users of the Parliamentary Library be overlooked.
(3) Will the Attorney-General elaborate and clarify the new legal responsibility of public educational institutions under s. 183 of the Act as amended.
Mr N. A. Brown-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(1) Section 183 of the Copyright Act 1968 provided certain protection from infringement of copyright in respect of acts 'done for the services of the Commonwealth or State'. Section 24 of the Copyright Amendment Act 1980 added a new provision to s. 183 which qualifies this protection in relation to educational institutions 'of, or under the control of, the Commonwealth, a State or the Northern Territory.' The extent of that qualification is a matter of legal opinion. I would add that on 12 July 1981 the Attorney-General announced that his Department would carry out a review of the provisions of the Copyright Act concerning copying by audiovisual means.
(2) Infringement of copyright is, under the Copyright Act, mainly a matter for civil proceedings, and the decision to commence any such proceedings is thus a matter for the owner of a copyright which is alleged to have been infringed.
(3) See answer to (I) above.

Fuel Freight Subsidy Scheme
(Question No. 3167)
Mr Kerin asked the Minister for Business and Consumer Affairs, upon notice, on 17 September 1981:
(I) Has his attention been drawn to the considerable alarm, concern and confusion caused by telephone calls from a spokesman for the Petroleum Products Pricing Authority to several fishing 'enterprises and business concerns in the Northern Territory and Queensland, in which it was stated that consideration was being given to changing the Fuel Freight Subsidy Scheme by removing existing freight differentials for safe anchorage locations in northern Australia and restricting these differentials to 6 mainland points only.
(2) Is there any sustance in this report; if so, by what authority was this action taken.
(3) Can he assure the House that before the action was taken, his colleague the Minister for Primary Industry was consulted on the matter; if so, what was the reaction of the Minister for Primary Industry.
(4) Was the fishing industry consulted on this matter.
(5) Has he considered the very serious effect the reported action would have on the operations of the northern prawn trawling industry.
Mr Moore-The answer to' the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I) to (5) The Petroleum Products Pricing Authority (PPPA) has a statutory responsibility to determine whether the prices of certain petroleum products, or services associated with their supply, are justified. In this context the PPPA has been examining fu~1 freight differentials for areas such as safe anchorages in the Gulf of Carpentaria where, over time, anomalies in the differentials have arisen.
The PPPA contacted oil companies and those fishing operators which, as agents of oil companies, have had freight differentials established for them and indicated that it was considering restriction of freight differentials to ports in the Gulf region. The PPPA invited those contacted to put views to it concerning the freight differentials.
As the examination by the PPPA has implications of the rates of subsidy payable on fuel delivered to safe anchorages, the Minister for Primary Industry and other relevant Ministers have been informed of the development. In addition I have met with representatives of the Gulf fishing industry and discussed the operation of the fuel freight subsidy scheme with them.
Currently with the PPPA examination of freight differentials, a number of aspects of the scheme are being reviewed by the Department of Business and Consumer Affairs. As part of that review, consideration is being given to the effects of the scheme on industries in remote areas.

Special Youth Employment Training Program
(Question No. 3229)
Mr McLean asked the Minister for Employment and Youth Affairs, upon notice, on 23 September 1981:
(I) What percentage of youths participating in the Special Youth Employment Training program (private sector) were retained in employment by the subsidised employer following the expiration of the subsidy period for each year from 1978-79 to 1980-81.
(2) What percentage of youths participating in the SYETP program (private sector) have succeeded in obtaining full time employment following the expiration of the subsidy period either by the subsidised employer or by other employers for each of the same years.
Mr ~. A. Brown-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
Information on the basis requested by the honourable member is not available. In late November 1979 my Department undertook a survey of a sample of employers and young people involved in Special Youth Employment Training (SYETP) program placements in the private sector in April 1979. The results of this survey indicated that:
(a) 2.5 per cent of trainees were dismissed by employers at the end of the scheduled completion of the subsidy period. 33.9 per cent of trainees did not complete the fu II subsidy period.
(b) 50.7 per cent of trainees were still employed by the SYETP employer some four to five months after the scheduled completion of the subsidy period and a further 17.8 per cent were in full-time employment elsewhere.

Instant Coffee
(Question No. 3289)
Mrs Darling asked the Minister for Business and Consumer Affairs, upon notice, on 13 October 1981:
(I) What percentage of Australian instant coffee is imported and from which countries is it imported.
(2) What Commonwealth Government regulations apply to importation of coffee.
(3) Do Commonwealth Government regulations in any way apply to the quality of imported instant coffee.
(4) Has any Australian import company trading with a near east country been prosecuted recently for substituting acorns and other grains in imported instant coffee; if so, under what State or Commonwealth Acts was action taken.
(5) What was the result of prosecution.
(6) What guarantees can he provide concerning the quality of imported instant coffee.
Mr Moore~The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I) The Australian Bureau of Statistics advises that separate statistics are not available to enable the percentage to be calculated.
(2) The Customs (Prohibited Imports) Regulations provide for a control over the importation of coffee in accordance with Australia's obligations as a member country of the International Coffee Organisation.
In addition those Regulations and the Commerce (Imports) Regulations provide for standardised sizes and labelling requirements for pre-packed coffee.
(3) Commonwealth import regulations do not apply to the quality of imported instant coffee. However, the National Health and Medical Research Council has approved a standard for coffee.
The standard covers the composition and quality of coffee in its many forms and specifies, inter alia, that coffee shall be derived from the coffee bean and shall not contain any foreign substance. Approved National Health and Medical Research Council Standards are recommended to the States and Territories for adoption into the food control legislation of the relevant authorities. When incorporated in the food laws of the States and Territories the standards are applicable to imported and local foods.
(4) and (5) I am not aware of any recent prosecution in connection with the quality of imported instant coffee. If a prosecution did take place it would have taken place under the relevant State or Territory food laws.
(6) A guarantee cannot be provided by me about the quality of imported instant coffee. The State and Territory food laws mentioned above are available however for quality controls.

Repatriation Pensions: Personnel Exposed to War Gases
(Question No. 3350)
Mr Holding asked the Minister representing the Minister for Veterans' Affairs, upon notice, on 15 October 1981:
Have any Australian ex-service personnel who were exposed to war gases tests in Australia during World War II (a) applied for and (b) refused repatriation pensions; if so, what are the details.
Mr Thomson~The Minister for Veterans' Affairs has provided the following answer to the honourable member's question:
Statistical information collected by my Department in relation to claims for acceptance of incapacity is not collected on the basis of the individual disabilities claimed or of the circumstances of the service on which the claim is based. It is not possible to provide accurate details of the number of ex-service personnel, exposed to war gas tests during World War II, who have applied for, and been refused, Repatriation benefits.
However, the Department of Veterans' Affairs has been able to identify fifty-one claims for Repatriation benefits which have been lodged on the basis of involvement in gas experiments on service. Of these claims, twelve have been successful.
These figures are not comprehensive and there may be other claims which have not been identified.
Should any ex-serviceman consider he is suffering some incapacity as a result of involvement in gas experiments, he should lodge a claim with the Department of Veterans' Affairs in the State in which he resides and arrangements will be made for it to be investigated.

Sharp PC1211 Pocket Computer
(Question No. 3405)
Mr Humphreys asked the Minister for Business and Consumer Affairs, upon notice, on 22 October 1981:
(I) Has his allention been drawn to the existence of correspondence between the (a) purchaser of a Sharp PCI211 Pocket Computer/Electronic Calculator and the Trade Practices Commission and (b) Sharp Corporation and the Trade Practices Commission regarding the defective operation of this equipment.
(2) H so, can he say whether this correspondence stated that the Sharp PCI211 pocket computer had recently been modified to rectify intermittent faults in the spontaneous reset and lockup modes.
(3) Hso, is he satisfied that (a) reasonable efforts have been made to inform retailers and owners that the unit referred to in part (I) may be faulty, (b) the unit is not still being sold without the purchaser being informed beforehand that the unit may be faulty and (c) the TRS80 pocket computer manufactured by Tandy Electronics Pty Ltd has not similar faults to those described in part (2).
Mr Moore-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I) (a) I am informed that there has been ongoing correspondence since March 1981 between a purchaser and the Trade Practices Commission concerning problems experienced with Sharp PC 1211 Electronic Calculators.
(b) Following receipt of the complaint, the Commission wrote to Sharp Corporation requesting their comments on the matters raised.
(2) In a leller to the Commission dated 27 August 1981, Sharp Corporation said that a permanent modification had been designed by the manufacturer in Japan in December 1980. The Company stated all stocks manufactured after December 1980 contain the modification and all current stocks of earlier production have been modified by its Service Department.
(3) (a) Sharp has advised the Commission that it notified all service agents of the potential problem and a bulletin to service agents of 27 April 1981 gave instruction on the incorporation of modification. However, as advice to service agents may not have filtered through to retailers, the Commission is taking up this point with Sharp Corporation. Sharp also advised that as only 5 units of 1709 units sold has been returned with similar problems, the incidence of failure was not

sufficient to warrant a recall or advertisement of the problem in the media.
(b) See (2) and (3) (a) above. Any units which are returned for repair for any reason will also have the modification filled free of charge if it has not already been done.
(c) I am advised the Commission's allention has been drawn to an article in an American journal which discussed faults in the Tandy TRS80 which seemed to be similar to those which had been experienced with the Sharp PC 1211. The Commission has recently learnt that there may be lillie significant difference between the two units. The Commission therefore intends to approach Tandy on the mailer.

Importation of Pollen
Mr MacKellar-On 8 September 1981 (Hansard, page 987) Mr Kent asked me a question, without notice, concerning the quarantine risk of the importation of pollen.
The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
My Department has undertaken an extensive study on the effects of chalkbrood fungus and the possibility of viable pseudosclerotia being included in consignments of pollen. My Department's investigations do indicate that a tenuous link exists that could create a risk of entry of the disease with importations of bee-collected pollen. For example, discarded containers holding the remnants of any infected pollen may prove attractive to bees and provide a possible means of introducing the disease into bees in the wild in Australia.
I have approved the drafting of legislation to han the importation of bee-collected pollen. Given the nature of the disease link and the need, where practical, to ensure that industry and others be given time to adjust, I do not consider that it would be appropriate to introduce interim measures to prevent the entry of bee-collected pollen before the proposed legislation hecomes effective.

Answers to Questions

17 November 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 2977

Hospital Insurance: Contribution Rates
Mr MacKellar-On 10 September 1981 (Hansard, page 1155) Mr Robert Brown asked me, as Minister for Health, a question without notice concerning contribution rates of hospital insurance funds.
The following is the answer to the honourable member's question:
Where a registered health insurance organisation changes its rates of contribution, it is required under the National Health Act (section 78) to notify the Permanent Head of the Department of Health in writing of the change within seven days after the change is made. The change is inoperative and has no legal effect unless and until the Minister's approval of the change has been given. Any change to an organisation's rules which has financial implications, such as a change in contribution rates, is referred to the Registration Committee for examination and report to the Minister. The Committee comprises the Commonwealth Actuary, or a person appointed by the Commonwealth Actuary to act in his stead, and two officers of the Department of Health appointed by the Permanent Head.
However. the prime responsibility for ensuring that contribution rates are viable lies with the organisations themselves. The onus is on an organisation, when notifying a change in rates, to provide sufficient supporting data that will enable the Registration Committee to undertake a proper assessment of the change and then make an appropriate recommendation to the Minister. Factors taken into account by the Committee include the following:
(i) The financial experience of both the hospital and medical benefits funds, by tables of benefit. for the latest year ended 30 June. This acquaints the Committee with the surplus or deficit situation and the level of reserves of each fund, thereby providing a basis for consideration of the effects of the proposed change.
(ii) The estimated financial experience of each fund, by tables, for the period between 30 June and the date of effect of the new rates. The financial experience is arrived at after taking into consideration any recent movements in membership, the average level of benefits and per capita claims utilisation as well as general cost movements which might affect the level of management expenses and the likely amount of nonoperating income accruing to the fund having regard to the funds' investments at 30 June. Detailed statistics relating to membership benefits and claims utilisation are maintained by the Department of Health from quarterly returns furnished by the health insurance organisations themselves.
(iii) A projected financial experience for each fund, by tables, for a period of twelve months from the date of effect of the new contribution rates. This takes into' account similar factors as in (ii) above. However, where the proposed change is the result of a major revision of the health insurance arrangements, particular attention is given to the likely impact on fund experience of new benefits or increases in levels of existing benefits. In this regard, an assessment is made of the effect on claims utilisation of a possible future redistribution of membership between tables. Similarly, the Committee considers to what extent new members might be attracted and the effect this might have on future experience.
(iv) The estimated result for twelve months having regard to the factors set out in (iii) above and level of reserves, expressed in months of contribution income, at

the end of the projected period. The Committee recognises that organisations might incorporate components in their proposed contribution rates to build-up reserves to satisfactory levels. The Committee also needs to be satisfied that, as far as it can determine, adequate provision has been made for outstanding claims of contributors.
(v) In the case of a hospital benefits fund, the extent of the distribution of moneys from the Hospital Benefits Reinsurance Trust Fund.
(vi) Whether the contribution rates proposed by the organisation generally adhere to the community rating principle. In this context the same contribution rates should be charged for all members of the one table, with the single rate set at half the family rate.
(vii) Any other factors that the Committee considers relevant in the case of a particular fund.
Some of the larger organisations employ the services of an actuary to determine changes in contribution rates and the Committee is sometimes furnished with a copy of the actuary's report for its information.
If, after examining the effect of a change, the Committee considers that the change imposes any unreasonable or inequitable condition affecting the rights of contributors or adversely affects the financial stability of a fund, the Committee is required to state its opinion and the reasons for its opinions in its report to the Minister.
The financial experience of the respective health insurance organisations often varies considerably. Therefore, the Registration Committee necessarily adopts a flexible approach when examining proposals to change contribution rates, having regard to the particular set of circumstances pertaining to each fund.
Mineral Export Controls
(Question No. 1993)
Mr Rocher asked the Minister for Trade and Resources, upon notice, on 29 April 1981 :
(I) Are (a) salt, (b) nickel, (c) copper ore (i) concentrate and (ii) matte and (d) refined copper, still subject to export controls.
(2) Did he make a statement on 15 December 1978 in which he foreshadowed that these controls would be removed; if so, and if the controls have not been removed, what is the reason.
Mr Anthony-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I) and (2) The commodities listed in Part I of the question were included in a list of commodities on which I indicated in my statement on 15 December 1978 that my Department would be consulting with industry and the States on their possible removal from export control.
Following those consultations nickel was included in the list of minerals for which I authorised blanket approvals on 12 June 1979. Copper ores, concentrates, matte and refined copper were added to the list in a joint statement by the Ministers for Industry and Commerce, Business and Consumer Affairs and myself on 22 July 1980. As a consequence, it is no longer necessary for exporters of these commodities to seek export approvals for individual shipments or contracts and exporters are able to seek approvals covering their annual tonnages. This procedures enables the Government to monitor the export of these minerals with a minimum of involvement..
In relation to salt, there is still a requirement for approval of individual shipments or contracts. However, this situation is at present under review.
