Brisbane Airport: Use of Adjacent Land
(Question No. 2795)
Mrs Darling asked the Minister for Housing and Construction, upon notice, on 18 August 1981:
(I) Has his attention been drawn to reports which appeared in the Brisbane Courier Mail prior to the 1980 Federal election, in which the former member for Lilley referred to advice provided by independent consultants to his Department on the matter of recreational uses to which land adjacent to the Brisbane Airport might be put.
(2) If so, did these reports refer to specific possible recreational uses, such as boating, horse riding, orienteering, bike riding and shooting.
(3) Can he say whether the former member said in the reports that these activities (a) had been recommended by the advisers and (b) would not impede the efficient running of the airport or add to the hazards of flying.
(4) Is the advice referred to contained in a report commissioned by the Government; if so, (a) who were the independent consultants, (b) when were they commissioned, (c) when and to whom was their report submitted, (d) is the report regarded as confidential, and, if so, what is the reason and (e) was the report made available officially or unofficially to anyone outside his Department and, if so, to whom, and on what basis was it made available.
(5) Can he say how the former member received the information he released to the media and used in election pamphlets.
(6) Did he receive a request from my office for a copy of the consultants' report to be made available to me.
(7) Is it a fact that that request was denied on the basis that the document was confidential.
(8) What recreational facilities does the report recommend as suitable for land adjacent to the airport.
(9) What is his Department's appraisal of the feasibility of each recommendation.
(10) If he will not release the recommendations of the report, what is the reason.
(II) What recreational facilities does the Government regard as suitable for development on land adjacent to the airport.
Mr McVeigh-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I) My attention has been drawn to an article which appeared in the Brisbane Courier Mail of 15 September 1980, which reported the former member for Lilley, as saying inter alia: that recreation experts had recommended to the then Minister for Housing and Construction, the Hon. R. J. Groom, that the area of the seven kilometre estuary and flood way associated with the airport could be used for more than 40 different leisure activities.
I am also aware of a similar but more detailed article on the front page of the North Eastern edition of the Express suburban newspaper of 17 September 1980.
(2) The newspaper reports did refer to specific possible recreational uses such as boating, horse riding, orienteering, bike riding and scouting. It is assumed that the reference to shooting in the question is intended to be scouting.
(3) I am not in a position to indicate what any previous member may have said or not said to the media. Both the above press articles included the comment, although it was not reported as being quoted by the former member, that the consultants say the recreational opportunities suggested

2980 REPRESENTATIVES 17 November 1981

Answers to Questions

would in no way impede the efficient running of the airport or add to the hazards associated with Hying.
(4) (a) Yes, the above quotation is contained in a report prepared jointly by the Department of Housing and Construction and consultants. The Department of Housing and Construction commissioned a firm of consultants called Recreational Resource Management Pty Ltd to undertake a study of possible recreational uses of the Iloodway to assist the Department in its advisings to the Department of Transport Australia. This study formed the basis of the report.
(b) The firm was commissioned on 30 May 1980.
(c) Followingsubmission of the consultants' study report to the Department of Housing and Construction it was then jointly modified with the consultants and finalised in Septem ber 1980.
(d) It is an internal Departmental report which was, and still is, subject to examination and review by Department of Transport Australia. It was prepared to assist Department of Transport Australia in their on-going considerations on possible land usage. As such, it did not and does not necessarily represent the final outcome as many other issues have still to be taken into account.
(e) The report was of course given to Department of Transport Australia by the Department of Housing and Construction. I am not in a position to comment on whether or not anyone other than Departmental officers gave the report or parts of the report to anyone else.
(5) I am not responsible for any action of any previous member of this Parliament.
(6) Yes.
(7) At my request, my Senior Private Secretary rang your office to advise that I was not prepared to release the report because I consider it premature to generate any public demand for space or facilities which might be incompatible with long term strategy for use, operation and maintenance of the Iloodway area and surrounds before all aspects and implications have been fully examined.
At the present time, the Iloodway usage aspects of the report are being taken into account in formulating the conditions under which possible use might be made of the floodway land for consideration by the various levels of Government. This is being done by the Joint Government Coordinating Committee (JGCC) on Brisbane Airport Redevelopment which has established a F100dway Usage Advisory Group to advise it. I have not agreed to release the report to anyone outside the respective Departments.
(8) The report does not make recommendations as to the suitability of any particular recreation facilities but rather canvasses a range of options and comments on the advantages and disadvantages of each particular option. Within the options, the sorts of activities which presented themselves are, inter alia:
barbecuing, boating, cycling, eating, fishing, horse-riding, trail bike riding, photography, picnicking, reading, relaxing, sigh t seeing, canoeing, caravan park, dog training, fossicking, hiking, orienteering, rowing, sailing, swimming.
(9) The Department of Housing and Construction has made no assessment of the feasibility of the various options proposed pending effective stabilisation of the floodway banks, review of the flood way performance, and the outcome of the considerations of the JGCC's Floodway Usage AdvisoryGroup.
(10) See the answer to Part (7) above.
(II) I would expect that the Government would generally regard recreational facilities or activities as being suitable for the land adjacent to the airport if they are compatible with floodway performance; 'assist in maintaining the flood way,

and do not jeopardise flood way stability or increase the problems of bird hazards and hence risk to aircraft operations from the new airport. However, the whole question of eventual floodway usage is the subject of on-going co~siderations by the Joint Government Co-ordinating Committee and in particular the Department of Transport Australia.

Screenings of Multi-cultural Television at Parliament House
(Question No. 3075)
Mr Robert Brown asked the Minister for Communications, upon notice, on 10 September 1981:
(I) How many (a) members, (b) senators, (c) parliamentary staff members and (d) outside visitors attended the special screenings of multi-cultural television (0/28) at Parliament House in June 1981.
(2) What was the cost of providing this service.
Mr Sinclair-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:

(2) The total cost of providing the service was $1,957.45 which included the costs of the Telecom bearer, equipment hire and travel expenses for one Special Broadcasting Service officer to host the special screenings.

(2) (b) Under the Government's Federalism policy the detailed administration of the National Estate Program, is as far as it is possible, carried out by State Governments. In this process each State Government calls for and compiles grant applications for consideration within its State. Allowance for Travelling to Education Courses
(Question No. 3080)
Mr Beazley asked the Minister for Employment and Youth Affairs, upon notice, on 10 September 1981:
(I) Do unemployed young persons receive a travel allowance of $6 per week for travelling to education courses.
(2) Will this travel allowance be available to persons travelling to community-based centres which may replace Community Youth Support Scheme centres.
(3) As $6 is well below the level of fares some persons need to pay to attend courses will he take action to raise the maximumto$12.

Mr N. A. Brown-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I) Eligible young people attending approved full-time Transition courses in TAFE may receive the Transition Allowance which includes a component of $6 per week to cover incidental expenditure incurred in attending the course.
(2) The Government has decided that the Community Youth Support Scheme will not be terminated.
(3) Should it emerge that the rate of allowance is inadequate for its purpose the Government would give consideration to adjusting the rate.
Experimental Ethnic Television Programs
(Question No. 3121)
Mr Humphreys asked the Minister for Communications, upon notice, on 16 September 1981:
(I) Has an assessment been made of public reaction to experimental ethnic television programs which have been shown in Sydney and Melbourne; if so (a) what was the methodological basis of this assessment and (b) what were the findings.
(2) When will multi-cultural television presently operating in these centres, be extended to other capital and provincial cities throughout Australia.
Mr Sinclair-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I) I am advised that during the first year of operations the SBS commissioned three television surveys in Melbourne aimed at assessing audience reaction to various aspects of the Channel 0/28 service. These surveys referred to the multicultural television service, which commenced transmiSsion on 24 October 1980, not to the limited series of experimental ethnic television programs presented in late 1979 and early 1980. 11 should be noted that the surveys were confined to Melbourne because of the reception difficulties experienced with Channel 0/28 in Sydney.
Two audience surveys were undertaken by McNair Anderson Associates Pty Ltd. The first, undertaken between 21 December 1980 and JO January 1981, measured the viewing patterns of the total audience (i.e. both Australian and non-Australian born). The second, conducted during the period 16 January to 7 February 1981, surveyed nine ethnic communities:i.e. Italians, Greeks, Yugoslavs, Arabians, Germans, Maltese, Spanish, Poles and Vietnamese.
The third survey, undertaken by In view Market Research between 23 March and 30 April 1981, involved an 'in-depth' survey of four ethnic communities: Italians, Greeks, Poles and Vietnamese.
Aspects upon which viewer reactions were sought included, in all three surveys: quality of reception (Channel 0 and 28); the channel on which the station was viewed; the quality of Channel 0/28 programs in comparison with commercial and ABC channels; Channel 0/28 program presentation style in comparison with commercial and ABC channels; individual programs presented by Channel 0128; Channel 0/28 subtitles.
It is noteworthy that there was a reasonable consistency in the findings obtained in all three surveys. The resulls themselves were encouraging because they pointed to a successful introduction of the new service among the non-Australian born audience; over half of Australian born residents had watched Channel 0/28; the great majority of Channel 0/28 viewers regarded its programming and style ~of presentation as better or equal to that of commercial and ABC channels; there was a high level of acceptance of Channel 0/28's subtitling among the station's viewers; viewers in all language groups watched other language programs as well as their own;

2982 REPRESENTATIVES 17 November 1981

Answers to Questions

and, there was a favourable reaction to Channel 0/28's news, films and sports programs.
(2) The 1981-82 Budget did not allocate any funds for the extension of the multicultural television service beyond Sydney and Melbourne.
The Government is conscious that there is considerable demand for the multicultural television service in other regions. However, before any decision is taken to extend the service to other areas, considerable economic, social and technical planning and research will need to be undertaken. As part of this process the Government will also have to balance the competing claims of a variety of areas for the service.
Australian Light-stations
(Question No. 3134)
Mr Morris asked the Minister for Transport, upon notice, on 16 September 1981:
(1) What services are used by the Navigational Aids service of Transport Australia in transporting men and materials to Australian light-stations.
(2) What has been the cost of providing these various services in each of the last 10 years.
(3) What has been the cost of maintaining each of the ships owned by the lighthouse service in each of the last \0 years.
(4) What has been the cost of providing helicopter services to Australian light-stations over each of the last 10 years.

(5) What has been the number of (a) persons involved in supervising the fleet of ships operated by Transport Australia, and (b) crew members for the same period.
(6) Are there any plans to increase the number of persons in his Department's Transport Operations Branch; if so, what are the details.
(7) Has his Department undertaken any studies to determine the relative efficiency of helicopter vis-a-vis Cape-class ship services to light-stations; if so, what were the results of the studies and did any of the studies take account of the higher proportional overheads to be absorbed by the Department's ships as helicopter services are increased.
Mr Hunt-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(1) Men and equipment are transported to Commonwealth light-stations by ship, helicopter, aircraft, launch, motor vehicle or a combination of these depending on the accessibility of the site.
(2) The cost of providing the services referred to in (1) above is not shown separately in departmental accounts.
These services are an integral part of the comprehensive navigational support task carried out by the personnel of the Coastal Transport Branch of the Department of Transport.
This task includes the performance by ships' crews of special technical work aboard ships and ashore and takes in the operation of boats and amphibians as well as the construction and maintenance of navigational aids, stevedoring and other support tasks necessary for operations in isolated areas.

Exclusions from Pre-paid Envelopes
(Question No. 3146)
Mr Scholes asked the Minister for Communications, upon notice, on 16 September 1981:
(I) Why has the National floral emblem been excluded from the new issue of pre-paid envelopes which includes all States plus the Northern Territory.
(2) Why is the Australian Capital Territory not recognised as a separate entity by the Postal Commission in this series.
Mr Sinclair-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I) The definitive pre-stamped envelope series featuring floral emblems depicts only officially proclaimed Australian floral emblems. As the 'traditional' national floral emblem (The Golden Wattle-'Acacia Pycnantha') has not been officially proclaimed, it was not included.
(2) The Australian Capital Territory has no official floral emblem.
Plant Variety Rights Legislation
(Question No. 3163)
Mr Kerin asked the Minister for Primary Industry, upon notice, on 17 September 1981:
(I) In view of the invitation for public comment on the Plant Variety Rights legislation contained in his press release dated 3 August 1981, and taking into consideration the fact that further parliamentary debate has been deferred until February 1982 to allow for this public debate, will he provide, without delay up-to-date information, which has been denied to interested public groups, to enable informed public debate on (a) the substance of the discussions at the meeting of the Agricultural Council in August 1981 in Darwin concerning the Plant Variety Rights legislation and (b) proposed changes in the Bill introduced in May 1981 which (i) are under discussion within this Department, (ii) are planned for discussion or (iii) have been initiated since the presentation of the Bill.
(2) Will informed public debate be impossible without the provision of this information.
(3) Have details of the Agriculture Council Meeting referred to been passed to industry.
Mr Nixon-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I) Informed public debate on the Plant Variety Rights (PVR) issue in Australia depends more upon an understanding of the principles involved in the Scheme than the details of the legislation.
Information about the proposed Scheme has been distributed widely. My own Department and several State departments responsible for agriculture have prepared information papers for general distribution. 1 have issued a number of Press Releases on PVR and answered many hundreds of postal enquiries from interested persons and organisations. The legislation, designed to give effect to proposals for the Scheme, has been available as a public document since it was introduced into Parliament on 7 May 1981.
I have made it clear from the outset that the form the PVR Scheme will finally take will be determined in large part by the wishes of the Australian Agricultural council but as I indicated in my Press Release issued on 3 August 1981, Council has yet to make its final decision on a number of major issues including merit testing and the question of excluding certain species.

(2) Information necessary for informed public debate is available at present. I am issuing an updated information paper which deals with those matters of public concern most frequently raised with me.
(3) No. Agricultural Council discussions are confidential. However, my Press Release concerning the meeting has been available since 3 August 1981 and the Resolutions from the meeting are being tabled in Parliament.

Department of Industrial Relations: Record of Travel Warrants
(Question No. 3352)
Mrs Darling asked the Minister for Industrial Relations, upon notice, on 20 October 1981:
(I) Further to his reply to my question No. 1134 (Hansard, 9 September 1981, page 1124) concerning the number of travel warrants issued for air travel for officers of his Department, why did it take 5 months for him to reply that the information which I sought was not available and would not be made available.
(2) Has it been drawn to his attention that other Ministers' Departments do record this information.
(3) Can he say what are the reasons for the different approaches of Ministers and their departments to this matter of public accountability.
Mr Viner-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I) The delay was caused by the pressure of other work requirements in that area of my Department responsible for the preparation of the reply. Administrative staffing arrangements have been reviewed to ensure that questions on notice are answered promptly.
I did not say that the information was not available, I said that it was not readily available.
(2) I am not aware of the records held by other Ministers' Departments.
(3) I am not aware of the reasons for the different approaches of Ministers and their Departments but again emphasise that the information is not readily available. It would involve the manual examination of over 9,000 movement requisitions and reference to a large volume of associated airline accounts (and their numerous attachments). I am not prepared to authorise the use of the substantial resources that this would require.
Importation of Baby Seal Products
(Question No. 3357)
Mrs Darling asked the Minister for Home Affairs and Environment, upon notice, on 20 October 1981:
Further to the reply to question No. 2791 by the Minister for Business and Consumer Affairs (Hansard, 13 October 198 I, page 1935), will the Government, in respect of its position on the importation of baby seal products, take into account whether or not the baby seals from which the products are derived were slaughtered in an inhumane manner, and in the event that this is so, will he undertake to have prohibited the importation of products derived from baby seals.
Mr Wilson-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
The Government is aware that there is controversy over the methods used to kill harp seals in Canada. The Canadian Government authorities claim that clubbing, using an instrument called a hakapik, is the most humane of a number of alternative killing methods tried under field conditions.
Thai Navy Anti-piracy Unit
(Question No. 3366)
Mr Humphreys asked the Minister for Foreign Affairs, upon notice, on 20 October 1981:

(I) Has the Australian Government been asked by either the United Nations Organisation or the Thai Government to make a financial contribution toward the revival of the Antipiracy Unit to be administered by the Thai Navy.
(2) Has the Government made any decision as to whether Australia should give support to this cause, following renewed attacks on Vietnamese refugees and even Australians sailing in the Gulf of Thailand or the South China Seas and following the contribution of $2,600,000 by the United States of America.
Mr Street-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I) There has been no direct bilateral approach from the Thai Government to the Australian Government to make a financial contribution towards the Thai Government's antipiracy activity. The Thai Government has however submitted to the United Nations High Commissioner for Refugees proposals for additional funds and equipment which it considers necessary in order to resume anti-piracy patrolling in and around Thai territorial waters. These and similar proposals for the resumption of anti-piracy patrolling are presently being informally discussed between international agencies ill1d interested governments, including Australia.
(2) The Australian Government has consistently condemned the cruel and barbaric practice of piracy and will of course give sympathetic consideration to the provision of appropriate assistance, once the present round of consultations is completed.
Brisbane Airport: Air Traffic Controllers
(Question No. 3371)
Mr Humphreys asked the Minister for Transport, upon notice, on 20 October 1981:
(I) How many hours of overtime were worked during the period I January to 19 October 1981 by each air traffic controller based at Brisbane Control Tower.
(2) What is the national average number of hours of overtime worked per air traffic controller.
(3) On how many occasions during 1981 has a checkcontroller had to stand in for an air traffic controller because of unexpected absences of staff at the Brisbane Control Centre.
Mr Hunt-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I) Each controller averaged 41 hours 22 minutes overtime.
(2) Approximately 70 hours per annum.
(3) This information is not recorded as the check controller is already on duty when a stand in for a duty controller is required.
Investigator Strait Fishery
(Question No. 3376)
Mr Kerin asked the Minister for Primary Industry, upon notice, on 20 October 1981:
(1) Is it a fact that the Fisheries Division of his Department, as well as the South Australian Fisheries Department, recommended in late 1980 that the Investigator Strait fishery be closed to prawn trawling for 2 years.
(2) Did he refuse to close the strait to trawling unless the South Australian Fisheries Department consented to absorb the vessels operating in Investigator Strait, under Commonwealth exploratory fishing permits, into South Australian prawn fisheries.

2986 REPRESENTATIVES 17 November 1981

Answers to Questions

(3) Did he or his Department grant 12 month exploratory permits for Investigator Strait in 1977 against strong protests from the South Australian Government.
(4) Has the adjoining St Vincent Gulf fishery collapsed because of this action and do the permits remain.
(5) If he did not require the arrangement referred to in part (2), what proposal did he put to the South Australian Department of Fisheries or the South Australian Minister on the question.
(6) If he did require the arrangement referred to in part (2), will it lead to over-fishing and eventually the destruction of other South Australian fisheries.
(7) Did he or his Department co-operate with the South Australian Fisheries Department in a management policy for the joint Commonwealth/State prawn fishery on the West Coast of South Australia, off Venus Bay.
(8) Is it a fact that the fishery was reduced within the 3 year period 1976to 1979 from an average catch of 239tonnes to 16 tonnes as a result of the failure of the Commonwealth to co-operate.
Mr Nixon-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I) No. I am unable to say what the South Australian Department recommended to their Minister. However, my Department, following extensive consultations with industry was clearly of the view that a decision on closure of Investigator Strait should not be made until final comments were received from industry and other interested community groups.
(2) No.
(3) No. On 10 November 1977 the then Minister for Primary Industry approved an interim arrangement whereby the Commonwealth boat licences in respect of 8 authorised operators would be endorsed subject to certain conditions to permit operations for a 3 month period.
It is true that at the time of the Minister's decision the Commonwealth and South Australian Governments had not reached final agreement on the administration and management of the fishery. Consultations continued until 29 June 1978 when Governments agreed to new management measures which were effectively the same as those implemented by the Commonwealth on 10 November 1977.
(4) On information available to my department, it would not appear that the St Vincents Gulf prawn fishery could be said to have collapsed. However, as this fishery is solely a State-based fishery and therefore managed by the State department I make no further comment.
It would appear that only 6 of the 8 operators authorised to operate in the Investigator Strait fishery are in fact so engaged. Inquiries are being made to determine the rights of the remaining 2 fishermen.
(5) On 10 March 1981 I indicated to Mr Rodda that I would be prepared to consider a 2 year closure of Investigator Strait on the condition that fishing would resume when stocks recovered but stressed that no final decision should be taken until all interested parties had been given opportunity for comment. A joint announcement to this effect was made by Mr Rodda and myself on 27 March 1981.
(6) Not applicable.
(7) Yes. In June 1978 the Commonwealth and South Australian Governments announced agreement on management measures for the prawn fishery on the west coast of Eyre Peninsula.
(8) Annual catches from the fishery for the period 1976 to 1979 inclusive were 51.0 tonnes, 22.8 tonnes, 16.4 tonnes and 32.5 tonnes respectively. Following the implementation of a

joint Commonwealth/State management regime catches have recovered to pre-1976 levels.
Sydney (Kingsford-Smith) Airport
(Question No. 3377)
Mr Morris asked the Minister for Transport, upon notice, on 20 October 1981:
What is the specific order of priority foilowed by his Departmental officers in clearing aircraft for landing at Sydney Kingsford-Smith Airport during periods of air traffic congestion.
Mr Hunt-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
The Aeronautical Information Publication issued by my Department currently contains the following instructions on the subject of 'Regulation of Flight-Assessment of Priorities'.
7.I-ATC will regulate operations to minimise the possibility of conflict and provided that safety is in no way jeopardised, will apply priorities in the following order:
(a) an aircraft in an emergency, including being subjected to unlawful interference, will be given priority in all circumstances;
(b) an aircraft which has suffered radio communications failure will be granted priority for landing;
(c) an aircraft which has declared a mercy flight;
(d) an aircraft participating in a Search and Rescue (SAR), Medical (MED), Hospital Aircraft (HOSP), or Fire and Flood Relief (FFR) flight shall be granted priority as necessary;
(e) an aircraft engaged in the personal transport of Heads of State or of Government or other selected dignitaries, on official visits to Australia, or the personal transport of the Governor-General or the Prime Minister;
(f) a landing aircraft will have priority over a departing aircraft if the latter cannot take-off with prescribed separation standards;
(g) an aircraft landing or taking off will be given priority over taxiing aircraft;
(h) an aircraft which is first able to use the landing area or desired airspace, in the normal course of its operation, will be given priority except;
(i) when significant economic benefit would result for a number of other aircraft by deferring this priority;
(ii) that flights desiring to operate in other than the normal pattern for operational reasons, will be given the same priority as other flights unless it involves a short-notice change to a clearance already issued to another aircraft. In this case, permission to operate will be deferred.
7.I.I-Training flights will be given the same priority as other flights except that:
(a) flights operating in the traffic pattern in general use will be given priority over flights desiring to operate in conflicting patterns for training purposes;
(b) when a training instrument approach is approved, priority will be given to that aircraft from the time it commences its final approach until the approach is completed.
7.1.2-Notwithstanding para. 7.1 (h), during periods when all requirements for operation in a capital city primary control

Answers to Questions

17 November 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 2987

zone, or the overlying control area, cannot be accommodated, priority will be given in the following order:
(a) regular public transport, military and charter aircraft;
(b) aircraft engaged in aerial work;
(c) private aircraft proceeding to the primary airport;
(d) other private aircraft.
Except that at Sydney the following order of priorities will apply:
(a) regular public transport aircraft, charter aircraft of DC9 and heavier type (if associated with RPT operations), IFR charter aircraft proceeding to Bankstown and military aircraft (other than training flights);
(b) charter aircraft (other than in (a) above);
(c) aircraft engaged in aerial work and military training aircraft;
(d) private aircraft types unable to use Bankstown or Camden;
(e) other private flights.
Additionally, instructions have been issued to ATe to provide aircraft engaged in personal transport of State Governors and Premiers and the Administrator and Chief Minister of the Northern Territory with the same priority as the other categories of flight mentioned in paragraph 7.1.2 (a), as applied generally and at Sydney.
Export of Live Sheep
(Question No. 3399)
Mr Kerin asked the Minister for Primary Industry, upon notice, on 22 October 1981:
(I) Did 9,000 sheep die on the ship Persia during a recent trip from Australia to Egypt; if not, how many did die.
(2) Were the deaths due to a failure of the ventilation system on the ship after 2 days' travel from Fremantle, WA.; if not, what were the reasons.
(3) What is the (a) normal and (b) range of the percentage deaths in the sea transport of live sheep.
(4) What steps can he take and does he intend taking to prevent a high level of deaths in future shipments.
Mr Nixon-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I) The number of sheep that died on the Persia during a trip from Australia to Egypt in September 1981 is reported to be 8,764.
(2) The Persia completed loading of 49,500 sheep on I September 1981. On 4 September the ventilation system was out of action for 35 minutes and 266 sheep died. On 8 September the ventilation system was out of action for 5l hours and 1,003 sheep died. Both periods of non ventilation were caused by a breakdown in the electrical switching system.
Following these two ventilation failures the main engines had to be run at reduced capacity due to a crack developing in a cylinder head liner. This resulted in a reduction in production of drinking water. The ship altered course and called at Aden to take on additional water. There were 6,028 deaths attributable to this water shortage.
The other 1,495 deaths were due to other miscellaneous causes.
(3) (a) The average mortality rate on ships carrying sheep from Australia is 2 per cent approximately and is being progressively reduced due to improved methods.

(b) The range of mortality rates is from less than I per cent

to about 4 per cent. When a major interruption to conditions

is experienced, such as that on the Persia, the rate can be

higher. The majority of ships have death rates below 3 per

cent.

.

(4) The Australian Bureau of Animal Health has developed a set of Standards for the Preparation and Carriage of
Sheep by Sea. I have informed the industry that these Standards are to be met.

My colleague the Minister for Transport is shortly to introduce revised Marine Orders to control vessels engaged in the carriage of live animals.

Steps have been taken to increase research inputs into this
industry. State and Commonwealth officers are planning these inputs at the present time. Funds have been made available to
enable veterinarians and other scientists to accompany shipments to carry out observations and measurements and to
conduct research.

Experimental Building Station
(Question No. 3612)
Mr Jacobi asked the Minister for Housing and Construction, upon notice, on 29 October 1981:
(I) Is the scientific testing of building materials for fire safety rating and structural soundness at the Experimental Building Station an essential service to the building industry and to the general public as consL!mers.
(2) Is it a fact that the services of the station are unique in Australia.
(3) Must the service provided by the station, by its nature, be seen to be independent and unconnected with any commercial interest.
(4) How can the integrity of work performed by the station be guaranteed if it is sold to private interest.
(5) Can he provide any guarantee, that if the station is sold, the full range of testing and related services will continue to be provided for councils and members of the public who depend on these services.
(6) Will the Government urgently review its decision to sell the station in view of the clear need for the retention of the services of the station under government control.
Mr McVeigh-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I) Yes.
(2) Yes.
(3) Yes.
(4) The Government's decision was to dispose of the Station on certain conditions. The successor organisation is required to demonstrate that it could maintain impartiality and technical excellence. This condition was included in the public advertisement for expressions of interest which closed on 9 October.
(5) The public advertisement for expressions of interest also stipulated that the Government's aim is for the Experimental Building Station to be taken over as a going concern, and the Government is prepared to continue its role in building systems accreditation and the development of building regulations. Provision has been made in this year's budget.
(6) See the answer to (5) above.
